# 庄子注

Xiang Xiu and Guo Xiang’s *Annotations on the Zhuangzi*
*Zhuangzi, Inner Chapters, "Free and Easy Wandering" (First)*

Though small and great differ, when released into the realm of self-attainment, things follow their nature, affairs match their capacity, each fitting its allotted portion—free and easy wandering is one and the same. How could victory or defeat find place between them?

In the Northern Darkness there is a fish, its name is Kun.
The greatness of Kun—who knows how many thousand *li* it spans?
It transforms into a bird, its name is Peng.
The reality of Peng and Kun, I have not yet fathomed.

The great purport of Zhuangzi lies in free and easy wandering, in non-action and self-attainment. Thus he exhausts the extremes of small and great to illuminate the suitability of inherent nature. Those of far-reaching vision should grasp its convergence and let go of its incidental trappings, not troubling themselves to argue point by point with the living. So long as the grand intent remains unharmed, all may be roughly understood.

The back of Peng—who knows how many thousand *li* it spans?
When it rouses itself to flight, its wings are like clouds hanging from the sky.
This bird, when the seas stir, will journey to the Southern Darkness.
The Southern Darkness is the Celestial Pond.
Only the dark sea suffices to bear its body; only ninety thousand *li* suffice to support its wings.
Is this a fondness for the strange? It is simply that great things must arise from great places, and great places must give rise to such great things—principle is inherently natural, not fearing loss. Why trouble the mind over it?

*Qi Xie* is a record of marvels.
Its words say: “When Peng journeys to the Southern Darkness, it strikes the water for three thousand *li*, then spirals upward on the whirlwind for ninety thousand *li*. With wings so vast, lifting is difficult—only by seizing the whirlwind can it ascend; ninety thousand *li* are needed to sustain itself. With such wings, how could it suddenly take flight, only to descend a few *ren*? This is all unavoidable, not a matter of delight. It departs with the six-month breath.”

The great bird departs for half a year before resting at the Celestial Pond; the small bird flies for half a morning before darting to the elm or sandalwood to alight. Though their capacities differ, in following their nature they are one. The wild horses of the air, the motes of dust—all living things are blown about by one another’s breath. These are but what the Peng relies upon to fly.

The "wild horses" are wandering *qi*. Is the deep blue of the sky its true color? Or is it simply so distant that it has no end? From above, the Peng sees the earth as we see the sky—thus it halts and plans its journey south, for it knows not the distance, only that it must go far enough to sustain itself and depart.

If water is not deep, it lacks the strength to bear a great boat. Pour a cup of water into a hollow in the floor, and a mustard seed becomes a boat; place a cup there, and it sticks fast—too shallow for so large a vessel. This makes clear why the Peng flies so high: its wings are vast. A small creature requires little, so a great one cannot make do with less. Thus principle has its ultimate divisions, and things have their fixed limits; each suffices for its task, and their fulfillment is the same.

If one forgets the root of life and pursues it beyond what is fitting, undertaking what exceeds one’s strength and acting against one’s nature, then even wings spanning the sky cannot escape exhaustion, and a sudden flight cannot avoid distress. If the wind is not deep, it lacks the power to bear great wings. Only when the wind lies nine thousand *li* below can the Peng now rise upon it; only by shouldering the sky and overcoming all obstacles can it now set its course south. Yet its journey south is not out of love for height or longing for distance—it is simply that without accumulated wind, no path opens before it. Such is the Peng’s freedom.

The cicada and the fledgling dove laugh at it, saying: *"We burst forth in flight, dart to the elm or sandalwood, and if we fall short, we simply land on the ground—why go ninety thousand *li* to the south?"* If one is content in one’s nature, then even the great Peng has no cause to value itself above the small bird, nor does the small bird envy the Celestial Pond, for its desires are already fulfilled. Thus, though great and small differ, their freedom is the same.

Those who journey to the near wilds return in a day with bellies still full; those who travel a hundred *li* must pound grain the night before; those who go a thousand *li* must gather provisions for three months. The farther the destination, the more provisions are needed—just as the greater the wings, the deeper the *qi* must be. What do these two creatures know?

These two creatures—the Peng and the shrimp—contrast great with small to show how their aims are equal. Why do their aims differ? Not because they knowingly choose difference, but because each follows its nature without contrivance. This is the essence of freedom.

Lesser knowledge does not reach greater knowledge; short years do not reach long years. All things have their nature, and each nature has its limit—like years and wisdom, how could mere striving attain what lies beyond? From this point to Liezi, examples of long and short years are given, each true to its kind, none sufficient to surpass the other.

Only the person free of all dependence, who forgets self and others, who merges with all differences—only such a one, whose freedom bears no name, can unite great and small. For if there is great and small, then even the Peng and the sparrow, the minister and the wind-rider, are equally bound by things. If there is life and death, then even the great *chun* tree and the mayfly, Pengzu and the morning mushroom, are equally cut short.

Thus, to wander where there is no great or small is to be boundless; to merge with what is neither life nor death is to be limitless. But if freedom is tied to a fixed direction, then even if set loose to roam, it will reach an end—for it is not yet free of dependence. How do we know this is so?

The morning mushroom knows not the waxing and waning of the moon; the summer cicada knows not spring and autumn—these are short years. South of Chu there is the *Mingling* tree, for which five hundred years make a spring, five hundred years an autumn; in high antiquity there was the great *zhuang* tree, for which eight thousand years make a spring, eight thousand years an autumn—these are long years. Yet Pengzu alone is famed for his longevity; for common people to compare themselves to him is truly lamentable!

The disparity in years and wisdom is so vast, yet when measured against what common people find lamentable, it is even more pitiable. But people never lament this, for each nature has its limit. If one knows this limit, then even the slightest difference cannot be bridged—what in the world is there to lament? Things never long for what is small because of its smallness, but always envy what is great because of its greatness. Yet the difference between great and small is fixed by nature, beyond the reach of envy or desire—thus the burden of envy and desire can be cut off.

Sorrow arises from bondage; when bondage is severed, sorrow departs. And when sorrow departs, life and destiny are at peace—this has never been otherwise. This is what Tang asked Ji about.

Tang’s question to Ji also concerned how all things have their limits, and how following them brings harmony. Thus Zhuangzi affirmed his question as correct.

North of the barren lands lies the Dark Sea—the Celestial Pond. There is a fish there, thousands of *li* in breadth, whose length no one knows; its name is Kun. There is a bird there, named Peng, whose back is like Mount Tai and whose wings are like clouds hanging from the sky. It spirals upward on the whirlwind for ninety thousand *li*, piercing the clouds and shouldering the blue sky, then sets its course south, bound for the Southern Darkness.

The marsh sparrow laughed at it, saying: "Where is it off to now? I leap and bound upward, but descend after only a few *ren*, soaring among the mugwort and wormwood—this is the perfection of flight. And where is it off to now?" This is the distinction between small and great.

Each attains its nature as perfection, exhausts itself as the limit. Earlier, it was said that the two creatures differ, so their destinations vary—one soars to the Celestial Pond, the other reaches only the elm and sandalwood—each is content with its form and sufficient, unaware of why it is so. Now, speaking of the distinction between small and great, each has its natural endowment. This is not something attainable by striving or admiration. Each rests in its own nature, untroubled by their differences, so the point is reiterated.

Thus, those whose knowledge suffices for a single office, whose conduct is matched by a single village, whose virtue aligns with a single lord, or whose merit is recognized by a single state—such men regard themselves as this sparrow does, content within their own bounds, like a bird at ease in its domain. Yet Song Rongzi would still laugh at them. Because he has not yet equalized all things, he finds cause for laughter.

Moreover, though the whole world praises him, he is not encouraged; though the whole world blames him, he is not discouraged. He has firmly grasped the division between inner and outer—within, the self; without, things. He distinguishes the boundary between honor and shame—honor for himself, shame for others. This is the limit. He cannot surpass it further. In his dealings with the world, he is not restless. He is sufficient unto himself, thus at ease in the world. Yet even so, he has not yet established the ultimate.

He can affirm himself, but cannot be without something to affirm. Liezi rode the wind and traveled, lightly and skillfully—*lingran*, the appearance of effortless grace. After fifteen days, he returned. If one relies on something, then even riding the wind is merely that—one cannot circumnavigate in an instant. In his pursuit of fortune, he was not restless. He naturally rode the wind, not seeking knowledge through restless striving. Though he avoided walking, he still depended on something. Without wind, he could not travel—this is necessarily reliance. Only one who accomplishes all without reliance is free from dependence.

As for one who rides the constancy of Heaven and Earth, and harnesses the transformations of the six qi, roaming the boundless—what does he rely on? Heaven and Earth are the collective names for the myriad things. Heaven and Earth take the myriad things as their body, and the myriad things must take spontaneity as their constancy. Spontaneity is that which is not done yet naturally is. Thus, the great Peng’s ability to ascend, the marsh sparrow’s ability to descend, the tree stump’s ability to grow, the morning mushroom’s ability to be short—all these are what spontaneity enables, not what effort enables. Not acting yet naturally able, this is what is meant by constancy.

Thus, riding the constancy of Heaven and Earth is to follow the nature of the myriad things; harnessing the transformations of the six qi is to roam the paths of change. Proceeding thus, where is there an end? What is harnessed and ridden—what more is there to rely on? This is the carefree wandering of one of utmost virtue, who merges self and other in profound unity.

If one relies on something, then even Liezi’s light and skillful flight cannot proceed without wind. He must obtain what he relies on before attaining carefree wandering—how much more so for the great Peng! Only one who merges with things and follows great transformation can be without reliance and always unobstructed. Is this merely self-unobstructed? It also guides those who rely on something, ensuring they do not lose what they depend on. If what is relied on is not lost, then it is equal to great unobstruction.

Thus, reliance and non-reliance are not opposed; each rests in its nature, the heavenly mechanism unfolds of itself, received without awareness—then there is nothing that cannot be distinct. Non-reliance is not enough to distinguish reliance, let alone the great and small of those who rely!

Therefore, the Perfected Person has no self—without self, one follows things and reaches the ultimate. The Spirit Man has no merit—things have never thanked spontaneity for their lives, yet must rejoice in reliance on acupuncture and stone. When principle reaches its limit, traces vanish. Now, following without assisting, one becomes one with the ultimate principle—thus, no merit. The Sage has no name. The Sage is merely the name for things attaining their nature; it is not enough to name how they attain it.

Yao offered the world to Xu You, saying: "The sun and moon have risen, yet the torch still burns—is it not difficult for its light to compare? The timely rain has fallen, yet one still irrigates—is it not laborious for its moisture to compare? You stand, and the world is ordered, yet I still occupy the throne. I see myself as lacking. I beg to offer the world to you."

Xu You replied: "You govern the world, and the world is already ordered. One who can order the world does not govern it. Thus, Yao governs by not governing—he does not order the world and yet it is ordered. Now, Xu You clearly understands that once order is achieved, there is nothing to rely on, and order truly comes from Yao. Thus, his words 'you govern' should be forgotten to seek what they illustrate. Yet some say that one who governs and orders is Yao, while one who does not govern and yet enables Yao to order is Xu You—this is far from the truth.

Order arises from non-governance; action emerges from non-action. Taking from Yao is sufficient—what need is there for Xu You? If one claims that only by folding one’s hands in silence within the mountains and forests can one be called non-active, this is why the teachings of Zhuangzi and Laozi are rejected by those in power. Those in power are necessarily bound to the realm of action and do not turn back—this is the reason. Yet if I were to replace you, would I do so for the sake of a name? A name is but the guest of reality."

Shall I then be but a guest to reality? One who takes on burdens faces things, while one who follows things encounters no opposition—thus Yao had no counterpart in the world, while Xu You and Hou Ji and Qi were his equals.

Why say it is so? One who merges with things cannot be separated from them. Thus, with no mind, responses arise purely from resonance; drifting like a boat untethered, east or west is not of one’s own doing. Therefore, one who takes no action yet shares all with the people goes nowhere without being the lord of the world. To rule thus is as if the world elevates itself—this is truly the virtue of the lord. But if one stands alone, loftily atop a mountain’s peak, this is not the natural inclination of man. To cling to one corner is but a partial preference—how can one claim it exclusively? Such a one is but a single thing among the common, an outer minister to Yao. To replace the inner lord with an outer minister is to bear the name of ruler without its true responsibility.

The wren nests in the deep forest, yet needs but a single branch; the mole drinks from the river, yet takes only to fill its belly. Each nature has its limit—when that limit is met, the rest of the world’s wealth is superfluous. Return to rest, O lord—I have no use for the world! If you have no use for it, why should Yao alone possess it? Clearly, one who embraces openness has no fixed direction, and so the world delights in elevating him without weariness.

Though the cook does not prepare the meal, the invocator does not step beyond the sacrificial vessels to take his place.

Cook and invocator each remain in their proper office; birds and beasts and the myriad things each are content with what they receive; Emperor Yao and Xu You each rest in what they encounter—this is the ultimate reality of the world. When each attains their reality, what more is there to do? It is enough to attain it alone.

Thus, though the paths of Yao and Xu You differ, their freedom is one. Jian Wu asked Lian Shu: *"I heard Jie Yu’s words—they were vast yet without measure, boundless and never returning. I was alarmed by them, like the Milky Way stretching endlessly; so far-reaching, so removed from human feeling."*

Lian Shu said: *"What did he say?"*

Jian Wu replied: *"On the distant Guye Mountain dwells a spirit-man—his skin is as if ice and snow, his grace as if a maiden’s. These are but borrowed words. The spirit-man is what we now call the sage. Though the sage stands in the halls of state, his heart is no different than if he were in the mountains and forests—how could the world recognize this? They see only his imperial canopy and jade seals and think these enough to bind his heart; they see him traverse mountains and rivers, sharing in the people’s affairs, and think these enough to weary his spirit. How could they know the perfection of the most perfect remains unharmed?

Now, to speak of one of royal virtue and lodge him on this mountain is to show that the world has no way to recognize him. Thus, he is placed beyond the farthest reaches, beyond the bounds of sight and hearing. The maiden does not let the outer harm the inner. She does not eat the five grains, but draws in the wind and drinks the dew. Both eat the five grains, yet only she becomes a spirit-man—this makes clear that the spirit-man is not made by the five grains, but is uniquely endowed with the wondrous qi of nature. She rides the clouds, drives flying dragons, and roams beyond the four seas. Her spirit is concentrated, so that things suffer no blight and the harvests ripen. Yet I deem this mad and do not believe it."*

One who embodies the spirit, dwells in the numinous, and exhausts the subtleties of principle—though abiding in the stillness of a quiet chamber—is mystically one with the farthest reaches beyond the four seas. Thus, they ride the Two Modes and harness the Six Abandonments, moving among the multitudes yet driving the myriad things. If nothing is not compliant, then floating clouds become their mount; if no form is not borne, then flying dragons become their steed. They leave the body behind and attain self-realization, though seemingly indifferent and without reliance. Sitting in forgetfulness, acting in forgetfulness, they forget even the act of forgetting—thus, their movement is like dragging withered wood, their stillness like a heap of dead ashes. This is what is meant by the spirit’s concentration. When the spirit is concentrated, what is not concentrated attains its own realization.

Yet the world judges all by what it sees—how could it ever believe this?

Lián Shū said: "Indeed. The blind cannot partake in the spectacle of patterns, nor the deaf in the sound of bells and drums. Is it only the body that suffers blindness and deafness? Knowledge, too, has its own. To dismiss the most profound and wondrous words as madness and refuse to believe them—this is the deafness and blindness of understanding. Such words are like timely rain. To say that what Jiē Yú spoke of is naturally sought by things, while those who are deaf and blind to knowledge deny its truth—this is to misunderstand the person and their virtue. They spread their influence across the ten thousand things, unifying the age and seeking to end its chaos. Who would toil and trouble themselves over the world’s affairs?

The heart of the sage reaches the ultimate convergence of the Two Modes and exhausts the wondrous numbers of the myriad things. Thus, they embody transformation and harmonize with change. There is nowhere they cannot go, pervading the ten thousand things so that nothing is not so. The world, in its chaos, seeks me, yet I have no mind. If I have no mind, why should I not respond to the world? Yet one who embodies the mysterious and exhausts the wondrous—through this, they harmonize with the natures of the ten thousand things and shape the transformations of the world, thereby attaining the fame of Yao and Shun. But they always act by means of non-action. Who would toil and trouble their spirit, exhausting their thoughts over affairs, and only then achieve it?

Such a person—nothing harms them. If one is at ease with what harms, then harm cannot harm; if harm cannot harm, then things do not harm them. The great flood may rise to the heavens without drowning them; the great drought may melt metal and stone, scorch earth and mountains without heating them. Wherever they go, they are at ease—every place is fitting. Life and death bring no change to them, much less drowning or scorching! Thus, the perfected one does not entangle themselves in calamity and disaster—not by avoiding it, but by following principle straightforwardly, naturally meeting with good fortune. Their dust and chaff, their husks and refuse, are what would mold Yao and Shun. Who would trouble themselves over things!

Yao and Shun are but names for worldly affairs; those who act for the sake of names are not truly named. Thus, Yao and Shun—are they merely Yao and Shun? There must be the reality of the spirit-man within. What the world calls Yao and Shun is but the dust and chaff, the husks and refuse of their names. The people of Song carried ceremonial caps to Yue, but the Yue people, with shorn hair and tattooed bodies, had no use for them. Yao governed the people of the world and brought order to the realm’s affairs, yet when he went to see the Four Masters on the distant Gu Ye Mountain, north of the Fen River, he was suddenly bereft of his world.

Yao’s non-use of the world is like the Yue people’s non-use of ceremonial caps. Yet one who leaves the world behind is truly revered by the world. Though the world reveres Yao, Yao never possessed the world. Thus, he was suddenly bereft of it, having long roamed his mind in the boundless darkness. Though seated above the ten thousand things, he was never not carefree. The Four Masters are but figures of speech to show that Yao was not confined to being Yao. Yao was truly in the dark; his traces were but Yao. To view the dark from the traces is to see inner and outer as separate realms—no wonder it is strange! The world sees only Yao as Yao—how could it recognize his darkness?"

Huì Zǐ said to Zhuāng Zǐ: "The King of Wei gave me seeds of a great gourd. I planted them, and they grew to hold five *dàn*. When I used it to hold water or broth, its strength was not enough to lift itself. When I split it to make a ladle, it was too large and unwieldy to hold anything. It was not that it wasn’t vast—it was that I deemed it useless and smashed it."

Zhuāng Zǐ said: "You are truly unskilled in using the great. There was a man of Song who was good at making a salve that prevented chapped hands. For generations, his family made their living by washing silk floss in water. The salve kept their hands from cracking, so they could always bleach silk in the water. A traveler heard of it and offered a hundred gold pieces for the recipe. The family gathered and deliberated: ‘For generations, we’ve washed silk floss and earned only a few gold pieces. Now, in one morning, we can sell this skill for a hundred gold. Let’s give it to him.’ The traveler obtained it and persuaded the King of Wu. When Yue raised a rebellion, the King of Wu sent him to lead the troops. In winter, they fought a naval battle with Yue, and he utterly defeated the Yue forces, winning a fiefdom. The ability to prevent chapped hands was the same, yet one man gained a fiefdom while another remained washing silk floss—the difference lay in how it was used.

Now you have a gourd that holds five *dàn*—why not consider making a great float and drifting upon the rivers and lakes, instead of worrying that it is too large and unwieldy to hold anything? Then you still have the heart of a clogged reed!"

A clogged reed is not one who attains thorough understanding. This passage speaks of how things each have their proper use. If one finds the proper use, where can one not be carefree?

Huì Zǐ said to Zhuāng Zǐ: "I have a great tree—people call it a *chū*. Its great trunk is gnarled and does not meet the inked line; its small branches are twisted and do not fit the compass or square. It stands by the road, yet carpenters do not glance at it. Now your words are vast but useless—just what everyone rejects."

Zhuāng Zǐ said: "Have you never seen a wildcat or weasel? They crouch low to lie in wait, pouncing on their prey. They leap east and west, not avoiding high or low..."

They are caught in traps and snares and die in nets. Now consider the yak: its size is like a cloud hanging in the sky. This creature is great indeed, yet it cannot catch mice. You have a great tree and fret that it is useless—why not plant it in the land of Nothing Whatever, the vast and boundless wilds? Wander idly at its side, roam carefree beneath its shade. No axe will fell it prematurely, no thing will harm it. Useless? Where then will you find trouble?"

For things great and small, if they miss their proper measure, the principles of benefit and harm are the same. When use finds its proper place, all things are carefree.

---

In *The Equality of Things*, Book Two of the *Inner Chapters* of the *Zhuangzi*:

Nan Guo Ziqi leaned against his armrest, gazed at the sky, and sighed, his response so absent it seemed he had lost his counterpart. Yan Cheng Ziyou stood in attendance before him and asked, "What is this? Can the body truly be made like withered wood, the heart like dead ashes? The one who leans here now is not the one who leaned here before."

Those who affirm themselves and deny others, who praise themselves and despise others—all things are like this. Yet though right and wrong differ, self and other are equal. To unite with Heaven and humanity, to equalize self and other, is to be outwardly without companions for joy, and in response, the body dissolves as if it has lost its match. Dead ashes and withered wood take their stillness and lack of feeling. Those who follow nature and forget right and wrong—what do their bodies hold but the solitary task of Heaven’s truth? Thus, when still, they stand like withered trees; when moving, they sway like dry branches. When seated, they are like dead ashes; when walking, like drifting dust. In their movements and stillness, I cannot find unity. In their effortless self-possession, I cannot find duality.

Ziyou had seen those who leaned on armrests before, but never one like Ziqi. "I have lost myself," he said, "and in losing myself, I have forgotten all things. If I have forgotten all things, what in the world is worth recognizing? Thus, I forget both inner and outer, and only then do I transcend and attain both."

Ziqi said, "Yan, how excellent that you ask! Just now, I lost myself—did you know it? You have heard the *human pipes*, but not the *earthly pipes*; you have heard the *earthly pipes*, but not the *Heavenly pipes*."

The *pipes* are flutes. Flutes and reeds vary in length, their notes differing in pitch, producing myriad sounds of varying heights and depths. Though the sounds are myriad, the measure they receive is one, and thus there is no place for superiority or inferiority to intervene.

Consider the things of wind: different sounds share the same nature, each taking what is its own—thus the *pipes of Heaven* are revealed.

Ziyou said, "May I ask the method?"

Ziqi said, "The Great Clod exhales its qi, and its name is wind. It does nothing, yet when it acts, the myriad hollows roar in anger. Have you alone not heard its long sighing? The Great Clod is nothing. Does the exhalation of qi require a thing? The qi simply exhales of itself. When things are born, none do not arise of themselves—thus the body of self-arising is vast, and so it is named the Great Clod.

The towering forests and jagged cliffs, the great trees with hollows as wide as a man’s embrace—some resemble noses, some mouths, some ears, some hairpins, some rings, some mortars, some depressions, some pools. They hiss, they shriek, they scold, they inhale, they cry, they wail, they moan, they howl. Those in front sing *yu*, those behind sing *yong*. A gentle breeze elicits a small harmony; a whirlwind, a great harmony. When the fierce wind ceases, all hollows become empty.

Have you alone not seen their swaying, their quivering?"

The wind does nothing, yet when it acts, the myriad hollows all rage and produce sound. The sound of the long wind is what the great wind stirs. Here, the shapes of the hollows are briefly described. Here, the differing sounds of the hollows are briefly described. Though the notes of sound vary endlessly in pitch and harmony, in loudness and softness, none fail to match what they receive and each finds its proper place. *Ji* means to cease. When the fierce wind blows, the hollows are full; when it ceases, they are empty. Though fullness and emptiness differ, in each finding its proper place they are the same. *Tiaotiao* and *daodao* describe the appearance of movement. Though the sounds of things differ, and the movements of their forms differ as well, in attaining equality they are one. Is *tiaotiao* alone right and *daodao* alone wrong?

Ziyou said, "The *earthly pipes* are simply the myriad hollows; the *human pipes* are simply the bamboo flutes. May I ask about the *Heavenly pipes*?"

Ziqi said, "The *Heavenly pipes* blow through the myriad differences, yet make them return to themselves. All take what is their own—who is it that stirs them to anger?"

These are the *Heavenly pipes*. Do the *Heavenly pipes* require some other thing? They are simply the hollows, the bamboo flutes, and all that joins the realm of the living, gathering to form a single Heaven. Nonbeing cannot generate being; being, before it is born, cannot be the source of life."

Then who is it that gives life to life? It simply arises of itself, spontaneously. It arises of itself—it is not that I give life to it. Since I cannot give life to things, and things cannot give life to me, then I am self-so. Being self-so, it is called "Heavenly-so." It is Heavenly-so, not something made, and thus we speak of it as Heaven. [Speaking of it as Heaven] is to clarify its spontaneity—how could it refer to the azure vault? Yet some say the *Heavenly pipes* command things to follow them. But if Heaven itself cannot possess itself, how could it possess things? Thus, "Heaven" is but the collective name for the myriad things. None is uniquely Heaven; who then masters and commands things? Therefore, things each arise of themselves, with no source from which they emerge—this is the Dao of Heaven. All things attain their own nature—who stirs them to anger, making them so?

This further clarifies the *Heavenly pipes*.

The great wisdom is leisurely, the small wisdom is meddlesome.
The great words blaze forth, the small words chatter on.
In sleep, their spirits intertwine; in waking, their forms open.
They engage in contention, daily battling with their hearts.
Some are vague, some are guarded, some are secretive.
Small fears make them tremble; great fears leave them dazed.
Their outbursts are like the release of a crossbow—this is what it means to wield judgments.
Their restraints are like sworn oaths—this is what it means to cling to victory.
Their decline is like autumn and winter, speaking of their daily decay.
Their immersion in their pursuits cannot be reversed.
Their weariness is like a sealed vessel, speaking of their aging and stagnation.
The heart near death cannot be revived.
Joy and anger, sorrow and delight, worry and sighing, change and fear, frivolity and indulgence, coquetry and affectation—
pleasure arises from emptiness, steam gives rise to mushrooms.
Day and night they alternate before us, yet none knows where they sprout.
Enough! Enough! Morning and evening, this is how they come to be—this is how life arises!

Here lies the difference in knowledge.
Here lies the difference in speech.
Here lies the difference between waking and sleeping.
Here lies the difference in interaction.
Here lies the difference in fear and trembling.
Here lies the difference in movement and stillness.
Their decay and daily decline are like this.
Their immersion leading to irreversible change is like this.
Their drowning in desire, aging and growing ever more stagnant, is like this.
Their pursuit of profit and disregard for calamity, secretly plotting to achieve their aims, is like this.
Here lie the differences in nature and disposition.
Here lie the differences in the vicissitudes of affairs.

From this point onward, we have briefly set forth the boundlessness of the *Heavenly pipes*; from this point downward, we clarify the spontaneity of the boundless.

Things each are self-so, not knowing why they are so yet being so. Thus, though their forms may differ endlessly, their *so-ness* is endlessly the same. Day and night alternate, the old giving way to the new. The myriad things of Heaven and Earth transform and renew daily, advancing with time—what thing gives rise to them? They are simply self-so, spontaneously so. This speaks of their self-arising. It is not that without the other, I would not exist; nor is it that without me, there would be nothing to take. This is close, yet one does not know what compels it. As if there were a true master, yet no trace of it can be found. One may act in good faith, yet not see its form—it has essence but no form.

That is spontaneity. Spontaneity gives life to me; I give life to myself spontaneously. Thus, spontaneity is simply my own spontaneity—how distant could it be? All things, in their multitude, are simply thus of themselves; they are not made to be so by one another. Therefore, if one leaves them be, principle will arrive of itself. The myriad things and their myriad dispositions take different paths and make different choices, as if some true master made them so. Yet when one seeks the traces of this true master, one finds nothing in the end. This makes clear that all things are spontaneous, with nothing making them so.

Consider one who walks: he trusts that he can walk, yet does not see the form that enables him to do so. His disposition matches the thing, so the form is not separately perceived. The face, the nine orifices, the six viscera—all are complete and present. With whom should I be intimate? Do you delight in all of them? Or do you have a preference? Are they all like servants? Are the servants insufficient to govern one another? Do they take turns being lord and servant? Or is there a true lord present? Whether one seeks to grasp its essence or not, it neither adds to nor detracts from its reality. Entrust it to spontaneity, and all will be preserved. It simply preserves itself. If one delights in all, then there is partiality. With partiality, one cannot preserve all completely. Thus, it is not by delighting in them that they are preserved, nor by acting that they arise of themselves. If one were partial to all, then intentions would exceed their proper bounds, high and low would encroach upon one another, and none would be servants. If servants’ talents are not content with the role of servants, they are lost. Thus, the principle of lord and servant, high and low, outer and inner, is the natural pattern of Heaven—how could it be the doing of a true person?

Servants simply each fulfill their proper role; this is not insufficient for mutual governance. Mutual governance is like the hands, feet, ears, and eyes, the four limbs and hundred parts, each having its function and alternating in their use. Those whom the times deem worthy become lords; those whose talents do not match the age become servants. Just as Heaven is naturally high and Earth naturally low, the head naturally resides above and the feet naturally reside below—how could there be alternation? Though there is no error in their proper places, they are necessarily so of themselves. If one entrusts them to their own nature, then it is not artificial.

All who attain their true nature and employ their own spontaneity, though they be mere menials, still do not heed slander or praise but rest content in their work. Thus, whether known or unknown, all remain as they are. But if one opens the path of vain hopes, with inferiors encroaching upon superiors, things lose their reality, people forget their roots, and in the space between slander and praise, they lose their bearings.

Once one receives this completed form, one does not perish until its end. Locked in mutual conflict and wear, one’s conduct rushes forward like a galloping horse, yet none can stop it—is this not lamentable? Toiling one’s whole life without seeing success, wearily exhausted yet not knowing where one is headed—is this not sorrowful?

People call it "not dying," but what benefit is there? The form dissolves, and the heart follows suit—can this not be called great sorrow? Is human life inherently so deluded? Am I alone deluded, or are there others who are not?

Each nature has its allotted portion. Thus, the wise guard their knowledge to the end, while the foolish cling to their folly until death—who can alter their nature midway? The myriad beings, in their teeming multitudes, clash in discord and accord, each trusting their own partial views and indulging their own conduct, none able to turn back. This—compared to what the common people deem sorrowful—is indeed sorrowful. Yet the common people never regard it as sorrow, for it is the nature of their nature. Each thing follows its own nature—what in the world is worth grieving over?

The passions of things know no bounds; those who know contentment are few. Thus, having attained this, they do not stop but pursue that instead. All exhaust themselves their whole lives, never sating their ambitions, ceasing only in death. Therefore, their success is never seen. All things, driven by their preferences, wear out their bodies until they are weary and spent, yet do not know where their loves ultimately lead! In truth, this is no different from death.

When heart and form race together, exhausted yet unable to turn back, this surpasses what the common people deem sorrowful—here lies the greatest sorrow. Yet the common people never take this as sorrow, so what they sorrow over is not truly sorrowful.

All the matters above occur without knowing why they occur—hence, delusion. Now, those who do not know all do not know how they know yet know nonetheless; the living all do not know how they live yet live nonetheless. Though the myriad things differ, in that their living does not arise from knowing, there is no difference—thus, none in the world are not deluded.

If one follows their completed heart and takes it as teacher, who alone would lack a teacher? Why must one know the succession of ages to possess it? Even the foolish have it! To have affirmations and negations before the heart is complete is like arriving in Yue today when you set out yesterday. Thus, to take what is not as what is.

To take what is not as what is, even the divine Yu could not comprehend—what can I alone do? The heart’s sufficiency to govern the body’s functions is called the completed heart. If one takes their completed heart as teacher, then each person has their own teacher. Each person having their own teacher, they entrust themselves to it and find it fitting.

To use the completed to replace the incomplete is not knowledge—it is the heart’s own attainment. Thus, even the foolish take their completed heart as teacher, unwilling to employ what they deem deficient and abandon what they deem sufficient. If you arrive in Yue today, how did you get there yesterday? Before the heart is complete, how do affirmations and negations arise?

Those who clarify affirmations and negations cannot be without them, so the perfected person accords with both. In principle, there is no affirmation or negation, yet the deluded take them as existing—this is taking what is not as what is. Once the deluded heart is formed, even sages cannot dispel it, so one entrusts it to its natural state without forcing knowledge.

Words are not like the wind’s blowing; speakers have their words. Yet what they say is not yet settled. Is there truly speech? Or has there never been speech? Do they differ from the chirping of fledglings, or is there discernment? Or is there no discernment? Each has their own say, hence differing from the wind. I deem it right, while another deems it wrong; what another deems right, I deem wrong—thus, it is not settled.

Not settled because of the partiality of their perspectives. Do they have words? Yet these are insufficient to settle anything. Do they have no words? Yet they already have words based on themselves. Words and the chirping of fledglings reach the same end—whether there is discernment or not is truly unsettled.

The world’s sentiments need not be the same, yet their words cannot differ, so affirmations and negations arise in profusion, with no settled ground. Where is the Dao concealed to have truth and falsity? Where are words concealed to have affirmation and negation? Where does the Dao go yet not abide? Where do words abide yet remain untrustworthy?

The Dao is concealed in petty accomplishments; words are concealed in florid embellishments. Hence, there are the affirmations and negations of the Confucians and Mohists, each affirming what the other negates and negating what the other affirms. To affirm what they negate and negate what they affirm, nothing surpasses using illumination.

Where is the Dao not present? How are words concealed to have truth and falsity, giving rise to the names of affirmation and negation? All abide. All are permissible.

Petty accomplishments and florid embellishments naturally conceal the Dao, yet the Dao cannot be concealed. Thus, truth and falsity, affirmation and negation arise in florid embellishments yet cease in proper accord; they appear in petty accomplishments yet vanish in great wholeness.

Confucians and Mohists alternately affirm and negate each other, yet all in the world are Confucians and Mohists. Thus, the hundred schools arise, each prizing their own views, never departing from their own domains.

Those who have affirmations and negations are what the Confucians and Mohists affirm; those who have no affirmations and no negations are what the Confucians and Mohists negate. Now, to affirm what the Confucians and Mohists negate and negate what they affirm is to illuminate the absence of affirmations and negations. To illuminate the absence of affirmations and negations, nothing surpasses using the Confucians and Mohists to mutually clarify each other.

When mutually clarifying, what they affirm is not affirmed, and what they negate is not negated. Not negated, there is no negation; not affirmed, there is no affirmation. Nothing is not "that"; nothing is not "this." From "that," one does not see; from knowing, one knows. Thus, it is said: "That emerges from this, and this also relies on that." This is the theory of "that" and "this" arising together.

Yet even as they arise, they die; even as they die, they arise. Even as they are permissible, they are not; even as they are not, they are permissible. Following affirmation, following negation; following negation, following affirmation. Therefore, the sage does not proceed thus but illuminates it by the heavens—this too is following affirmation.

All things deem themselves right, hence nothing is not "this."

All things deem others "that," hence nothing is not "that." Nothing is not "that," then the world has no "this"; nothing is not "this," then the world has no "that." Without "that" and without "this," this is called the profound unity.

The partiality of things arises from their failure to see what others see, knowing only what they themselves know. Knowing only what they themselves know, they deem themselves right. Deeming themselves right, they regard others as wrong. Thus it is said: "That emerges from this, and this also relies on that"—"that" and "this" arise in mutual dependence.

The transformations of life and death are like the progression of the four seasons—spring, autumn, winter, summer. Though the states of life and death differ, in each finding peace in what they encounter, they are one. Now, the living insist that life is life, while the dead insist that life is death—thus there is no life. The living insist that death is death, while the dead insist that death is life—thus there is no death. Without life and without death, without affirmation and without negation, the debates of Confucians and Mohists are what I cannot align with. Yet when each abides in its own lot, that is what I cannot oppose.

The one who embraces openness follows the world’s affirmations and negations yet remains free of them. Thus, not proceeding by the path of affirmation and negation, yet ensuring no error in either, is simply to illuminate their natural state without imposing anything. This is also "that," and "that" is also "this." "That" has its own affirmations and negations, and "this" has its own affirmations and negations. Is there truly a "that" and "this"? Or is there truly no "that" and "this"? Where "that" and "this" find no counterpart, this is called the pivot of the Dao. Once the pivot is grasped at the center of the ring, it can respond endlessly. Affirmation is one endlessness, and negation is another endlessness. Thus it is said: "Nothing compares to illumination."

I, too, am deemed "that" by "that." "That," too, deems itself right. "This," too, deems itself right and "that" wrong; "that," too, deems itself right and "this" wrong. Within each, there is one affirmation and one negation. Now, if one calls "that" "that," yet "that" still deems itself right; if one calls "this" "this," yet "this" is still deemed "that" by "that." Thus, the presence or absence of "that" and "this" remains undetermined.

A counterpart is an opposition. "That" and "this" are opposites, yet the sage harmonizes both. Thus, the one without intent merges with things and never sets up oppositions in the world. The pivot is the essential point. Abiding in this essential point and meeting the profound extremity, one responds to the boundless. Affirmation and negation cycle endlessly, hence they are called a ring. The center of the ring is empty. Now, if one takes affirmation and negation as the ring and attains its center, one attains neither affirmation nor negation. Without affirmation and without negation, one can respond to all affirmations and negations. Since affirmations and negations are endless, the response is also endless.

The world deems itself right and others wrong, hence one affirmation and one negation proceed endlessly. Only the one who enters emptiness and attains the center remains unbound, riding it in freedom. To illustrate the non-finger with a finger is not as good as illustrating the non-finger with a non-finger. To illustrate the non-horse with a horse is not as good as illustrating the non-horse with a non-horse. Heaven and earth are one finger; the myriad things are one horse.

To deem oneself right and others wrong is the common tendency of "that" and "this." Thus, if I use my finger to illustrate "that" finger, then "that" finger alone becomes a non-finger to my finger—this is illustrating the non-finger with a finger. If, in turn, "that" finger is used to illustrate my finger, then my finger becomes a non-finger to "that" finger—this is illustrating the non-finger with a non-finger. To clarify the absence of affirmation and negation, nothing compares to reciprocal illustration. Through reciprocal illustration, "that" and "this" are alike in deeming themselves right and alike in deeming each other wrong. Alike in deeming each other wrong, the world has no right; alike in deeming themselves right, the world has no wrong.

How can this be demonstrated? If affirmation were truly affirmation, the world would have no one to negate it. If negation were truly negation, the world would have no one to affirm it. Now, with no fixed ground for affirmation or negation, they are confused and chaotic. This shows that these petty distinctions are merely each trusting their own partial views and aligning in uniformity. Looking up and looking down, all are like this. Thus, the perfected person knows that heaven and earth are one finger and the myriad things are one horse. Therefore, they abide in vast tranquility, and heaven, earth, and the myriad things each rest in their lot, merging in self-contentment without affirmation or negation.

Is it permissible? Is it not permissible? What is permissible to oneself is called permissible; what is not permissible to oneself is called not permissible. The Dao is completed in its course, and things are so called by others. There is that by which it is permissible, and that by which it is not; there is that by which it is so, and that by which it is not. How is it so? It is so in being so. How is it not so? It is not so in not being so. How is it permissible? It is permissible in being permissible. How is it not permissible? It is not permissible in not being permissible.

Things inherently have that by which they are so, and inherently have that by which they are permissible. Nothing is not so, and nothing is not permissible. Thus, I raise the examples of a stalk and a pillar, of an ugly woman and Xi Shi, of the expansive, the strange, the deceitful, and the bizarre—all are united as one. In their division, they are completed; in their completion, they are destroyed. All things are without completion and destruction, for they are united as one. There is nothing that is not completed, nothing that is not so. Each is so in its own way, each is permissible in its own way.

A stalk lies horizontally, while a pillar stands vertically; an ugly woman is repulsive, while Xi Shi is beautiful. What is meant by equality is not necessarily equal in form or identical in measure! Thus, I raise the examples of vertical and horizontal, beauty and ugliness, the expansive, the strange, the deceitful, and the bizarre—each is so in its own way, each is permissible in its own way. Though their principles are myriad, their natures share the same attainment. Thus it is said: "The Dao unites all as one."

Things may be deemed dissolution by one and completion by another. What I call completion, another may call destruction. Completion and destruction arise from seeing only oneself and not the other. Thus, there is no completion or destruction, just as there is no right or wrong. Only the enlightened know that all is united as one, and though they do not employ such distinctions, they lodge them in the ordinary. Following what is so, affirming it without knowing why—this is called the Dao.

The enlightened are not confined to one perspective; thus, they suddenly forget themselves and entrust their function to spontaneous use. In spontaneous use, all things flow smoothly and find their own fulfillment. "Few" means "exhaustion." The ultimate principle is exhausted in self-fulfillment. The enlightened follow without contrivance. Do they know that following is good and thus follow? They do not know why they follow—they simply follow, and so it is called the Dao.

To toil the spirit in pursuit of unity while failing to recognize its sameness—this is called "morning three." What is "morning three"? The monkey keeper distributed chestnuts, saying, "Three in the morning and four in the evening." All the monkeys were enraged. He then said, "Very well, four in the morning and three in the evening." All the monkeys were pleased. The names and realities remained unchanged, yet joy and anger were put to use—this, too, is following what is so. Therefore, the sage harmonizes with right and wrong and rests in the balance of heaven, which is called "walking two paths."

Does the enlightened one labor the spirit to achieve unity? If one labors the spirit to achieve unity, it is not to be relied upon, for it differs not from those who fail to unite. It is no different from the monkeys' delusion, affirming themselves by what they prefer. Without partiality, one entrusts it to spontaneous balance and leaves it at that. Entrusting the world’s right and wrong to this principle:

The ancients’ knowledge reached its limit. To what limit? Some held that things had never yet begun to exist—this is the limit, the exhaustion, beyond which nothing can be added! Next were those who acknowledged the existence of things but had not yet imposed boundaries. Next were those who acknowledged boundaries but had not yet introduced right and wrong. The manifestation of right and wrong is the diminishment of the Dao; the diminishment of the Dao is the formation of attachment. Is there truly completion and diminishment? Or is there truly neither completion nor diminishment?

If there is completion and diminishment, then Master Zhao’s zither playing exists. If there is neither completion nor diminishment, then Master Zhao’s not playing the zither exists. Master Zhao’s zither playing, Shi Kuang’s wielding the baton, and Master Hui’s leaning against the parasol tree—these three were among the most eminent in knowledge, and so their stories are recorded at life’s end. Yet because they were fond of their ways, they differed from others. Their fondness led them to seek clarity, but what they sought to clarify was not truly clarifiable, so they ended in the obscurity of "hard and white." And Master Zhao’s son spent his life weaving the threads of his father’s art, yet achieved nothing. If this can be called completion, then though I have achieved nothing, it too can be called completion. If this cannot be called completion, then neither things nor I have achieved anything.

Thus, the dazzling confusion of doubt is what the sage contemplates. Not employing such distinctions but lodging them in the ordinary—this is called "illuminating through clarity." This is to forget heaven and earth, to leave behind the myriad things, to look outward without examining the cosmos, to look inward without perceiving one’s own body. Thus, one is vast and unburdened, moving with things and responding to all without exception.

Though one may not yet have forgotten everything, one can still forget the distinction between self and other. Though one may not yet have forgotten self and other, one can still forget their right and wrong. Without the Dao’s diminishment, emotions would not be partial, and attachment would not form. Failing to forget attachment and relinquish self-interest, one cannot achieve the profound unity of self and other. This is not perfection.

To know neither existence nor nonexistence—this is the ultimate limit.

The myriad sounds cannot all be enumerated. Thus, when blowing pipes or plucking strings, though the fingers may move intricately, many sounds are still lost. Those who grasp the flute or strike the zither seek to manifest sound, yet in manifesting sound, sound is lost; in not manifesting sound, sound remains whole. Thus, to strive for completion and thereby diminish it—this is how Zhaowen plays the zither. Not to strive and thus remain undiminished—this is how Zhaowen does not play the zither. "Almost" means "exhaustion." These three masters all sought to clarify what they themselves did not understand, thus exhausting their knowledge and depleting their thoughts, their bodies wearied and their spirits fatigued. Some leaned on their staffs and dozed, others rested against a tree and closed their eyes. They relied on their vigor to endure, for without it, they would have succumbed long ago.

These three spoke only of what they themselves understood, considering themselves distinct from the multitude. They sought to display their understanding to others, desiring that all align with their own preferences. This is like playing the reed pipes to an ox. Since others ultimately do not understand, their own Dao and techniques end in obscurity. Zhaowen’s son, in continuing his father’s legacy, likewise failed to achieve completion. Though these three sought to enlighten others, those others ultimately did not understand, and so they accomplished nothing in their lifetimes. If these three could be called accomplished, then even my own lack of accomplishment could be called accomplishment. All things illuminate themselves but do not illuminate others. If, because others do not understand, one deems them unaccomplished, then all things would be without accomplishment.

Thus, the sage does not display this to dazzle that, nor abandons the self to pursue things. Following and entrusting them to their own natures, each attains its proper capacity in obscurity. Thus, all things achieve completion without omission. Now, these three sought to display their own preferences to others—was this not delusional?

The sage is without self. Thus, when faced with the dazzling confusion of doubt, they chart and contain it; when confronted with the vast and strange, they unify and harmonize it. They allow all differences to rest in what they naturally find restful, so that the multitude does not lose what they deem right. Thus, the sage is not employed by things, and the use of all things is fully realized. When all things employ themselves, who is right and who is wrong? Therefore, even in the face of unbridled transformations and strange deviations, bending to follow them and entrusting them to their own use, though their uses may differ in ten thousand ways, all becomes clearly self-evident.

Now, suppose I were to speak here—how would I know if it is of the same kind as this? Or not of the same kind? Whether of the same kind or not, all are of the same kind when taken together, and thus no different from that. Nevertheless, let me attempt to speak:

There is a beginning; there is that which precedes the beginning; there is that which precedes what precedes the beginning. There is being; there is non-being; there is that which precedes non-being; there is that which precedes what precedes non-being. Suddenly there is being and non-being, yet we do not know which of the two—being or non-being—is truly so. Now, I have already spoken, yet I do not know if what I have spoken is truly speaking or truly not speaking.

If I say there is no right or wrong, how do I know if this is of the same kind as those who say there is right and wrong, or not? If I wish to call it the same kind, then I take non-being as right, while they take non-being as wrong—thus, it is not the same kind. Yet though this right and wrong differ, they are still not free from right and wrong, and thus are of the same kind as that. Therefore, whether of the same kind or not, all are of the same kind when taken together, and thus no different from that.

If one wishes to avoid such great dissimilarity, nothing is better than having no mind. Having already relinquished right and wrong, one must further relinquish the act of relinquishing. Relinquishing the relinquishing again and again until there is no relinquishing left, then there is no relinquishing and nothing not relinquished, and right and wrong vanish of themselves. The ultimate principle is without words; to speak is to fall into categories. Thus, I tentatively entrust these words to this purpose.

If there is a beginning, there is an end. To speak of no end or beginning and to equalize life and death—those who equalize have not yet equaled as well as those who do not equalize and yet are naturally equal. This is to forget even the act of equalizing.

Where there is being, beauty and ugliness, right and wrong all arise. Where there is non-being but one does not yet know the absence of absence, right and wrong, love and hate still linger in the heart. Even knowing the absence of absence, one has not yet ceased knowing. To utterly forget this knowing—only then does one suddenly begin to comprehend nothingness. In comprehending nothingness, heaven and earth, the myriad things, self and other, right and wrong—all become clearly resolved.

To say there is no right or wrong is itself to speak. Yet not knowing whether this speaking is being or non-being—only then is the mind utterly unburdened, without the slightest trace of dust.

In the world, nothing is smaller than the tip of an autumn hair, yet Mount Tai is small; nothing lives shorter than an infant who dies young, yet Pengzu is short-lived. Heaven and earth are born together with me, and the myriad things and I are one. Since we are already one, can there be speech? Since we have already called it one, can there be no speech? One and speech make two; two and one make three. From this point onward, even the most skilled mathematician cannot arrive at a number, much less an ordinary person! Thus, from non-being to being, one reaches three—how much more so from being to being! Do not proceed further; rest here!

When things are compared by form, Mount Tai is greater than an autumn hair. But if each abides by its own nature and attains its limit, then a large form is not excessive, nor is a small form insufficient. If each is sufficient in its nature, then an autumn hair is not merely small in its smallness, nor is Mount Tai merely great in its greatness. If sufficiency in nature is deemed greatness, then nothing in the world is greater than an autumn hair. If sufficiency in nature is not deemed greatness, then even Mount Tai may be called small. Thus, it is said: "In the world, nothing is smaller than the tip of an autumn hair, yet Mount Tai is small." If Mount Tai is small, then nothing in the world is great; if an autumn hair is great, then nothing in the world is small. Without small or great, without long life or early death, the cicada does not envy the great Peng tree but rejoices in its own contentment; the sparrow does not prize the heavenly pond but takes pride in its own sufficiency.

If one is sufficient in Heaven’s natural endowment and at peace with one’s nature and fate, then even heaven and earth are not long-lived enough to surpass me in birth, nor are the myriad things so different as to differ from my own attainment. If heaven and earth are born, why should they not be born alongside me? If the myriad things attain, why should they not attain as one with me?

The myriad things in their myriad forms all find sufficiency in themselves, and their attainment is one. Once one has attained oneness, there is no principle left to speak of. Names and terms arise from a lack of clarity. Some things cannot clarify their own oneness and thus pursue others through these terms, so they use the term "one" to correct them. Once they have termed it "one," they have already resorted to speech. To speak of oneness with speech, when oneness is not speech, is to make oneness and speech two. Oneness is already one, yet speech makes it two; with one and two, how can this not be called three? To speak of oneness with a single word is still to produce three—how much more so when tracing its branches and all things’ distinct names? Even with skilled calculation, none can keep account of them.

Thus, those who term oneness do not differ from others, while those who forget oneness remain silent and attain oneness of themselves. Each abiding in what it can do is most fitting. The Dao has never had boundaries, nor has speech ever had constancy; it is for this reason that divisions arise. Let me speak of these divisions: there is left, there is right, there are relationships, there are duties, there are distinctions, there are debates, there is competition, there is contention—these are called the eight virtues.

Beyond the six directions, the sage preserves but does not discuss; within the six directions, the sage discusses but does not debate; regarding the *Spring and Autumn* and the records of former kings’ aspirations, the sage debates but does not argue. Thus, where there are divisions, there are also non-divisions; where there is debate, there is also non-debate.

Someone asks: "Why is this?" The answer: "The sage embraces it, while the multitude debates it to show one another. Thus it is said: debate arises from what is not seen." Obscure yet omnipresent, when spoken of in terms of "this" and "that," right and wrong have no fixed ground. The Dao is unbounded, so the myriad things freely occupy their own domains, each differing in what suits them. Each thing has its principle, each affair its fittingness. They are grouped by kind and distinguished by category. Pursuing together is called competition; opposing debate is called contention. Briefly distinguishing them, we have these eight virtues.

What lies beyond the six directions refers to what is outside the nature and lot of things. Though principle exists even in what lies beyond things’ natures, since it is not within their nature and lot, it does not move the sage, and so the sage does not discuss it. If he were to discuss it, it would be like leading the myriad things to learn what they cannot. Thus, he does not discuss what lies beyond, and the eight divisions are all subsumed in self-sufficiency. He sets forth their natures and lets them rest in them, following their completed traces and congealing at the ultimate of what is fitting, without clinging to what he deems right to condemn others.

Each thing divides itself, each affair distinguishes itself. To desire to divide and distinguish them by oneself is to fail to see their self-distinction. The sage harbors no such intent; he does not see their self-debate, so he debates what he knows to show them. The great Dao is not named; great debate does not speak; great benevolence is not benevolent; great integrity is not modest; great courage is not aggressive. When the Dao is made manifest, it is no longer the Dao; when speech is debating, it does not reach; when benevolence is constant, it is not all-encompassing; when integrity is pure, it is not trustworthy; when courage is aggressive, it does not succeed. These five, though not accomplished, nearly approach the proper direction.

Entrusting it to self-naming, there is nothing to name. It distinguishes itself. Existing without attachment, it preserves itself. The utterly sufficient one is not affected by things’ comings and goings, so there is nowhere to harbor modesty or fullness. Going nowhere without compliance, it can traverse no peril without going. Using this to clarify that, both this and that are lost. It cannot reach their self-division.

Things have no constant attachment, and constant attachment is never all-encompassing. Brightly pure integrity is merely the greed of one who covets fame—it is not true integrity. Aggressive, contrary courage is detested by all under heaven, leaving no ground to stand on. These five all harm what is fitting by acting, unable to rest in their original nature and endlessly seeking what lies beyond. Seeking what cannot be sought is like trying to learn the square from the round or a fish longing to be a bird. Though one may aspire to the phoenix and emulate the sun and moon, the closer one draws to them, the farther one strays from reality; the more one learns, the more one loses one’s nature. Thus, when things are equalized, the burden of partial preferences is gone.

Therefore, to know to stop at what one does not know is perfection. Who knows the debate that does not speak, the Dao that is not the Dao? If there is one who can know this, it is called the Heavenly Storehouse. Pour into it without filling it; draw from it without depleting it; yet one does not know whence it comes—this is called preserving the light. What one does not know lies outside the nature and lot of things. Thus, stopping within what one knows is perfection. Vast and all-embracing, it entrusts everything to its course.

The mind of the perfected one is like a mirror: it responds but does not store, and so remains open and free from the changes of fullness and emptiness. The coming of the ultimate principle is naturally without trace. Allowing it to clarify itself, its light is never exhausted.

Thus, long ago, Yao asked Shun: "I wish to attack Zong, Kuai, and Xu’ao, yet when I face south, I cannot put it aside. What is the reason?" Shun said: "Those three lords dwell among the mugwort and reeds. Why can you not put it aside? Long ago, ten suns appeared together, and the myriad things were all illuminated—how much more so one whose virtue surpasses the sun!" Yao’s way of peaceful compliance was not yet vast, so he listened to court affairs without joy. He sought to entrust the principle of clarity and equality to the great sage, so he posed this strange question to elicit a response.

What things find peaceful has no baseness, so the mugwort and reeds are the wondrous abode of those three lords. When the doubled brightness ascends to heaven, the six directions are all illuminated, and there is no mugwort or reed that is not bathed in light.

The sun and moon, though impartial in their illumination, still leave some places unreached, but virtue suffers no such lack. Now, to force the aspirations of the mugwort and reeds and compel them to follow one’s own will—how could this expand the supreme Dao? Thus, the mind remains unsettled and the spirit unresolved.

If all things flourish according to their nature, each content in what it finds contentment, whether distant or near, hidden or deep, left to itself in natural ease—all would attain their utmost. Then, nothing would be amiss for them, and nothing would fail to bring me joy.

Nie Que asked Wang Ni: “Do you know what all things agree upon as right?”
Wang Ni replied: “How would I know?”
What they agree upon is not necessarily right, and what they differ on is not necessarily wrong. Thus, neither I nor others can correct one another, and so there is no use for such knowledge.

“Do you know what you do not know?”
“How would I know?”
If one knew what one did not know, that would itself be a kind of knowing. And if one knew, one could not entrust things to the self-sufficiency of the myriad talents.

“Then are things without knowledge?”
“How would I know? Though let me try to speak of it: How do I know that what I call knowing is not ignorance? How do I know that what I call ignorance is not knowing?

Moreover, let me ask you this: When people sleep in dampness, their waists ail and they become half-paralyzed—is that the case for loaches? When they dwell in trees, they tremble with fear—is that the case for monkeys? Of these three, which knows the proper place to dwell?

People eat domesticated animals, deer eat grass, centipedes relish snakes, owls and crows crave mice—of these four, which knows the proper taste? Apes and monkeys mate with their own kind, deer couple with elk, loaches swim with fish. Mao Qiang and Xi Shi are considered beauties by humans, yet fish plunge deep at the sight of them, birds take flight, and deer bolt away—of these four, which knows the true standard of beauty in the world?

From my perspective, the beginnings of benevolence and righteousness, the paths of right and wrong, are tangled and confused—how could I discern their distinctions?”

They remain unaware, and thus all things lie open without constraint. Precisely because they do not know, I dare not speak with certainty—only tentatively.

Fish swim in water, a capacity shared by all aquatic creatures, and all call this "knowing." Yet from a bird’s perspective, what was just called knowing becomes ignorance. The dung beetle’s knowledge lies in rolling its ball, while those who mock it prize storax instead. Thus, what is commonly called knowing cannot be taken as an absolute standard.

What is called "not knowing" is simply a matter of difference—it is still a kind of knowing, unique to its own domain. Since I cannot claim certainty, I tentatively ask you this.

Here, I briefly cite three examples to illustrate the differing aptitudes of the myriad things. Here, I briefly cite four examples to show that beauty and ugliness have no fixed standard. Here, I briefly cite four examples to show that the preferences of the world are not the same.

To condemn what differs is to have no way of knowing whether what is shared is necessarily correct. What benefits one may harm another, and since the "one" and "another" in the world are endless, the boundaries of right and wrong have no constancy. Therefore, only by refraining from debate and allowing each to follow its own nature can all things attain their full freedom.

Nie Que said, "If you do not know benefit and harm, does the Perfected Person likewise not know benefit and harm?"

Still unable to fathom the subtlety of not-knowing, he thus suspected that the Perfected Person ought to know.

This knot remains unresolved.

Wang Ni said, "The Perfected Person is divine! Though great marshes burn, they cannot make him hot; though the Milky Way freezes, they cannot make him cold; though swift thunder splits mountains, it cannot harm him; though whirlwinds shake the seas, they cannot startle him. One such as this rides the clouds and qi, mounts the sun and moon, and roams beyond the four seas. Death and life bring no change to him—how much less the mere tips of benefit and harm!"

Without intent, yet nothing is not accommodated. The one whose spirit is whole and complete, whose body merges with things, though traversing the most extreme changes, has never ceased to be himself. Thus, nothing lingers or clings in his breast. Borrowing things to act, it is not he who moves. Identifying with change as his very body, death and life are as one. Day and night pass without death or life—how much less can benefit and harm disturb his mind?

Ju Quezi asked Chang Wuzi, "I have heard from the Master that the sage does not engage in affairs, does not pursue profit, does not avoid harm, does not delight in seeking, does not follow the Dao. Where there is no speech, he speaks; where there is speech, he does not speak. And he roams beyond the dust and grime. The Master considered these wild and reckless words, but I believe they are the practice of the wondrous Dao. How close do you think I am to understanding?"

Chang Wuzi said, "Even the Yellow Emperor would be dazzled by such words—how could Confucius know them! Moreover, you are far too hasty in your calculations. Seeing an egg, you demand a crowing rooster; seeing a slingshot, you seek a roasted owl. I have spoken to you recklessly—why not listen to them recklessly as well?

"Stand beside the sun and moon, embrace the cosmos, and let your lips meet in harmony. Why set store by the murky and turbid, or vie for honor? The multitude toil and strive, while the sage appears foolish and dull, blending the myriad ages into a single purity. All things are thus, and with this they are mutually enfolded. Affairs come of themselves, and principle responds of itself—it is not a matter of following and engaging with them. Proceeding straightforwardly without seeking to avoid or approach, not rejoicing in attainment nor angered by direct seizure—this is the solitary perfection.

"All that bears a name is not what I speak of. Each calls itself by its own name, so there is no 'that' with speech and no 'this' without speech. Whatever is not true nature is but dust and grime. Things have their spontaneity, principles have their ultimate. Following them directly, one merges obscurely of itself—no words are needed. Thus, to speak is reckless, and to hear is dazzled. Even the Yellow Emperor could not prevent all things from harboring attachments and being dazzled to the end. Therefore, the sage entrusts things to the realm beyond dust and grime, mysteriously uniting with what lies beyond sight and hearing. Illumined by Heaven, he does not oppose with calculation; released to spontaneity, he does not force clarity.

"Now, Ju Quezi, upon hearing reckless words, you immediately take them as the practice of the wondrous Dao. This is no different from seeing an egg and demanding the service of a rooster at dawn, or seeing a slingshot and seeking the reality of a roasted owl. Those who cannot rest in their time and follow the natural order, who probe change and seek transformation, who contemplate death while alive, who cling to 'this' to debate 'that'—all are followers of opposing calculation. To speak of it is reckless, so I have tried speaking recklessly. If you listen to reckless words as though they were correct, you are again too hasty in your calculations. So why not listen recklessly?

"Taking death and life as day and night—this is the analogy of standing beside the sun and moon. Taking the myriad things as one body—this is the metaphor of embracing the cosmos. Because there is that which is deemed base, honor and lowliness arise, and murkiness and confusion proliferate, none able to set them right. Each asserts its own rightness in its own corner. Thus, for the Dao of effortless harmony, nothing is better than setting it aside without words and entrusting it to spontaneity. Flowing smoothly, without waves or bounds—this is what it means.

"To gallop through the realm of 'this' and 'that' is to be impure. Purity is to be unadulterated. To survey the myriad ages and blend their changes—this the multitude calls 'mixed.' Thus, they toil in body and startle in mind, shunning 'that' and clinging to 'this.' Only the great sage, free of attachments, proceeds straightforwardly and becomes one with change, ever roaming in solitude. Though blending countless eons, with a thousand differences and ten thousand distinctions, when the Dao acts and completes them, past and present are one. When things name them, the myriad things are as one. Nothing is not so, no time is not complete—this may be called purity.

"Nothing is not so. Enfolded, it accumulates. Accumulating 'this' over the myriad ages, the myriad ages are one 'this.' Accumulating 'so' over the myriad things, the myriad things are all 'so.' Thus, one does not know where death and life, before and after, reside, nor where victory and defeat between self and other lie.

"How do I know that delighting in life is not delusion? Death and life are one, yet one delights only in life, desiring to oppose change—how can one know this is not delusion? How do I know that hating death is not like one who, having lost his home in youth, is weak and scattered, never knowing to return? How do I know that life is not that weakness and scattering, and death not a return home—why then hate it?

"Li Ji was the daughter of the border warden of Ai. When the state of Jin first obtained her, she wept until her robe was soaked. Yet once she arrived at the king’s palace, shared his bed, and ate fine meats, she regretted her tears. How do I know that the dead do not regret their former seeking of life?

"Within a single lifetime, emotions change like this. On that day, one does not know the other—how much less can one know the change of death and life! Seeking is desiring. One who dreams of drinking wine may weep at dawn; one who dreams of weeping may go hunting at dawn. While dreaming, one does not know it is a dream. Within the dream, one may even divine the dream—only upon waking does one know it was a dream."

Moreover, only after the great awakening does one know that all this was but a great dream. Yet fools believe themselves already awake, priding themselves on their keen discernment: "This is a lord! That is a herdsman!" How rigid! Even you and I, Confucius, are but dreams—when I say you are dreaming, I too am dreaming. Such words are called "perverse and strange."
