Should a great sage arise once in ten thousand generations to comprehend their meaning, it would be as if encountering him at dawn or dusk.

This is the transformation of waking and dreaming. If circumstances change, emotions differ; thus, the wishes of life and death cannot be the same. Therefore, those who delight in life when alive will delight in death when dead. Though life and death differ, each attains its wish equally—what bondage remains? From this perspective, at the moment of death, one does not know it as death but simply follows one’s inclination. The dreamer who dreams and then interprets the dream within the dream differs not at all from the waking man. In every encounter, nothing is lacking—why then fret over life while fearing death?

The great awakened are sages. They alone know that those who harbor anxieties and concerns remain unawakened. Fools, lost in their great dream, believe themselves awake, smugly distinguishing lords from herdsmen, joyfully clinging to the biases of their clan—how narrow-minded! Unable to forget words and attain spiritual understanding, they are not truly awakened. Extraordinary discourse is beyond the grasp of ordinary minds, hence called "perverse and surpassingly strange," for they fail to recognize its profound liberation.

Those who can shed all attachments and harmonize life and death are exceedingly rare. Even if you and I were to debate, and you prevailed over me, or I over you—would you truly be right? Would I truly be wrong? If I prevailed over you, and you did not prevail over me, would I truly be right? Would you truly be wrong? Is one of us right? Is one of us wrong? Are we both right? Are we both wrong? You and I cannot know. People are inherently shrouded in darkness—whom could I ask to judge? If I asked one who agrees with you to judge, since they agree with you, how could they judge rightly? If I asked one who agrees with me to judge, since they agree with me, how could they judge rightly? If I asked one who differs from both you and me to judge, since they differ from us, how could they judge rightly? If I asked one who agrees with both you and me to judge, since they agree with us, how could they judge rightly? Thus, you, I, and others cannot know one another—must we await another?

You, *er*, and *ru* all mean "you." Without knowing, one speculates; without seeing, one debates. Debating brings no self-assurance, for it opposes things. Debate all day, yet darkness prevails—ultimately, none can be set right. Thus, one must leave it to self-correction. Agreement does not suffice as proof; difference does not suffice as grounds for rejection. If your view were truly right, none in the world could oppose it; if your wrong were truly wrong, none could affirm it. Now, we affirm what we agree on and reject what we differ on—yet with agreement and difference both present, right and wrong have no foundation.

Thus, right and wrong arise from contentiousness and rest in the balance of Heaven. Entrust them to the two courses and let self-correction prevail. Each corrects itself. Relying on another cannot set this right, so none in the world can set one another right—thus, one must leave it to self-correction to reach its end.

The "Heavenly Boundary" is the natural division. Right and wrong, affirmation and negation, self and other oppose one another—hence, no debate. Without debate, harmonize with the Heavenly Boundary, abide in one’s natural division, and do not rely on another to set it right. The mutual dependence of changing voices is like their independence. Harmonize with the Heavenly Boundary, follow the boundless transformation, and thus pass the years.

"What does it mean to harmonize with the Heavenly Boundary?" It means: "Is it so? Is it not so? So or not so." If "is" were truly "is," then its difference from "is not" would require no debate; if "so" were truly "so," then its difference from "not so" would require no debate. Forget years, forget righteousness, rouse yourself in the boundless, and lodge in the limitless. The debate over right and wrong is but a changing voice. The mutual dependence of changing voices cannot set one another right—thus, they are as if independent. Harmonize with the natural division, follow the infinite transformation, and as you proceed, the realm of right and wrong will vanish of itself, and the essence of life and destiny will be fully realized.

Forgetting years unites life and death; forgetting righteousness pervades right and wrong. When right and wrong, life and death dissolve into one, this is the ultimate principle. The ultimate principle flows into the infinite—thus, those who lodge in it cannot be exhausted.

Shadowy Doubt asked Shadow: "Earlier you walked, now you stand; earlier you sat, now you rise. Why so lacking in constancy?" Shadow replied: "Do I depend on something to be thus? Does what I depend on also depend on something to be thus? Do I depend on snake scales or cicada wings? How would I know why it is so? How would I know why it is not so?"

Shadowy Doubt is the faint yin beyond the shadow. It speaks of the celestial mechanism moving of itself—sitting and rising without dependence. That which is without dependence and attains singularity—who could know its cause and demand its reason? If one demands its dependence and traces its origin, the tracing and demanding would be endless, ultimately reaching the state of no-dependence, and the principle of spontaneous transformation would become clear. If one were to depend on snake scales or cicada wings, the lack of constancy would not be difficult to understand. The reason it is not understood now is precisely because it does not depend on such things but transforms spontaneously.

Some in the world say that Shadowy Doubt depends on the shadow, the shadow depends on form, and form depends on the Creator. Let us ask: Does the Creator exist or not? If it does not exist, how could it create things? If it does exist, it would be insufficient to account for the multitude of forms. Only by recognizing that the multitude of forms arise of themselves can one begin to speak of creation. Thus, within the realm of existing things, even Shadowy Doubt does not fail to transform spontaneously in the profound obscurity. Therefore, the Creator has no master, and each thing creates itself. Each thing creates itself without dependence—this is the norm of heaven and earth.

Thus, self and other are mutually arising, form and shadow emerge together; though profoundly united, they do not depend on one another. Understanding this principle will lead all things to return to their source within themselves without relying on externals. Externally, nothing is to be rejected; internally, nothing is to be prized. Thus, all things emerge in serene spontaneity, unaware of why they are born; all attain harmony, unaware of how they attain it. Now, Shadowy Doubt’s reliance on the shadow is like saying they arise together without dependence. Though myriad things gather and together complete heaven, each distinctly manifests its singularity. Therefore, Shadowy Doubt is not controlled by the shadow, nor is the shadow manipulated by form, nor is form transformed by nothingness. Thus, transformation or non-transformation, being so or not being so, following others or following oneself—all arise of themselves. How could I know their cause?

Thus, to follow without aiding is to allow root and branch, inner and outer, to flow harmoniously together, leaving no trace. If one clings to proximate causes and forgets their spontaneous arising, externalizes the source of things, and loses the master within, then attachment and esteem arise. Though one may seek to equalize all things, what is esteemed already resides in the heart—how could there be any leveling?

Once, Zhuang Zhou dreamed he was a butterfly, fluttering and joyous—a butterfly content with itself, unaware of being Zhou. Suddenly he awoke, and there he was, solidly Zhou. Did Zhou dream he was a butterfly, or did the butterfly dream it was Zhou? Between Zhou and the butterfly, there must be a distinction. This is called the transformation of things. Delighting in one’s own joy, acting with ease and pleasure—while dreaming of being a butterfly and unaware of Zhou, it was no different from a distinct death. Yet wherever one is, there is no lack of contentment. Thus, those who cling to life while alive will surely cling to death when dying. From this perspective, those who grieve death while alive are mistaken.

Speaking from Zhou’s perspective, he called it waking—yet it may not have been a dream. Now, not knowing the butterfly is no different from the dream’s not knowing Zhou. Each suits its momentary contentment, so there is no way to prove that the butterfly does not dream of being Zhou. There are those who doze and dream of a hundred years—how can we prove that this hundred years is not a dream in a doze? Time does not pause even briefly, and the present does not endure. Thus, yesterday’s dream has already transformed today. The change between life and death—how is it different from this? Why trouble the heart over it?

To be absorbed in one is to be unaware of the other—this is like dreaming of being a butterfly. To take it from a human perspective, in a single lifetime, the present does not know the future—this is like Lady Li. Yet fools secretly believe they know that life is joyful and death is bitter, never having heard of the transformation of things.

*From the "Inner Chapters" of the Zhuangzi, "Nourishing Life" (Chapter Three):*
"My life has limits, but knowledge is without limit. To pursue the limitless with the limited is perilous! And to do so while claiming to be knowledgeable is perilous indeed! In doing good, avoid seeking fame; in doing wrong, avoid punishment. Follow the central path as your guiding principle—this can preserve the body, fulfill life, nourish kin, and complete one’s years.

Life exists to be nourished and preserved, so nourishing life is the ultimate principle. Yet if nourishment exceeds its limit and harms life, it is no longer the true nourishment of life. The allotment one receives has its own limit. Lifting heavy weights and carrying light ones while the spirit and qi remain unperturbed—this is the limit of one’s strength. Yet those who crave fame and competition, even if they break their backs, still cannot satisfy their desires—this is the limitlessness of knowledge. Thus, the pursuit of knowledge arises from imbalance and vanishes into profound obscurity. Profound obscurity means following one’s utmost allotment without the slightest excess.

Therefore, though bearing ten thousand jun, if it accords with one’s capacity, one suddenly forgets the weight on one’s body. Though responding to ten thousand affairs, one remains oblivious to their presence. This is the true nourishment of life. To seek limitless knowledge with a limited nature—how could one not be exhausted? Already exhausted by knowledge yet not knowing to stop, and then using knowledge to remedy it—this is nourishing yet harming, truly perilous indeed.

Forgetting good and evil and abiding in the center, allowing all things to act of themselves, merging obscurely with what is most fitting—thus, punishments and names stay far away, and the whole principle resides within. Following the center as one’s constant—nourishing kin with appropriateness. If one attains the center and moves in profound obscurity, then nothing is impossible. Nourishing life does not seek to exceed one’s allotment; it is merely to fulfill the principle and complete one’s years.

When Cook Ding carved an ox for Lord Wenhui, his hands touched, his shoulders leaned, his feet stepped, his knees pressed. With a *hua* sound, the blade sang *huo*—all in perfect rhythm, matching the dance of Mulberry Grove, attaining the harmony of Jing Shou’s music."

Lord Wenhui exclaimed, "Ah, excellent! How has your skill reached such heights?" He spoke of how Cook Ding seized every advantage to apply his craft, leaving no joint unloosed, fully exhausting the principle—both perfectly suiting the ox’s structure and harmonizing with the rhythm. Cook Ding set down his blade and replied, "What your servant delights in is the Dao, which advances beyond mere skill. When I first began carving oxen, all I saw was the whole ox. After three years, I no longer saw the whole ox. Now, I encounter it with spirit rather than sight; my senses halt while my spirit moves. I rely on the natural patterns, cleaving great gaps, guiding into great cavities, following what is inherently so. Even the finest knots of sinew and bone have never once impeded me—how much less the great bones! A good cook changes his blade every year, for he cuts; an ordinary cook changes his blade every month, for he hacks. My blade has now endured nineteen years, carving several thousand oxen, yet its edge is as if newly whetted. For where the joints have spaces, and the blade has no thickness—entering with what has no thickness into what has space, how vast is the room for the blade to move! Thus, after nineteen years, my blade’s edge remains as if newly whetted. Yet whenever I come to a complex cluster, I see the difficulty, grow wary with caution, focus my gaze, slow my action, and move the blade with utmost subtlety. Then, with a *huo* sound, the ox falls apart, unaware of its death, like earth crumbling to the ground. I stand holding the blade, look around in all four directions, linger in satisfaction, then wipe the blade clean and put it away." Here, the principle is lodged within the skill—yet what he delights in is not the skill itself.

Though he has yet to perceive the spaces within the principle, he merely perceives the spaces within the principle. The office of scrutiny is abandoned, and the mind is left to follow the principle’s course without obstruction. He does not cut across it forcibly. Where there are boundaries, he strikes along them to part the joints; where nodes and hollows lie, he guides the blade to separate them. The knife is never applied recklessly. The marvel of his skill lies in its constant movement through emptiness, never once encountering even the slightest resistance.

The *gū* is the great bone that turns back on itself, dulling the blade—because it does not meet the spaces within the principle. To strike bone is to break the knife. The *xing* is the whetstone. Where sinews and bones intertwine and cluster, he no longer fixes his gaze on other things. His hand moves slowly, for when he finds the proper point, little force is needed. The principle is divided without trace of the blade, as if earth were merely heaped together.

"Leisurely steps, ample ease, and self-contentment"—this is what it means. He wipes the blade clean and sheathes it.

Prince Wenhui said, "Excellent! From the words of Pao Ding, I have learned how to nourish life." Since the knife may be nourished, he knew that life, too, may be nourished.

Gongwen Xuan, seeing the Master of the Right, was startled and said, "Who is this man? Why is he one-legged? Was this given by Heaven?"

"Was it man who did this to him?"
"It was Heaven, not man. Heaven’s creation made him solitary; man’s form has companionship. From this we know it was Heaven, not man."

*Jie* is the name for partial amputation. What knowledge cannot remedy is Heaven’s doing; to violate what one knows is man’s doing. Partial amputation is called solitude. If a master’s knowledge, confined to one school, cannot preserve both feet, then this is what knowledge cannot remedy. Were the Master of the Right to insist on preserving both with his knowledge, his spirit would be inwardly distressed and his body outwardly ruined—how could it be merely partial amputation? To walk with both feet is to have companionship. One who has companionship in form never doubts it is fate. Since having companionship is fate, we know that solitude is likewise not of one’s own doing.

Thus, one who grasps the essence of life does not strive for what life cannot achieve; one who grasps the essence of fate does not strive for what fate cannot remedy. They merely preserve their natural state.

A marsh pheasant takes ten steps for a single peck, a hundred steps for a single drink, yet it does not seek to be kept in a cage. Though its spirit flourishes, it is not content. *Qi* means to seek; *fan* is what cages the pheasant. To move freely between heaven and earth, to wander in the realm of self-contentment—this is the subtlety of nourishing life. Why seek to enter a cage and be tamed?

What begins in comfort and never ceases to be comfortable forgets comfort. The pheasant’s spirit flourishes, its will and qi overflow with ease, and it roams the clear and open land, unaware that it is doing well.

When Lao Dan died, Qin Shi went to mourn him. He cried out three times and left. A disciple said, "Was he not your friend?"
"Yes."
"Then is it proper to mourn him like this?"
"At first I thought he was a man like others, but now I see he was not."

When others mourn, I mourn; when others wail, I wail. They found it strange that I did not linger at the door to observe the transformations, yet I cried out three times. "Yes. At first I thought he was a man, but now I know he was not."

Earlier, when I entered to offer condolences, I saw elders weeping for him as if for their own children, and the young weeping as if for their own mothers. Those who gathered there must have been moved to speak when they had no wish to speak, to weep when they had no wish to weep. This is fleeing from Heaven and betraying one’s nature, forgetting what one has received—what the ancients called "the punishment of fleeing from Heaven." When he came, Master, it was timely; when he departed, it was in accord. To rest content in timeliness and dwell in accord is to be beyond the reach of sorrow and joy—what the ancients called "the Great Release."

The perfected person has no fixed emotions, merely joining the crowd in lamentation, and thus this was fitting. The nature one receives from Heaven has its own proper measure; it cannot be escaped, nor can it be augmented. To flee from Heaven’s principle and multiply worldly attachments, to let sorrow and joy pass through one’s heart, is to bind the spirit in suffering, no different from torture or punishment. The enlightened of old held such views.

Life arises in its time; principle dictates death. Sorrow and joy arise from loss and gain. But the one who has attained profound communion with change is at peace in every moment, at home in every circumstance, darkly one with the workings of creation. Then nothing is not oneself—what gain or loss, what life or death could there be? Thus, following what one has received, sorrow and joy find no place to settle.

To take what is bound as "suspended" is to see that what is unbound is "released from suspension." Released from suspension, the true nature of life and destiny is attained. This is the essence of nourishing life.

A finger may be exhausted in feeding the flame, but the fire is passed on, never knowing its end. "Exhausted" means used up; "feeding the flame" is like the earlier fuel. The earlier fuel is consumed by the finger, and when the principle of the earlier fuel is exhausted, the fire is transmitted and does not die. The heart receives nourishment within, so life continues without end. This makes clear that nourishing life is the very means by which life lives.

Time does not come again; the present does not pause even once. Thus human life is but a single breath, a single moment of attainment. The breath just past is not the breath now; thus nourishment is received and life continues. The earlier flame is not the later flame; thus the fuel is consumed and the fire is passed on. The fire is passed on and life continues—this comes from nourishing to its utmost. How could the world know that what is exhausted is reborn?

From *Zhuangzi*, "Inner Chapters," "The Human World" (Chapter Four):
Yan Hui sought an audience with Confucius to request leave to depart.

Confucius said, "Where are you going?"
Yan Hui replied, "I am going to the state of Wei."
Confucius asked, "What will you do there?"
Yan Hui said, "I have heard that the lord of Wei is in his prime, yet his conduct is willful. He treats his state lightly and does not recognize his faults. He treats the lives of his people as of little worth, and the dead are measured by the state as if they were marshland—like reeds, the people have nowhere to turn. I once heard you say, ‘Leave a well-ordered state; go to a disordered one. The gate of a physician sees many illnesses.’ I wish to apply what I have heard and reflect on what I might do, hoping that the state may yet be cured."

One who mingles with others cannot depart from them. Yet the vicissitudes of the human world differ in every age, and only those who are without fixed intent and do not rely on their own judgment can adapt to change without bearing its burdens. They do not share the desires of the people.

Now, one who rules others acts only by riding upon them. In anger, corpses lie strewn and blood flows; in pleasure, carriages and caps fill the roads. Thus the ruler’s use of the state cannot be taken lightly. None dare remonstrate with him. He treats life as if it were of no account, leading the entire state into the realm of death, beyond numbering, regarding them as mere grass or mustard seeds. They have nowhere to turn.

Confucius said, "Alas! You are likely to go and be punished! The Dao does not tolerate杂乱. When it is杂乱, it becomes多; when it is多, it becomes扰; when it is扰, it brings忧, and忧 cannot be saved. The utmost person of old first established it within themselves before establishing it in others. If what is established within oneself is not yet settled, how can one have leisure to attend to the conduct of a violent man? The Dao is insufficient to save their troubles.

It is fitting to find the right person. If one does not find the right person, then even if a hundred physicians guard the illness, it will only deepen doubt and fail to bring even a single cure. If one does not respond to things with an empty mind but instead exhausts their thoughts in reckless action, it shows that what is established within themselves is not yet settled.

Only by setting aside knowledge to nurture the true, entrusting the wondrous to the talents of the multitude, attributing merit and fame to others while keeping worries and concerns far from oneself—only then can one attend to the conduct of a violent man.

Moreover, do you also know whence virtue’s dissipation and knowledge’s emergence arise? Virtue dissipates for the sake of fame; knowledge emerges from contention. Fame is that by which people vie; knowledge is the instrument of contention. Both are inauspicious tools, not the means to fulfill one’s conduct.

The reason virtue flows away is pride in fame; the reason knowledge spreads recklessly is contention over goodness. Even Jie and Zhi, in what they prized, sought nothing but fame and goodness. Fame and wisdom are what the world employs, yet when fame arises, contention follows; when wisdom is used, strife emerges. Thus only by abandoning fame and knowledge can one’s conduct be fulfilled.

Moreover, thick virtue and steadfast trust do not yet reach others’ *qi*; a reputation for non-contention does not yet reach others’ hearts. To forcibly display the language of benevolence and righteousness, compass and square, before a violent man is to make others resent what is beautiful in oneself—this is called ‘harming others.’ One who harms others will surely be harmed in return. You are likely to be harmed by others.

If one merely delights in the worthy and despises the unworthy, what need is there to seek distinction? If you do not issue commands, the lords and dukes will surely ride upon others to vie in cleverness. Your eyes will be dazzled, your complexion will become placid, your mouth will scheme, your demeanor will take shape, and your heart will be formed. This is like using fire to save fire, water to save water—it is called ‘increasing abundance.’ Following the beginning without end, if you go with insincere grand words, you will surely die before the violent man!

When one offers a luminous pearl at night, few do not grasp their sword—because they do not understand. Now, your virtue, trust, and reputation for non-contention are things he does not yet understand. To forcibly apply the standards of benevolence and righteousness to him, he will think you wish to destroy others to establish yourself.

Thus the utmost person does not exhaust their will in ordering the world but responds to things with an empty mind. Sincerity and trust are manifest in heaven and earth; non-contention flows freely among the myriad things. Only then do the myriad things return to embrace them, heaven and earth do not oppose them. Thus their virtuous reputation spreads, and the world responds in harmony; their illustrious conduct shines, and the six directions all answer. Only then can one traverse cold and heat, cross order and chaos, without clashing with the scales of the dragon.

If one’s approach is not trusted, they will think you contend for fame and turn to harm you. If one can delight in the worthy and despise the foolish, hear of righteousness and submit, then they are a enlightened ruler. If they are an enlightened ruler, then they do not苦于 lack worthy ministers—your going would not be extraordinary. If they are not so, going will surely bring harm.

Thus, to go with intent is to go without success; to respond without intent is to have response come of itself—then there is no going that is not possible. You need only be silent and speechless. If you speak, the lords and dukes will surely ride upon others with the power of rulership, using their clever eloquence to reject remonstrance and adorn their faults. Their words will be clever and swift, dazzling the eyes. You will not be able to distinguish yourself from them, nor will you have leisure to save yourself—you will only cast yourself aside to follow them. If you cannot save them, you will only strengthen their authority.

The common and constant cling to the old and are unwilling to change. If one is not trusted and remonstrates, even grand words will bring harm.

Moreover, in ancient times, Jie killed Guan Longfeng, and Zhou killed Prince Bigan. Both were those who cultivated themselves and humbly served the people, yet they offended their superiors—thus their lords used their cultivation to cast them out. They were lovers of fame.

In ancient times, Yao attacked Cong, Zhi, Xu, and Ao; Yu attacked Youhu. Their states became desolate and ruined, their persons subjected to punishment and execution. Their use of arms did not cease; their pursuit of fame and substance knew no end. These were all seekers of fame and substance—have you alone not heard of this? Fame and substance are things even the sages cannot overcome—how much less can you!"

"Even so, if you must have some way, try telling it to me."

Guan Longfeng and Prince Bigan, though subordinates, took on the burdens of their superiors—tasks not rightfully theirs. They did not wish to let ministers gain the reputation of surpassing their lords. Tyrannical rulers not only seek to indulge their desires but also pursue fame; it is only that what they seek is not the Dao.

Rulers who covet fame and desire, even if they were Yao or Yu, cannot be transformed. Thus, the multitude attacks them—yet you would go empty-handed to transform them with the Dao?

Yan Hui said, "If I maintain integrity while emptying my heart, and strive for unity, would that suffice?"

"To rectify one’s form while emptying one’s heart—this is to speak with deference and without duplicity." He replied, "It will not suffice."

Yan Hui asked, "Why not? If one’s yang qi is full to overflowing, with shifting colors and an unsteady demeanor—common people dare not oppose it. One seizes upon others’ emotions to seek harmony with their hearts. This is called a virtue that gradually fails to take shape—how much less can great virtue be achieved! Such a one clings rigidly without transformation, outwardly compliant yet inwardly unyielding—how could this ever work?"

This describes the Lord of Wei: his overbearing yang nature swells within and flaunts itself without, reaching the height of arrogance. His joy and anger are unpredictable; none dare defy him. Such extreme obstinacy leads him to respond to others’ flattery with indulgence, then crush them to assert his dominance, seeking ease and self-indulgence to satisfy his extravagant desires. His words grow ever more excessive, with no restraint or humility. Thus, he clings to his original intent. Outwardly compliant yet inwardly unyielding—this is merely the earlier "integrity with an empty heart and striving for unity," which is insufficient to transform him.

"Then let me be inwardly upright and outwardly yielding, achieving through emulation of the ancients. To be inwardly upright is to follow Heaven. One who follows Heaven knows that the Son of Heaven and oneself are both children of Heaven—why then should one seek others’ approval for one’s words, whether they deem them good or not? Such a one is called a childlike follower of Heaven.

To be outwardly yielding is to follow others. Raising hands in respect, kneeling in submission, bowing in deference—these are the rites of a minister. If all others do so, how dare I not? To do what others do is to avoid their censure—this is called following others.

To achieve through emulation of the ancients is to follow the past. Though my words may instruct, their substance is fitting and true, for they belong to the ancients, not to me. Thus, though upright, they bring no harm—this is called following the ancients. Would this suffice?"

Yan Hui further expounded these three principles.

All things, noble or base, share the same gift of life. Thus, whether good or not, one entrusts it to impartial judgment—seeking nothing from others. Following Heaven’s principle, one extends from one’s nature and trusts destiny, proceeding as straightforwardly as an infant. Outwardly yielding, one adapts to what human affairs demand. Achieving in the present while emulating the past, though this be common instruction, it carries a veiled admonition. By lodging uprightness in antiquity, one avoids bringing harm upon oneself.

Confucius said: “Alas! How can this suffice? Too many policies and laws breed discord. Though firmly held, they incur no blame. Yet this is all there is—how can it bring about transformation? It remains self-willed. Though the principle admits no duality, to govern by these three methods is to fail to merge with affairs. Though not grand, one may yet avoid censure. Blame is absent, but transformation is not. Wielding these three arts to suit circumstances is not to act without intention and entrust all to the world.”

Yan Hui said: “I have nothing more to advance. May I ask for your method?”

Confucius said: “Fast, and I will tell you. To act with intention—is this easy? Those who deem it easy do not accord with the vastness of Heaven.”

Yan Hui said: “My family is poor. For months now, I have neither drunk wine nor eaten meat. May this be considered fasting?”

Confucius replied: “This is the fast of sacrificial rites, not the fast of the mind.”

Yan Hui asked: “May I inquire about the fast of the mind?”

Confucius said: “Unify your will. Do not listen with the ears but listen with the mind; do not listen with the mind but listen with qi. The ears are limited to hearing, the mind to recognition. Qi, however, is emptiness that awaits things. Only the Dao gathers in emptiness. Emptiness is the fast of the mind.”

To act with intention in the mind is indeed not easy. Those who deem intentional action easy have not seen its propriety. Abandon heterodoxies and entrust yourself to singularity. Forsake the senses, discard the mind’s intentions, and align with the self-attainment of qi and nature—this is emptiness awaiting things. When the mind is emptied, the supreme Dao gathers within.

Yan Hui said: “Before I was enabled to practice this, I was truly Yan Hui; once enabled, I was no longer Yan Hui—may this be called emptiness?”

Not yet having practiced the fast of the mind, one remains bound to the self.

The Master said: “Perfect! I shall tell you: if you can enter and wander within its enclosure without being moved by its name, then when it enters, you will respond; when it does not enter, you will cease. Without gate, without governance, dwelling in a single abode and lodging in inevitability—this is near indeed. Having attained the service of the mind’s fast, you will be without self. The realm where the unmoored mind attains itself is where one halts at the real. It is like the notes of the pentatonic scale: they respond without intention, and thus are called ‘resonance.’ To respond without intention is to follow the other’s ear, not to force a response. Let things be as they are—this is ‘without gate.’ Entrust the world’s natural ease—this is ‘without governance.’ *Du* means ‘to govern.’ Inevitability is what principle necessarily entails, the abode of supreme oneness meeting the tally of necessity. Principle is exhausted in this.

“To sever traces is easy; to walk without treading the earth is difficult. To be employed by man is easy to feign; to be employed by Heaven is difficult to feign. We have heard of flying with wings, but never of flying without wings; we have heard of knowing with knowledge, but never of knowing without knowledge. Behold that empty chamber: in a vacant room, whiteness arises, and auspiciousness comes to rest. If it does not come to rest, this is called ‘sitting in motion.’ To follow the ears and eyes, turning inward while the mind’s knowledge remains external—then even ghosts and spirits will come to dwell, let alone men! This is the transformation of the myriad things, the pivot of Yu and Shun, the ultimate course of Fuxi and Jiqu, and how much more so for those who are scattered!

“Not acting is easy; to act without treading the earth is impossible. Non-doing is easy; to act without harming one’s nature is unattainable. What sight and hearing obtain is coarse, and thus easily deceived. But the recompense of the natural is subtle, and thus difficult to feign. The less one loses of the real, the less one is incomplete; the more one loses of the real, the more one is incomplete. The recompense of loss and gain never fails to match its measure. To defy Heaven and act falsely—is this not difficult indeed?

“Words must have their means to accomplish their ends. Now, without the abode of utmost emptiness, how can one have the reality of transforming things? To regard being as non-being is the vacant room. In a vacant room, purity and whiteness arise alone. Where auspiciousness gathers is in utmost emptiness and stillness. If one does not halt at what is fitting, does not meet the ultimate, this is to spend the day sitting in response while galloping without cease. Thus, before external enemies arrive, one is already exhausted within—how can one transform things?

“To close the ears and eyes and yet attain naturally is to have turned the mind’s knowledge outward. Therefore, to follow one’s nature and move directly, going nowhere without darkness—how could one be burdened by the obscure and hidden, let alone the entanglements of the human world? Things have no nobility or baseness; none fails to communicate through the mind’s knowledge and the ears and eyes. Thus, what the world calls ‘knowing’—is it knowing by desiring to know? What it calls ‘seeing’—is it seeing by desiring to see? If knowing and seeing could be obtained by desire, then could one desire worthiness and obtain worthiness, or act as a sage and become a sage? Certainly not. Yet the world does not know that knowing knows itself, and so desires to act as knowing to know; it does not see that seeing sees itself, and so desires to act as seeing to see; it does not know that life lives itself, and so seeks to act as life to live. Thus, seeing the eye, one demands the clarity of Li Zhu; hearing the ear, one demands the acuity of Shi Kuang. The spirit gallops within, the ears and eyes are exhausted without, the body is ill at ease, and one is no longer darkly attuned to things. Not being darkly attuned, how can one harmonize with the changes of the human world or respond to the rhythms of generation after generation? This has never been.

Duke Ye of She, about to be sent on a mission to Qi, asked Confucius: ‘The king’s charge to me is most weighty. Qi’s treatment of envoys is likely to be respectful but not urgent. Even a common man cannot be moved, let alone a feudal lord! I am deeply apprehensive. You once told me: “In all matters, great or small, few fail to achieve joy through the Dao. If the matter does not succeed, there will surely be human calamity; if it succeeds, there will surely be the calamity of yin and yang. Only one of power can succeed or fail without disaster.” I eat coarse food without refinement, and my cook has no desire for coolness. Yet this morning I received my charge and by evening was drinking ice—is this not inner heat? I have not yet reached the truth of the matter, yet I already suffer the calamity of yin and yang! If the matter does not succeed, there will surely be human calamity—this is a double burden. As a minister, I am not equal to this task. Pray, tell me what to do!’

The weight of the mission stems from the desire for something. One fears that Qi will merely return empty respect without urgently meeting the request. In matters great or small, few fail to achieve joy through speaking of success. This is what Confucius once told me. Those who speak of success as joy will be angered if it does not come. This is something even the King of Chu cannot avoid. Though the human calamity may pass, joy and fear still war in the breast, and ice has already congealed in the five viscera. Only one of power can entrust success or failure to the other without troubling the mind.

Facing the fire without thinking of coolness shows the frugality of his fare. That his fare is frugal yet he drinks ice with inner heat—this is truly due to anxiety over the difficulty of the matter, not the allure of fine food. Before the matter is settled, one only fears it will not succeed. If it truly does not succeed, then fear will congeal within while the net of punishment and the web of law ensnare without.”

Confucius said: “Under Heaven there are two great precepts: one is fate, the other is duty. Your love for your parents is fate, which cannot be untied from the heart. A minister’s service to his lord is duty—there is nowhere he can go that is not his lord, no place in Heaven and Earth he can escape.”

This is called the great precept. Thus, in serving one’s parents, to be at ease without choosing the place is the utmost of filial piety; in serving one’s lord, to be at ease without choosing the task is the height of loyalty. In serving one’s own heart, neither sorrow nor joy alters what lies before it—knowing the inevitable and accepting it as fate is the perfection of virtue. For a minister nearing the throne, there are indeed things he cannot avoid. Acting with the truth of the matter while forgetting himself—how could he have leisure to delight in life and dread death? The Master’s conduct is thus fitting! Naturally bound and firm, it cannot be undone.

When a thousand gather, if none is taken as master, disorder will lead to dissolution. Thus, many worthy men cannot have many lords, and without worthies there cannot be no lord—this is the Dao of Heaven and man, the inevitable principle. If a lord could be fled and parents untied, there would be no need for precepts. Knowing the inevitable as fate and accepting it, one has neither sorrow nor joy—how could these be altered? Thus, obscurely taking what one encounters as fate and not applying the mind to it, merging utterly with what is most fitting without joy or sorrow within, even in serving ordinary men, one finds no place that is not fitting—how much more so with lord and parents!

Matters have their inevitabilities, principles are fundamentally constant. Thus, entrusting them brings success, and where matters succeed yet the self is not preserved—this has never been. Why then trouble the mind over the self? Principles are unobstructed, so one should simply proceed with what one encounters. If one’s faith in the Dao is not steadfast and likes and dislikes linger in the heart, unable to proceed with what is most fitting while scheming for life and fearing death—I have never seen such a one succeed.

Qiu respectfully repeats what he has heard: In close relations, trust must be mutually confirmed; in distant relations, loyalty must be conveyed through words. Words must sometimes be transmitted. Transmitting words of mutual joy or mutual anger is the most difficult thing under Heaven. For mutual joy invariably brings excessive praise, and mutual anger invariably brings excessive blame. All excess is false, and falsehood cannot be trusted. Where trust is lacking, the transmitter of words brings calamity. Thus, the standard teaching says: *"Transmit the constant truth, do not transmit excessive words, and you will be nearly whole."*

Those nearby can verify, so their trust is confirmed through direct contact. Those afar must convey intent through words. Words of joy and anger, if they exceed reality, should be transmitted so that neither side loses the mean—thus it is not easy. *Excess* means going beyond. Words of joy and anger always exceed what is fitting. Suspecting the words are not his, one may think the transmitter fabricates. Doubt arises. If excessive words are transmitted, it seems like wild falsehood. The receiver doubts, and the transmitter is arbitrarily blamed for exaggeration or understatement. Even if one hears momentary excess, do not transmit it. One must cite the constant truth and grasp the sincere intent—then one is close to wholeness.

Moreover, those who contend with skill and strength begin in openness but often end in secrecy; when taken to excess, they resort to strange tricks. Those who drink with ritual begin in order but often end in chaos; when taken to excess, they indulge in strange pleasures. All things are thus: they begin in sincerity but often end in baseness. Their inception is simple, but their conclusion must be grand.

Words are like wind and waves; actions bring gain or loss. Wind and waves are easily stirred, gain and loss easily endangered. Thus, anger arises without cause, and clever words lead to biased speech. When beasts are cornered and dying, their cries are desperate, their breath disordered—then vicious hearts arise together. If scrutiny is too severe, unworthy intentions will respond, and one does not know why. If one does not know why, who knows where it will end? Thus, the standard teaching says: *"Do not alter commands, do not urge completion. Going beyond is harmful."* Altering commands and urging completion endanger affairs.

True excellence takes time; evil, once done, cannot be undone—can one not be cautious? Originally, they played together in good humor. When the desire to win arises, it secretly harms the other, no longer following reason. There are distinctions between high and low, and toasts follow order. Yet they sink into drunkenness, indulging without restraint, reaching every excess. Trouble arises from simplicity, affairs begin in small things—this is the inevitable course.

Words are like wind and waves, so acting on them brings gain or loss. Thus, if one leaves wind and waves unacted upon, gain is not lost. If affairs attain their truth, danger can be stabilized and turmoil settled. The arising of anger has no other cause—it always comes from clever words exceeding reality or biased speech missing the mark. Like wild beasts driven into a corner, their cries are urgent, their breath disordered, their anger violent, and both develop sores to face each other.

One who is tolerant and accommodates others will surely be sought out. If scrutiny is too precise, a miserly heart arises unnoticed. Thus, the great man lets things drift freely in their own realm, not begrudging others’ abilities, not exhausting others’ joy—thus the bonds of the Four Seas can be preserved. If one does not realize this, how can one know where fortune and misfortune converge?

Transmit the truth of the other. Let it come to fruition on its own. Adding to it is not entrusting the truth. This is how affairs become perilous. Excellence comes from allowing things to transform in their time—like planting, it cannot be rushed. If one urges what the other dislikes to force completion, regret and failure soon follow.

Moreover, riding things to roam the mind, entrusting the unavoidable to nurture the center—this is perfection. What need is there to act in return? It is best to deliver one’s mandate—this is the difficult part. Entrusting one’s intent to things, following the inevitable principles, the harmony of the mean is complete—this is the utmost in engaging with others. One should simply accept the truth of what is owed in return—why contrive intentions in the space between?

Delivering one’s mandate directly is easiest, yet applying joy and anger to the mind makes it difficult.

Yan He was about to serve as tutor to the crown prince of Duke Ling of Wei. He asked Qu Boyu, “There is a man here whose virtue is as harsh as Heaven’s execution. If I deal with him without restraint, I endanger our state; if I deal with him with restraint, I endanger myself. His knowledge suffices only to recognize others’ faults, not to see why they err. What should I do in such a case?”

The nature of petty men is this: if you guide them by rules, they resent you; if you let them run unchecked, they throw the state into chaos. Unaware that the people’s faults arise from themselves, they blame the people and never reform. Qu Boyu said, “Excellent question! Be on guard, be cautious—first rectify yourself! In form, nothing surpasses compliance; in mind, nothing surpasses harmony. Yet these two have their perils. Compliance must not become submission; harmony must not become display. If form complies and submits, you will be overthrown, destroyed, collapsed, or stumbling. If mind harmonizes and displays itself, you will be reduced to fame, reputation, omen, or calamity.

Let him be an infant, and you be an infant with him. Let him be without boundaries, and you be without boundaries with him. Let him be without limits, and you be without limits with him. Guide him to enter flawlessness. Through repeated encounters and meetings, both will thus rectify themselves. In form, do not oppose; in harmony, do not conform. Compliance in form is yielding; submission is merging completely. Harmony is aided by principle; display is self-promotion. If you merge completely, you will stumble and not support him, perishing together. Therefore, you must model yourself on Heaven and Earth, differing only in small matters.

If you display harmony, you will gain a reputation for tolerance, yet aiding his fame will make him resent your superiority and recklessly spawn calamity. Thus, you must remain obscure as dusk, blending like dust in radiance. Then you cannot be claimed as close or distant, benefited or harmed. Do not set small edges against his scales.

Do you not know the mantis? It brandishes its arms to block the chariot wheel, unaware it cannot bear the task—such is the beauty of its talent. Beware, be cautious! To accumulate your strengths and beauties to challenge him is perilous! The mantis’s brandished arm is not without beauty, yet blocking the chariot wheel is simply no match. Now, with knowledge insufficient to handle the task, you seek to forcefully bear it—this is the mantis’s brandished arm. To accumulate your talents, boast your beauties, and challenge this man is the path to danger.

Do you not know those who raise tigers? They dare not feed them live animals, for fear of provoking their killing rage; they dare not feed them whole animals, for fear of provoking their tearing rage. They time their hunger and satiety, understanding their angry hearts. Tigers and humans are of different kinds, yet tigers fawn on those who raise them—this is compliance. Thus, those they kill are those who oppose them. They fear that a killing intent will lead to rage. If you make the tiger bite and divide its food, it will use force and grow angry. Know the cause of its anger and comply with it. Compliance with principle makes even different kinds grow affectionate; opposition to rhythm makes even the closest kin turn to arms.

Those who love horses use baskets to collect their dung and clamshells to collect their urine. Yet if a mosquito or fly alights and they strike it untimely, the horse may break its bit or shatter its chest. Love can reach a point where it causes loss. Can one not be cautious?”

The dung and urine are most base, yet one uses precious vessels to collect them—this is the utmost love for the horse. Servants bustle about the horse, though they remove its troubles, they startle it unawares. Startling it when unprepared thus causes such alarm. The intent to eliminate harm, hastily striking it, leads to destruction—losing the very reason for love. Thus, in dealing with things in the world, one must be cautious at the juncture of compliance and opposition.

When Carpenter Shi traveled to Qi, he reached Quyuan and saw an oak that was a spirit tree. Its size could shade thousands of oxen, its girth measured a hundred spans, its height towered over the mountains, and only after rising eighty feet did branches appear. Those that could be made into boats numbered over ten at its side. Onlookers thronged like a marketplace, yet the master carpenter did not glance at it and continued on his way without pause. His disciple, having feasted his eyes, caught up to Carpenter Shi and said, “Since I took up the axe to follow you, master, I have never seen timber so fine as this. Why does my teacher refuse to look and walk on without stopping?” He replied, “Enough! Say no more! It is but a useless tree.”

"As timber for boats, it would sink; for coffins and outer caskets, it would swiftly rot; for vessels, it would quickly break; for doors and gates, it would ooze sap; for pillars, it would be worm-eaten—this is a tree of no use. Having no use, it has thus attained such longevity." Not counted among the useful, it is called a "scattered tree."

Carpenter Shi returned home, and the spirit oak appeared to him in a dream, saying: "With what will you compare me? Will you compare me to the cultivated trees? Those such as the hawthorn, pear, orange, and pomelo—fruit-bearing kinds—once their fruits ripen, they are stripped, and once stripped, they are shamed. Their great branches are broken, their small branches are torn. This is how their abilities bring suffering to their lives. Thus, they do not fulfill their natural lifespan but die midway, struck down by the common world. All things are like this. Moreover, I have long sought to be of no use! Nearly dead, only now have I attained it—this is my great use. Had I been useful, could I have grown so vast? Furthermore, you and I are both things—why then do you assess me as a thing? And you, a nearly dead scattered man, how could you understand a scattered tree?"

All useful trees are called "cultivated trees." All things harm themselves by their own usefulness. Many have glanced askance at me, but only you, Carpenter Shi, have understood. The accumulation of uselessness becomes the great use for preserving life. If useful, it would long since have been felled.

This was the spirit oak’s jest with Carpenter Shi. Carpenter Shi awoke and interpreted his dream. His disciple said: "If it delights in being useless, then why does it serve as a spirit tree?" He replied: "Hush! Say no more!"

That tree merely lodges there by chance! It takes those who do not understand it as its revilers. Had it not become a spirit tree, it would nearly have been felled! Moreover, what it preserves differs from the multitude, yet to explain it through righteousness—how far removed is that!

It still resents being honored as a spirit tree, rather than simply delighting in its uselessness. The spirit merely comes to lodge there—this tree does not seek to become a spirit. The tree takes being a spirit as a humiliation from those who do not understand it—how could this be an honor!

The tree itself takes its uselessness as its use; thus, even if it were not a spirit tree, it would still ultimately avoid the harm of being felled. It preserves itself by having nothing to preserve, while the multitude preserves itself by having something to preserve. Benefiting others, fostering growth, and restraining wrongdoing—these are the duties of a spirit tree.

The useless one, serene and inactive, allows all talents to be used; those who are "useful" each attain their proper place without interference. This is how the useless preserves life. You praise it as a spirit tree—how could it not draw near to harm!

---

Nanbo Ziqi wandered in the hills of Shang and saw a great tree, one unlike any other: its shade could shelter a thousand chariots, and travelers would take refuge beneath its foliage. Ziqi said: "What tree is this? It must possess extraordinary timber!" Looking up at its slender branches, he saw they were gnarled and unfit for beams or rafters; looking down at its massive roots, he saw they were split and unfit for coffins or outer caskets. Licking its leaves, his mouth blistered and burned; smelling it, he became wildly intoxicated, raving for three days without cease. Yet its branches’ shade could shelter a thousand chariots.

Ziqi said: "This is truly a useless tree—how else could it have grown so vast? Alas, the spirit-like person takes this uselessness as their way."

The king who is "useless" among his officials allows his ministers to manage their affairs: the clear-sighted to see, the keen-eared to listen, the wise to plan, the brave to defend. What does he do? He remains profoundly still. And when all talents do not overstep their proper place, this "uselessness" becomes what all talents ultimately rely upon. Thus, the world delights in exalting him without tiring, and he rides upon the ten thousand things without harm.

---

In Song there was a region called Jing, where catalpa, cypress, and mulberry thrived. Those with trunks the size of an arm-span were felled by those seeking pegs for monkey perches; those three or four spans around were felled by those seeking timber for grand and famous structures; those seven or eight spans around were felled by the households of nobles and wealthy merchants seeking sideboards for their halls. Thus, they did not live out their natural years but were cut down midway by axes—this is the calamity of being useful.

Therefore, when exorcists release a white-foreheaded ox, a snub-nosed pig, or a person with hemorrhoids, they do not allow them to cross the river. The shamans know this—these are considered inauspicious. Yet this is precisely what the spirit-like person regards as the greatest auspice.

Those with talent cannot help but cherish it. Shamans perform exorcisms, casting aside these three, and only then do they carefully select unblemished sacrificial animals before daring to use them. The shamans know that the untalented remain whole. To preserve life is what the world calls auspicious, yet shamans regard the untalented as inauspicious and do not use them. They, by being "inauspicious," preserve life—this is the greatest auspice.

The spirit-like person has no mind of their own and follows things as they are. Thus, what the world calls the greatest auspice, the spirit-like person does not oppose.

---

There was a man named Zhili Shu, whose chin was hidden in his navel, shoulders higher than his head, the crown of his skull pointing to the sky, his five organs above, his thighs beside his ribs. By mending needles and washing clothes, he earned enough to feed himself; by sifting husks and winnowing grain, he could feed ten people. When the authorities conscripted soldiers, Zhili Shu waved his arms and wandered among them; when there were great labor projects, Zhili Shu, citing his chronic illness, was exempt from service; when the authorities distributed grain to the sick, he received three bushels and ten bundles of firewood.

Though his body was deformed, he could still nourish himself and live out his natural years—how much more so one whose virtue is "deformed"! Relying on his uselessness, he did not hide himself away. Exempt from corvée labor, he did not participate; when gifts were given, he accepted them.

The spirit-like person is of no use to things, yet things each attain their own use. They ascribe merit and fame to the multitude, merging with things without a trace. Thus, they avoid the harm of the human realm and dwell in the substance of constant beauty—this is one whose virtue is "deformed."

---

When Confucius traveled to Chu, Jie Yu, the madman of Chu, wandered by his gate and sang:

Phoenix, phoenix,
How your virtue has declined!
The future cannot be awaited,
The past cannot be retrieved.
When the world has the Dao,
The sage achieves;
When the world lacks the Dao,
The sage survives.
In this present age,
One barely escapes punishment.
Fortune is lighter than a feather,
Yet none know how to bear it.
Do not look ahead or behind—
Fully grasp the present moment.
Oblivious, merge with the times,
And only then can you be perfectly attuned, avoid punishment and disgrace.
Leave your feet free to wander,
Your hands free to act,
Let your ears hear what they hear,
Your eyes see what they see.
Know to stop where you do not know,
Do only what you can do.
Use what is naturally useful,
Act as is naturally fitting.
Follow your nature within,
Without the slightest concern for what lies beyond—
This is the utmost ease of non-action.

No one who practices non-action fails to preserve their nature and life; and where nature and life are preserved, misfortune is, in principle, unheard of. Thus, what we call fortune is nothing other than this very preservation—it is not borrowed from external things, nor does it bear the weight of a goose feather. To move in accord with one’s nature, never exceeding its bounds, is the utmost ease in the world; to lift what lifts itself, to bear what bears itself, is the utmost lightness in the world. Yet because people harm their nature with boundless knowledge and disturb their true heart with likes and dislikes, they abandon this utmost ease of non-action to pursue the utmost difficulty of action, and discard this utmost lightness of self-lifting to take on the utmost weight of bearing others. This is the common affliction of the world.

Disaster is heavier than the earth,
Yet none know how to avoid it.
If one acts from within their nature,
Even bearing ten thousand jun feels weightless;
If one relies on external things,
Even a weight no heavier than a zhu or a zhi becomes unbearable.
Acting from within brings fortune,
Thus fortune is supremely light;
Acting from without brings disaster,
Thus disaster is supremely heavy.
That disaster is supremely heavy yet none know how to avoid it—
This is the world’s great delusion.

Enough, enough!
To govern others with virtue is perilous indeed!
Drawing lines on the ground to make others follow—
Perilous, perilous!
Forcing others to follow a path you have traced leaves tracks that cannot be concealed;
Imposing one’s own standards upon things prevents harmony with them.
Thus, the great person does not dazzle others with their own brilliance but allows others to find their own clarity; they do not impose their virtue upon others but entrust others to discover their own virtue. In this way, they pervade and unite all things, merging self and other in profound unity, dissolving all distinctions to become one with the world, so that inner and outer share the same fortune.

Bewildered yang, bewildered yang—
Do not harm my path.
"Bewildered yang" means lost yang. If one remains true to their solitary nature without being swayed by externals, their path remains whole.
If all in the world preserve their own wholeness,
Then everything called "I" is preserved without exception.

Crooked, crooked—
Do not harm my feet.
When one’s conduct is perfectly curved, it is self-sufficient.
Mountain trees bring ruin upon themselves;
Fat is consumed by its own flame.
Cinnamon is edible, so it is felled;
Lacquer is useful, so it is cut.
People all know the usefulness of what is useful,
But none know the usefulness of what is useless.
What is useful serves others’ purposes;
What is useless preserves its own life.
Those who carve their flesh for the world are known to the world.
But those who ensure the people do not lose their self-preservation, so that self and other are both at ease—
Such subtlety is so near at hand that none perceive its wonder within themselves.

From *Zhuangzi, Inner Chapters, "Virtue’s Perfect Correspondence" (Chapter Five)*
In Lu there was a man named Wang Tai who had lost a foot, yet those who followed him were as numerous as those who followed Confucius. His virtue was full within, and things responded to him without; inner and outer merged in profound harmony, so that he trusted his fate as if it were a tally, forgetting his physical form. His disciples were many, rivaling Confucius in number.
Changji asked Confucius, “Wang Tai is a man who has lost a foot, yet those who follow him divide Lu equally with you. He neither teaches when standing nor discusses when sitting. Those who go to him empty return full.”

Had there not been a teaching without words, a shaping of the heart without form? What manner of man is this? Each finds fulfillment in himself. How strange that though his form is maimed, his heart is so full! When the heart is whole, it leaves the body behind, forgets the five viscera, and wanders alone—none under heaven can depart from it.

Confucius said, “This Master is a sage. I am but a latecomer who has yet to follow him! I shall take him as my teacher—how much more should those who are not my equal! Why limit it to Lu? I shall lead all under heaven to follow him.”

When spirit is whole and the heart complete, the body merges with things. One who merges with things cannot be distanced by all under heaven—how could it be merely one state?

Changji said, “He is a cripple, yet he surpasses Master Wang—how far he stands above the common! If this is so, how does he employ his heart?”

Confucius said, “Though life and death are great indeed, he does not change with them. Though heaven and earth collapse, he will not be lost with them. He discerns the unborrowed and does not shift with things; he commands the transformations of things while guarding their root.”

Though people change daily, life and death are the greatest of changes. He moves with change, so life and death do not alter him. This is following it. It clarifies the constancy of nature and fate. He takes transformation as fate and does not oppose it, never departing from the ultimate of rightness.

Changji said, “What does this mean?”

Confucius said, “Viewed in their differences, liver and gall are as distant as Chu and Yue. Viewed in their sameness, the myriad things are all one. One who is thus wanders his heart in the harmony of virtue, unaware of what ears and eyes deem fitting. He sees what is one in things and does not see what is lost—seeing the loss of a foot as discarding dirt.”

When the nature of tranquility and suffering differs, the feelings of beauty and ugliness diverge. Though what is deemed beautiful varies, all share in finding something beautiful. Each finds beauty in what they deem beautiful, so the myriad things share one beauty. Each affirms what they affirm, so all under heaven share one affirmation.

If one distinguishes things by their differences, then all under heaven is different. But one who gazes with vast equanimity governs heaven and earth, contains the myriad things, and knows that differences are not truly different. Thus, following what is shared, all under heaven is the same. Knowing that sameness is not truly possessed, following what is absent, all distinctions of right and wrong, beauty and ugliness dissolve into nothingness.

To affirm oneself and negate others, to find beauty in oneself and ugliness in others—from those of middling wisdom down to crawling insects, none fail to do this. Yet this is merely clarity about oneself and obscurity about others. As for those of profound penetration and merging unity, they illuminate all under heaven through all under heaven. When none under heaven say “I am not,” it reveals that none are not; when none say “that is right,” it reveals that none are right. Without right or wrong, all blends into one. Thus, they ride change and follow transformation, opposing things without fear. Having forgotten all preferences, nothing is not entrusted. To entrust all and not attain—this has never been; to attain all and not harmonize—this too has never been heard.

Thus, the heart roams freely within the bounds of Dao and virtue, drifting without anything amiss, vast and without constraint. Embracing the wondrous heart of ultimate principle, one is the same with all things. Being the same with all things, in life and death, in change, there is nowhere that is not oneself. Thus, life is my time, death is my following; time is my gathering, following is my scattering. Though gathering and scattering differ, I am always myself. Life is merely my moment—there has never been gain; death is also myself—there has never been loss.

Viewing the changes of life and death as one, once their oneness is seen, one sheds all attachments, merges self and other in profound unity, treats life and death as waking and dreaming, the body as an inn. Departing life is like shedding sandals, severing a foot like discarding dirt—I have never seen anything that could entangle such a heart.

Changji said, “He cultivates himself. Through his knowledge, he attains his heart; through his heart, he attains his constant heart. Why should things vie to surpass him?”

King Tai appeared unable to forget knowledge yet preserve himself, unable to relinquish the heart yet attain self-possession. To attain one’s constant heart is to go forth in equanimity. He seemed unable to go forth in equanimity and encounter things, thus always compelling things to come to him.

Confucius said, “No one mirrors themselves in flowing water, but in still water. Only stillness can still the many stillings. Receiving mandate from earth, only the pine and cypress stand upright, ever green through winter and summer. Receiving mandate from heaven, only Yao and Shun stand upright, at the head of all things. Fortunate to rectify life, they rectify the lives of all. The proof of preserving one’s beginning lies in fearlessness—one brave warrior can charge into nine armies. Those who seek fame and can restrain themselves are yet like this; how much more one who governs heaven and earth, stores the myriad things, treats the six senses as a temporary abode, models the ears and eyes, unifies the knowledge of knowledge, and whose heart has never died! He will choose a day to ascend on high, and people will follow him. How would he deign to make things his concern?”

The stillness of water that enables reflection is not stillness sought for reflection. Thus, when King Tai gathered the multitude, the multitude came to him of their own accord—how could he draw things to follow him? If he acted to make it so, he could not abide in the stillness of the myriad things. The pine and cypress uniquely receive the concentrated qi of nature, thus they stand as the finest of trees—not through effort but by attainment. Those who uniquely receive the upright qi of nature are exceedingly rare: below, only the pine and cypress; above, only the sages. Thus, all that are not upright come seeking rectification. If all things possessed perfect verdancy, the pine and cypress would not be prized; if each person were self-upright, none would admire the great sage and hasten to him. Fortunate to be upright oneself, one does not rectify in order to rectify others. Not striving to renounce fame yet leaving nothing unborne, darkly embodying all—this is what is called a temporary abode.

People use their ears and eyes, yet they are not their ears and eyes. When knowledge moves with transformation, there is nowhere it does not darkly pervade—this is the oneness of knowledge. When the heart flows with life and death, there is no time it is not alive—this is the heart that has never died. To choose is to fail to meet; this person makes no choice, following heaven’s course and moving with the times. Thus, those who borrow and lend are surpassed by him. His tranquility preserves his wholeness.

Shentu Jia, a man without feet, studied with Zheng Zichan under Bohun Wuren. Zichan said to Shentu Jia, “If I leave first, you stay; if you leave first, I stay.” The next day, they again sat together in the same hall. Zichan said to Shentu Jia, “If I leave first, you stay; if you leave first, I stay. Now I am about to leave—can you stay? Or not? Moreover, when you see the one in power, you do not yield. Do you consider yourself equal to the one in power?” Ashamed to walk alongside one without feet, he pressed the question, wishing to ensure they would not leave together. Always valuing his own position, he directly said, “You consider yourself equal to the one in power,” implying his lack of deference.

Shentu Jia said, “In the Master’s gate, are there truly those in power like this? You delight in your position and belittle others. I have heard: ‘When a mirror is bright, dust does not settle; if it settles, it is not bright. Long dwelling with the worthy leaves no faults.’ Now, what you take as greatest is the Master, yet you still speak thus—is this not a fault?” This speaks of virtue’s place, not rank. He mocked his pride in position, wishing to precede others. To serve a clear teacher yet retain a stingy heart is truly a fault.

Zichan said, “You are already like this, yet still vie with Yao in goodness. Measuring your virtue, is it not enough to reflect on yourself?” As one with a maimed form, he spoke without self-examination, wishing to slight those in power and stand with the virtuous. Measuring his virtue, it was insufficient to compensate for his maimed form’s transgression.

Shentu Jia said, “Those who describe their faults, claiming they should not have lost what they lost, are many; those who do not describe their faults, claiming they should not have kept what they kept, are few. Knowing there is nothing to be done and accepting it as fate—only those with virtue can do this. Wandering within Yi’s drawn bow: the center is the target, yet missing it is fate. Many have laughed at my incomplete feet, and I bristled with anger. But when I came to the Master’s place, I returned in calm. Did the Master wash me with goodness, or did I awaken myself? I have wandered with you for nineteen years and never known I was without feet. Now you wander with me within the form, yet seek me outside the form—is this not a fault?” He often recounted his faults, claiming many believed he should not have lost what he lost.

Yi was an archer of old renowned for his skill. The range of his bow and arrows was called the "drawn bow." Where profit and harm contend, the world is all Yi. Those who do not abandon themselves, forget knowledge, and flow with things—all wander within Yi’s drawn bow. Even if one ventures forth like Zhang Yi or dwells in seclusion like Shan Bao, they cannot escape the target’s center. Whether one hits or misses it rests solely with fate. Yet the petty-minded each encounter their own lot and do not realize that fate is thus of itself. Those who escape harm from bow and arrow deem themselves clever, rejoicing in their superiority. But when they cannot escape, they resent their error, their wills wounded and spirits shamed—these have not yet grasped the nature of fate.

Our lives are not of our own making. Within a single lifetime, a hundred years, all our sitting and rising, moving and resting, inclinations and aversions, emotions, nature, knowledge, and abilities—all that we possess, all that we lack, all that we do, all that we encounter—are not of ourselves. The principle is thus of itself. To arbitrarily generate joy and sorrow within it is to go against nature and lose the Way. All this stems from not knowing fate and thus indulging in such laughter.

Seeing the perfected one’s knowledge of fate and abandonment of form, he cast aside his former anger and returned to equilibrium. Did the Master wash me with the goodness of the Dao, or was I able to turn back myself? This was to forget the form and leave burdens behind—forgetting the form indeed. The form lies without, while virtue resides within. Now you wander with me in virtue, not in bodily exchange, yet you seek my external adornments—is this not a fault?

Zi Chan, startled, altered his expression and said, “Say no more!” Having awakened, he found the words excessive.

The heel is the cheek. In Lu there was a toe-less man, Shushan Wuzhi, who came to see Confucius on his heels. Confucius said, “You were not careful, and previously suffered such misfortune. Though you come now, what can be done?”

Man’s life arises of itself from principle. Simply do nothing and let it arise naturally—this is to value the body and know one’s duty. If one forgets this natural arising, and instead is cautious and self-important, one lightly uses the body without knowing one’s duty. Thus the five organs attack one another within, and hands and feet are maimed without.

Wuzhi said, “I only failed to know my duty and lightly used my body, which is why I lost my foot. Now I come, and what remains is still worthy of respect—I thus strive to preserve it whole. Heaven covers all without exception, Earth bears all without exception. I took you, Master, to be like Heaven and Earth—how could I have known you would still be like this?”

To cast aside self-importance and caution, and let life arise naturally—this is to strive for wholeness. To reproach one for lack of care is to fall short of Heaven and Earth.

Confucius said, “I am indeed crude! Sir, why not come in? Please share what you have heard.”

Hearing what was to be heard, he departed, preserving his non-action. Wuzhi left.

Confucius said, “Disciples, take heed! Though Wuzhi is maimed, he still strives to study and redress the faults of his past conduct—how much more should one of whole virtue do so!”

One of whole virtue is born and forgets life. Wuzhi said to Lao Dan, “Is Confucius not yet a perfected person? Why does he so assiduously take me as his student? He seeks to be known for marvels, illusions, and strange wonders, unaware that the perfected person regards these as his own fetters.”

He found it strange that Confucius should again study under Lao Dan. One without preconceptions learns when others learn. Yet the ancients studied for themselves, while those of today study for others—this flaw has led them to act as others act.

One who teaches others to attain self-realization follows what is constant; one who abandons the self to imitate others and chases externals seeks an extraordinary reputation. Such extraordinary reputation arises from what is constant.

Thus, the learner does not pursue illusions and wonders, yet illusions and wonders necessarily arise from learning. Ritual is not for ornate display, yet ornate display necessarily arises from ritual. This is an inevitable principle, something the perfected person cannot help but regard as fetters.

Lao Dan said, “Why not simply let him see life and death as a single thread, the permissible and impermissible as one continuum, and thus free him from his fetters? Would that be possible?”

He wished to guide him directly to the obscure truth, hoping he would leave no trace.

Without Toes said, “Heaven has punished him—how can it be undone!”

Now, Confucius is not without obscurity in his understanding. Yet by the natural principle, where conduct goes, the shadow follows; where words go, echoes attend. One who accords with things establishes a name and trace, though according with things is not for the sake of name. Not acting for name—this is perfection. Yet ultimately one cannot escape name. Who, then, can free himself from it?

Thus, name is the shadow and echo; shadow and echo are the fetters of form and sound. When this principle is understood, name and trace may be relinquished. When name and trace are relinquished, attachment to externals may be severed. When attachment to externals is severed, one’s nature and life may be preserved whole.

Duke Ai of Lu asked Confucius, “In Wei there was an ugly man named Aitai Tuo. Men who lived with him thought of him and could not leave. Women who saw him pleaded with their parents, saying, ‘Better to be this man’s concubine than another’s wife’—by the tens, without end. He never initiated speech, only harmonized with others. He held no lordly position to save men from death, no store of wealth to fill men’s bellies. Moreover, his ugliness startled all under heaven. He harmonized without initiating, his wisdom did not extend beyond his immediate sphere, yet men and women alike gathered before him. There must have been something extraordinary in him.

I summoned him to observe him, and indeed, his ugliness startled all under heaven. Yet after less than a month in my company, I took notice of his character. Before a year had passed, I trusted him. The state had no chief minister, so I offered him the governance. He responded with reluctance, as if declining. I was ashamed, yet in the end I entrusted him with the state. Before long, he left me and departed.

I grieved as though bereft, as if no one remained to share the joy of ruling this state. What manner of man was he?”

Ugliness is but outward deformity. Yet men came to him not by power or influence. They came not seeking sustenance. They came not for his comeliness. He summoned none, yet they gathered. His thoughts did not stray beyond their proper bounds.

The man of perfect talent harms nothing in the world. Entering the herd, he does not disturb the beasts; entering the flock, he does not scatter the birds. Thus he becomes a refuge for all things. Before a month had passed, his depth was already perceived. Entrusted with the governance of the state, neither favor nor disgrace could unsettle his spirit. When others declined, so too did he.

Confucius said: “I once traveled to Chu and saw young boars nursing from their dead mother. After a moment, they glanced about in alarm and fled. They saw not their own kind, nor found anything familiar. What they loved in their mother was not her form, but that which animated her form. When warriors die in battle, their burials do not include the ceremonial shields of the living. No one cherishes the shoes of the mutilated. In both cases, the root is lost. The consorts of the Son of Heaven do not cut their nails or pierce their ears. Those who take wives remain apart, no longer employed in service. If mere bodily integrity suffices to command such reverence, how much more the man of perfect virtue!

Now, Aitai Tu spoke not, yet was trusted; did nothing, yet was beloved. He was offered a state, yet feared only that he might refuse. Such a man must be one of perfect talent whose virtue remains unmanifest.”
