The living are drawn to those of like talent and virtue. When these depart in death, the living flee, for their kind is lost. Thus, one rich in inner virtue is like a newborn babe: wherever he goes, none harm him, for he is recognized as kin and thus made clear to all. If the heart is of like kind, even dissimilar forms arouse no hostility. But if the heart is not of like kind, even mother and child, though alike in form, cannot secure their bond.

That which animates form is talent and virtue. The ceremonial shield is the instrument of martial prowess. The fallen warrior has no use for martial prowess—where then would such a shield be placed? We cherish shoes for the sake of the feet. Shields and shoes alike depend on their root. To preserve the body whole is to fear harm to its form.

In selecting consorts or celebrating new marriage, the mind is set on physical beauty. Thus, bodily integrity alone can soften the passions of the most exalted and turn the resolve of the chastest maiden. How much more, then, should the man of perfect virtue be beloved by all things!

Duke Ai said: “What is meant by *perfect talent*?”

Confucius replied: “Life and death, survival and ruin, success and failure, wealth and poverty, worthiness and unworthiness, slander and praise, hunger and thirst, cold and heat—these are the changes of affairs, the workings of fate. They alternate before us day and night, yet knowledge cannot trace their beginnings. Thus, they must not be allowed to disturb the harmony within, nor enter the sacred chamber of the mind. Let them bring only ease and openness, never departing from joy. Let day and night be seamless, so that the heart ever abides in springtime, meeting all things with the vitality of the season. This is what is called *perfect talent*.”

The principle is inherently right—it cannot be escaped. Thus, human life is no mistake; what life possesses is no delusion. Though heaven and earth are vast and the myriad things numerous, what I encounter is precisely this—even the gods of heaven and earth, the sages of the state, exerting utmost strength and wisdom, cannot alter it. Therefore, what one does not encounter cannot be encountered; what one encounters cannot be unencountered. What one does not do cannot be done; what one does cannot be undone. Entrust it to fate, and it naturally fits.

Fate moves, affairs change, ceaseless day and night, pressing forward without retreat, lingering without pause. Thus, the man of perfect talent follows what he encounters and entrusts himself to it. Beginnings cannot be traced by knowledge, nor can endings be clung to by emotion. Therefore, those who know that fate must run its course and affairs must change—how could they scheme for endings or cling to beginnings, fixate on the new or yearn for the old? Even with utmost wisdom, they cannot trace it. The past is gone—what can I do about it? If one knows that nature and fate are inherently right, then though life and death, success and failure, undergo a thousand changes and ten thousand transformations, one remains serene and at ease, harmony and principle dwelling within.

The sacred chamber is the dwelling of the spirit. The perfectly content do not let cares and troubles disturb their spirit, passing over them like skin-deep matters. If one keeps harmony unperturbed and the sacred chamber at ease, then though one traverses the greatest changes, one does not lose one’s joy. Calmly entrusting oneself to it, one becomes the reliance of all living things, transforming together with the four seasons.

“What is meant by *virtue not manifesting*?”
“Stillness is the fullness of water at rest. It can serve as a model: inwardly preserved, outwardly undisturbed. Virtue is the cultivation of perfect harmony. *Virtue not manifesting* means things cannot depart from it.”

Of all things in the world, none is more perfectly level than still water. Utterly without bias, it is thus the standard for the world. When affairs are accomplished and things are harmonized, this is called *virtue*. When no affair is unaccomplished and no thing is disharmonious, this is *virtue not manifesting*. Therefore, the world delights in exalting it without tiring.

Another day, Duke Ai told Minzi: “At first, I thought that by facing south as ruler of the world, holding the people’s guiding threads and worrying over their deaths, I had attained perfect understanding. Now, hearing the words of the perfected man, I fear I lack their substance, have lightly used my person, and lost my state. I and Kong Qiu are not ruler and minister—we are friends in virtue!”

Those who hear the wind of virtue’s fullness, even Duke Ai, wish to cast off their bodily forms and forget nobility and baseness.

Yin Qi, the hunchbacked, lipless man, persuaded Duke Ling of Wei, who was so pleased that he compared whole-bodied men and found their necks too slender. The man with the great goiter persuaded Duke Huan of Qi, who was so pleased that he compared whole-bodied men and found their necks too slender. Thus, virtue has its excellences, and form has its forgettings. People forget what should be forgotten yet forget what should not be forgotten—this is called *true forgetting*.

Partial feelings lead one way, and the ugly become more lovely while the lovely become more ugly. If one’s virtue excels in following things, things forget their ugliness; if it excels in opposing things, things forget their loveliness. In life, they are loved; in death, they are discarded. Thus, virtue is what the world does not forget; form is what principle does not retain. Therefore, those who forget form do not truly forget; those who forget virtue while clinging to form—*they* are the ones who truly forget.

Thus, the sage wanders freely, yet knowledge becomes a snare, covenants become glue, virtue becomes a bond, skill becomes commerce. The sage does not scheme—why use knowledge? Does not carve—why use glue? Has no loss—why use virtue? Does not trade—why use commerce? These four are the heavenly nourishment. *Heavenly nourishment* is the food of heaven. Having received sustenance from heaven, why use man?

Possessing the form of a human but lacking human emotions, one has the form of a human and thus mingles with people; lacking human emotions, right and wrong do not adhere to one’s person. How small and insignificant—this is how one belongs to people! How vast and towering—this is how one completes one’s heaven. Roaming in the realm of self-attainment, released and reaching everywhere—this is the fullness of talent and virtue. These four arise naturally from one another; their principle is already complete. Since it is naturally complete, the sage has no use for his own contrivance. Words arise from nature and are endowed by it. Once endowed by nature, the principle is already sufficient. Thus, though one may ponder deeply to avoid calamity or clearly admonish to evade misfortune, nothing happens by delusion—all are the convergence of heaven and earth, the direction of the highest principle. What must be pondered is not my pondering; what must not be pondered is not my not pondering. Some ponder and avoid it; some ponder and do not avoid it; some do not ponder and avoid it; some do not ponder and do not avoid it. None of these are of me—what is there to do? Entrust it, and it comes of itself.

Their appearance is like that of a human. Like gathers with like, kind separates from kind—this is the Dao of nature. Without emotion, one entrusts oneself to things. Their appearance is like that of a human; without emotion, one is vast and unburdened by anything. Unburdened by anything is what those with emotions cannot attain—thus, without emotion, one alone completes heaven.

Thus, virtue has its excellences, and form has its forgettings. People forget what should be forgotten yet forget what should not be forgotten—this is called *true forgetting*.

Huizi said to Zhuangzi: “Do humans truly have no emotions?”
Zhuangzi said: “Yes.”
Huizi said: “If humans have no emotions, how can they be called human?”
Zhuangzi said: “The Dao gives them appearance, heaven gives them form—how can they not be called human?”

Human life is not born of emotion.

What life knows is not what emotion knows. Thus, those with emotion strive for detachment yet cannot attain it—yet detachment is achieved through being without emotion; those with emotion strive for sagehood yet cannot attain it—yet sagehood is achieved through being without emotion. Is it merely that sagehood is distant and detachment hard to long for? Even the dullest, the deaf, the blind, the crowing of roosters, the barking of dogs—though they have no emotion in doing so, they too ultimately cannot achieve it. Whether far or near, even if separated by but a hair’s breadth, in the space between brow and eye, it remains forever unattainable. Thus, when the myriad things are shut away, one turns to take from oneself: ears and eyes cannot exchange their tasks to succeed, hands and feet cannot substitute for one another to accomplish work.

When an infant first comes into the world, it does not seek milk with its eyes, does not turn its ears toward light, does not grasp objects with its feet, does not crawl with its hands. How could the hundred limbs lack fixed offices, the bodily form lack an original master, and be governed solely by emotion?

Huizi said: “If one is called human, how can one be without emotion?”
He had not yet understood that bodily form is not emotion.

Zhuangzi said: “This is not what I mean by emotion. What I mean by being without emotion is that one does not internally harm one’s body with likes and dislikes, but constantly follows nature without adding to life.”

If one takes right and wrong as emotion, then one without right or wrong, without likes or dislikes—though possessing bodily form—is merely human in name; where would emotion reside? Acting appropriately and proceeding directly is not emotion. It ceases where appropriateness ceases.

Huizi said: “If one does not augment life, how can one possess a body?”
He had not yet understood that life generates itself, and principle suffices of itself.

Zhuangzi said: “The Dao bestows its form, Heaven bestows its shape—do not internally harm your body with likes and dislikes. Now you estrange your spirit, exhaust your essence, lean against a tree chanting, or recline on a withered catalpa to sleep. Heaven has selected this form, yet you clamor about ‘hard and white.’”

The principle of life is already self-sufficient within bodily form; simply follow it, and the body endures. The emotions of likes and dislikes do not augment life but only harm the body, for life has its allotted portion. If the spirit does not rest within the bounds of its nature, it becomes estranged; if essence does not abide at the limit of its self-generation, it becomes exhausted. Thus, when walking, one leans against a tree chanting; when sitting, one reclines on a catalpa to sleep—this is the self-entrapment of those with emotion. All you do—estranging the spirit, exhausting essence, leaning against trees, reclining on catalpas, chanting and sleeping—this is what the world calls emotion. Yet you speak of ‘Heaven’s selection,’ making clear that what arises from emotion (or: what is called emotion) is not born of emotion—how much less anything else! Thus, though the myriad things and myriad forms, their goings and comings, all emerge from the realm of no-emotion, what use is emotion between them?

* * *

In *The Great and Venerable Master* from the *Inner Chapters* of the *Zhuangzi*:

To know what Heaven does and what man does—this is perfection!
To know what Heaven does is to be born of Heaven; to know what man does is to nourish with what one’s knowledge knows what one’s knowledge does not know, and so complete one’s Heaven-appointed years without dying prematurely—this is the fullness of knowledge.

Though heaven and earth are vast and the myriad things abundant, what they honor as master is the mindless. To know what Heaven and man do is to know that all is natural. Then one inwardly releases the body and outwardly darkens to things, merging with the multitude of mysteries, following them so that nothing is not attained.

“Heaven” means “the natural.” What acts through acting cannot act, but acts through self-acting; what knows through knowing cannot know, but knows through self-knowing. Self-knowing is not knowing; not knowing, then knowledge arises from not-knowing. Self-acting is not acting; not acting, then action arises from non-action. Action arises from non-action, so non-action is the master; knowledge arises from not-knowing, so not-knowing is the origin. Thus the true person abandons knowledge yet knows, does not act yet acts, is born of the natural, attains through sitting in forgetfulness—hence knowledge is called “cut off” and action “gone.”

Man’s life, though the body is but seven feet, must possess the Five Constants. Thus, though the body is small, it upholds heaven and earth to serve it. Therefore, heaven and earth and the myriad things—all that exists—cannot be without one another for a single day. If one thing is lacking, the living have no means to live; if one principle is absent, the Heaven-appointed years have no way to be completed. Yet what the body possesses, one may or may not know; where principle resides, one may or may not act. Thus, what knowledge knows is little, while what the body possesses is much; what action does is small, while where principle resides is vast. Those above cannot measure it to seek its completeness.

What people know need not be the same, nor what they do dare differ. To differ is to become false; once falsehood is complete, the true is never preserved. Some love knowledge and tirelessly exhaust their hundred limbs, yet what they love is but a branch while the root is ruined—this is using what one knows to harm what one does not know.

As for the fullness of knowledge: one knows that what man does has its portion, so one follows without forcing; one knows that what man knows has its limit, so one employs without excess. Thus, not using boundless knowledge to entrap oneself, within the single body, knowledge and non-knowledge darkly meet and are both preserved—this is using what one knows to nourish what one does not know.

Yet there is a danger: knowledge must await something to be fitting, but what it awaits is inherently uncertain. How do we know that what I call Heaven is not man, or what I call man is not Heaven? Even with full knowledge, it is not as good as abandoning knowledge and following Heaven without danger. Knowledge cannot be without approval or disapproval, so it must await something. But one who follows Heaven in life meets things and is fitting. To await is to lack certainty.

My life has its limit—this is Heaven; the heart desires to augment it—this is man. Yet this is only what man calls it; in things, there is nothing that is not (Heaven). “Heaven” means “the natural.” If all people are natural, then order and chaos, success and failure, encounter and non-encounter are not man-made but all natural.

Only with the true person is there true knowledge. What is the true person? The true persons of old did not oppose the few, did not boast of success, did not scheme. Such as these, when they erred, did not regret; when they were fitting, did not feel self-satisfied. Such as these, when climbing high, did not tremble; when entering water, did not get wet; when entering fire, did not feel heat—this is the knowledge that can ascend to the Dao in this way.

Only with the true person do all the world’s knowledges attain their truth and cannot be confused. Not opposing the few means one’s wishes are many; not relying on success and taking the lead among things, the heart roams freely and the multitude of scholars naturally gathers—this is not achieved through scheming. Simply preserving wholeness and fittingness without error, not weighing gain and loss in the mind. To say that knowledge ascends to the Dao is to speak of such distance.

Principle is inherently whole—this is not fear of death. Thus, when the true person walks on land, it is not to avoid getting wet; when far from fire, it is not to escape heat; without error, it is not to contrive fittingness.

Thus, though they do not regard heat as heat, they never rush into fire; though they do not regard wetness as wetness, they never tread into water; though they do not regard death as death, they never forfeit life. Therefore, is life truly produced by life? Is completion truly achieved by completion? One who entrusts all to the Way and leaves nothing unattained—this is the true person. How could they impose fixed intentions upon what they encounter?

The true person of old slept without dreaming, woke without worry, ate without relishing flavor, and breathed deeply. The true person’s breath reaches the heels, while the breath of ordinary people reaches only the throat. Those who are cramped and submissive speak as if choking. The deeper one’s cravings, the shallower one’s heavenly mechanism. They harbor no deliberate thoughts, resting content in whatever they encounter. Eating is simply a matter of principle—it arises from the root. When qi is not balanced and flowing, one must deeply root and settle to the utmost, only then returning to unity and desirelessness.

The true person of old did not delight in life nor detest death. They did not rejoice at departure nor resist return. They came and went with effortless ease, neither clinging to their origin nor seeking their end. They accepted and rejoiced in what came, forgot and returned to it—this is called not using the heart to harm the Dao, not using human effort to assist heaven. This is the true person, one whose body is the transformation itself. Serene and yielding, they entrust all to the supreme principle, thus coming and going without difficulty. They forget all transformations of beginning and end—how could they merely forget life while still probing the meaning of death? They do not question what they receive, meeting all with perfect accord. Their return is not guided by knowledge—it is the utmost attainment.

At birth, humans are still—this is heaven’s nature. Moved by things, they stir—this is nature’s desire. When the things that move people are endless and human desires know no bounds, heavenly principle is extinguished. The true person knows that using the heart turns one from the Dao, and assisting heaven harms life, so they do not act thus. In such a state, their heart is forgotten, their countenance still, their brow serene. They are chill as autumn, warm as spring, their joy and anger flowing with the four seasons, harmonizing with things yet leaving no trace of their depth. Where they dwell, they find contentment in their will. Though they act, they do not disrupt their stillness.

The character *kuí* (頯) describes the appearance of great simplicity. The killing of things is not for the sake of authority. Those who embody the Dao and harmonize with change share the warmth and severity of cold and heat, yet never do so with intent. Nevertheless, they possess the demeanor of warmth and severity, the rhythm of life and death—thus, their likeness is lent to joy and anger. Without intent toward things, they do not violate what is fitting for them; with nothing unfitting, none can fathom their depth.

Therefore, when the sage employs military force, he may overthrow a state yet not lose the people’s hearts. His beneficence extends to ten thousand generations, yet he does not act out of love for others. Thus, one who delights in connecting with things is no sage; one who has partiality is not benevolent; one who follows the seasons is not worthy; one who does not penetrate benefit and harm is no gentleman; one who pursues fame at the cost of self is no scholar; one who sacrifices himself without authenticity does not truly serve others.

Those like Hu Bujie, Wu Guang, Boyi, Shuqi, Jizi, Xuyu, Ji Ta, and Shentu Di—these are servants of others’ service, followers of others’ paths, yet never do they follow their own. They cause the downfall of what people’s hearts already desire to destroy, and thus do not lose the people’s hearts. The bright sun ascends the heavens, illuminating the six directions—not out of love for others that it shines.

Thus, when the sage dwells in the world, he is warm like the spring sun’s natural harmony, so those who receive his grace do not feel gratitude. He is chill like the autumn frost’s natural descent, so those who wither do not bear resentment. The sage has no joy—it is simply that nothing obstructs him, and things flow of themselves. Perfect benevolence has no partiality, entrusting principle to preserve itself. One who adheres to the seasons does not compare to one who forgets time and naturally aligns with it. Unable to unify the paths of right and wrong and pursue benefit while avoiding harm, one injures virtue and burdens what is fitting.

A true scholar abandons fame and finds contentment in himself, so his name matches his reality, and fortune responds to his person. One who loses his nature and forces himself to follow things is enslaved by much—how can he enslave others? These all abandon the self to imitate others, sacrificing themselves for external ends.

The true person of old bore righteousness yet did not form cliques; appeared insufficient yet did not submit. He was angular yet not rigid, expansive yet not florid. His countenance seemed joyful, his actions seemed compelled. His demeanor invited approach, his virtue restrained me. He seemed worldly yet untamable, appeared fond of seclusion yet was not forgetful of speech.

Taking punishment as his body, ritual as his wings, knowledge as his timing, and virtue as his guide: taking punishment as his body, he was lenient in execution; taking ritual as his wings, he moved through the world; taking knowledge as his timing, he acted only when necessary; taking virtue as his guide, he spoke of reaching the heights with those who have feet, yet others truly believed him to be diligent in conduct.

Thus, his liking was singular, and his disliking was singular. His singularity was singular, and his non-singularity was singular. In his singularity, he was a companion of heaven; in his non-singularity, a companion of man. Heaven and man do not overcome one another—this is called the true person. He harmonized with things without forming factions. Empty and without excess, he seemed insufficient yet did not submit. Lowering himself, he was never not exalted. He wandered alone yet did not cling stubbornly. Vast and without attachment, he attained true substance.

The perfected person has no joy, yet is harmonious and at ease—thus he seems joyful. In movement and stillness, action and rest, he always dwells in the ultimate of necessity. He does not allow things to harm himself. He has no direction to pursue. The perfected person is not harsh, yet moves with the world—thus he seems harsh. Lofty and self-content, his depth is boundless, and none see his gate. Unknowing and unperceiving, his heavenly mechanism operates of itself—thus he seems forgetful.

Ritual is how the world conducts itself—it is not of his making. Knowledge responds to the times—it is not his initiative. Virtue is what others follow—it is not his creation. Entrusting governance to its natural course, though it may kill, it is lenient. Following what the world practices, nothing is left undone.

The mutual reception of high and low is an irreversible flow; the grouping of small and great is an inevitable tendency. Vast and without emotion, he is the repository of all knowledge. Receiving the convergence of a hundred streams, dwelling at the pinnacle of human guidance—what does he do? He entrusts the world’s knowledge to the times, commits to what is necessary, and leaves it to the world.

The heights are the root. Speaking in terms of nature, it is the root of nature. Things each have what suffices, and what suffices is the root. Entrusting the self-following of all virtues, he thus reaches the root with those who have feet. When the root is reached, principle is exhausted.

All this is accomplished by others—its completion does not lie with him. Thus, though he dwells at the pinnacle of ten thousand affairs, he is always at leisure and at ease, suddenly unaware of affairs passing through him, forgetful of words in his mouth. Yet people’s great delusion is to truly believe the perfected person is diligent in conduct.

Always without intent, he follows others—thus his likes and dislikes, his approvals and disapprovals, are no different from theirs. His singularity is the companion of heaven; his non-singularity, the companion of man. The true person unites heaven and man, equalizes self and other, not allowing his singularity to differ from his non-singularity.

To be without and yet not singular is heaven. The true person unites heaven and man, equalizes the myriad outcomes. The myriad outcomes do not oppose one another; heaven and man do not overcome one another. Thus, he is vast and without non-singularity, obscure and without non-presence, darkly merging with others.

Life and death are fate; their alternation like night and day is heaven. What humans cannot partake in are all the conditions of things. He regards heaven as his father and loves it as his own body—how much more that which transcends it!

Men deem having a lord superior to themselves, yet still give their lives for him—how much more should they do so for the true! The alternation of day and night is the constant Way of heaven. Thus, to know that life and death are the ultimate of fate, not mere happenstance—like night and day—what is there to cling to? The true person in daylight attains daylight, in night attains night. Taking life and death as day and night, is there anything he does not attain? That men harbor sorrow or joy over what they cannot control is but the sentiment of things, not principle.

The transcendent is called "solitary transformation." The efficacy of mutual dependence is not as great as the perfection of solitary transformation. What men depend upon is heaven; what heaven engenders is solitary transformation. All men take heaven as their father, thus the changes of day and night, the cycles of cold and heat—they dare not resent them, but follow heaven and find peace in it. How much more should they submit to solitary transformation, reaching the realm of profound obscurity! How could they not yield to it? Once they yield, life and death, transformation—all follow fate.

The true is not borrowed from things but is natural. The natural cannot be avoided—how could this apply only to a lord’s command! When a spring dries, fish find themselves on land, moistening one another with breath, wetting one another with foam—better to forget one another in rivers and lakes. Rather than praise Yao and blame Jie, better to forget both and merge with the Dao. Rather than love one another in scarcity, better to forget one another in abundance! Praise and blame both arise from insufficiency.

Thus, the perfectly sufficient forgets good and evil, leaves behind life and death, and unites with transformation, vast and unbounded. Hiding a boat in a ravine, concealing a mountain in a marsh—one calls this secure! Yet in the dead of night, a strong man carries it away, and the deluded do not know. Hiding the small and large has its proper place, yet there is still escape. But to hide the world within the world is to leave no room for escape—this is the great truth of all things. Men rejoice merely in possessing a human form. Yet the human form is but one of ten thousand transformations, never reaching an end—how could its joy be measured?

Thus, the sage roams where things cannot escape and all are preserved. Excelling in early death, excelling in old age, excelling in beginning, excelling in end—men still emulate him, how much more the ten thousand things that depend on it, and the one transformation they await! Form, birth, aging, death—all are myself. Thus, form carries me, birth labors me, aging rests me, death gives me repose. Though these four change, they have never ceased to be me—why should I regret them? Death and life are both fate. Without goodness, there is death; with goodness, there is life—not goodness alone. If one takes my life as good, then my death is also good.

Having spoken of the inescapability of life, death, and transformation, one first presents the ultimate of inescapability, then clarifies it with the sign of inevitable change, to yield to transformation without attachment. The power of powerlessness is greatest in transformation; thus, it lifts heaven and earth to pursue the new, shoulders mountains and ridges to discard the old. Without the slightest pause, in an instant it has traversed the new—thus, heaven and earth and the ten thousand things never cease to shift. The world is ever new, yet men take it as old; boats daily change, yet they see them as before; mountains daily alter, yet they see them as the same. Now, in the time it takes to cross an arm, all has departed into obscurity. Thus, the "I" of a moment ago is no longer the "I" of now. I and the present both pass away—how could one cling to the past? Yet the world does not perceive this, stubbornly believing what it encounters can be grasped and held—how deluded!

Having nothing to hide and yielding entirely, one merges with things without obstruction, unites with transformation without division. Thus, there is no outside, no inside, no death, no life—embodying heaven and earth and uniting with transformation, seeking escape yet finding none. This is the great truth of constant existence, not the petty intent of a single corner. The human form is but one encounter among ten thousand transformations—hardly worth special delight. Within the boundless, all encounters are like the human form—how could only the human form bring joy while other things have none!

◎ Qingfan’s note: *Wen Xuan*’s commentary on Jia Yi’s "Fu on the Owl" cites Sima [Biao] as saying: "It will again transform into nothingness." The *Shiwen* is incomplete.

Originally not human, it transformed into human—having transformed into human, it lost its former state. Losing its former state and rejoicing is rejoicing in what it encounters. Transformation is endless—what is there it does not encounter? Rejoicing in what it encounters, how could its joy have an end! The sage wanders the paths of transformation, drifts upon the stream of daily renewal. The ten thousand things and ten thousand transformations transform with him; transformation is boundless, and he is boundless with it—who can escape it? In life, it is gone; in death, it remains—when is it not present? This is to equalize within a hundred years, not to favor youth and resent age, yet still unable to embody transformation and equalize life and death. Nevertheless, its purity is sufficient to serve as a teacher for others.

This is to merge with the ten thousand things in profound unity and embody transformation—thus, it is fitting that the world takes it as its model. The Dao has essence and trustworthiness, is without action and without form; it can be transmitted but not received, attained but not seen. It is self-originating, self-rooted, existing before heaven and earth, enduring since ancient times. It spiritually animates ghosts and gods, gives birth to heaven and earth. It is before the Great Ultimate yet not high, beneath the Six Poles yet not deep, born before heaven and earth yet not long-lasting, older than antiquity yet not aged.

Xiwei attained it and thereby harnessed heaven and earth; Fuxi attained it and thereby penetrated the Mother of Qi; the Northern Dipper attained it and never erred through all ages; the sun and moon attained it and never ceased through all ages; Kanpi attained it and thereby penetrated Kunlun; Feng Yi attained it and thereby roamed the great rivers; Jianwu attained it and thereby dwelled on the great mountains; the Yellow Emperor attained it and thereby ascended the cloudy heavens; Zhuanxu attained it and thereby dwelled in the Dark Palace; Yuqiang attained it and stood at the North Pole; the Queen Mother of the West attained it and sat in Shaoguang—none knew its beginning, none knew its end. Pengzu attained it and lived from the time of Youyu to the Five Hegemons; Fu Yue attained it and served as minister to Wu Ding, ruling the world, riding the Eastern Ridge and straddling the Tail of the Winnowing Basket to join the ranks of the stars.

There exists a passion without passion, thus it is non-action; there exists a constancy without constancy, thus it is formless. From antiquity to the present, it has been transmitted and dwelled in, yet none can receive and possess it. All things find their own accommodation within it, yet none perceive its form. Illumination requires nothing of being to be non-being. Non-being—how could it give birth to the numinous? Not being numinous, ghosts and emperors become numinous of themselves—this is the numinosity of non-numinosity. Not giving birth, heaven and earth are born of themselves—this is the birth of non-birth.

Thus, the numinosity of the numinous is insufficient to be numinous, while non-numinosity is truly numinous. What achievement is there to claim? What affair is there to rely upon? The Way’s ubiquity means that where it is high, there is no height; where it is deep, there is no depth; where it is enduring, there is no duration; where it is old, there is no age. It is everywhere, yet wherever it is, it is nothing.

Moreover, above and below, nothing fails to reach it—one cannot speak of it in terms of high or low. Within and without, nothing fails to attain it—one cannot name it in terms of inner or outer. Moving in unison with transformation, one cannot speak of it as enduring. Ever without beginning or end, one cannot call it old.

The Way is incapable. This speaks of attaining it through the Way, yet only clarifies that it is self-attained. Self-attained—yet the Way cannot cause one to attain it; nor, when I have not attained it, can I bring about attainment. Thus, all who attain it neither rely on the Way externally nor depend on themselves internally, but suddenly attain it of themselves and transform alone.

The difficulty of life is like this self-transformation and self-attainment. Once life is attained, why fear its loss and strive for it? Thus, striving for life is insufficient to preserve life, for life does not arise from oneself—striving for it only harms one’s true life.

Nanbo Zikui asked Nü Yu, “You are advanced in years, yet your complexion is like that of a child. Why is this?” Nü Yu replied, “I have heard the Way.”

Having heard the Way, one allows life to arise of itself—thus one’s qi and complexion remain whole.”

Nanbo Zikui said, “Can the Way be learned?”
“No! How could it be? You are not the right person. Now, Pulam Yi possesses the talent of a sage but not the Way of a sage, while I possess the Way of a sage but not the talent of a sage. I wished to teach him, hoping that he might indeed become a sage. If not, to impart the Way of a sage to one with the talent of a sage would be easy. Yet I persisted in teaching him, and after three days he was able to let go of the world. Having let go of the world, I again persisted, and after seven days he was able to let go of things. Having let go of things, I persisted once more, and after nine days he was able to let go of life. Having let go of life, he could then attain clarity at dawn. With clarity at dawn, he could then perceive the solitary. Perceiving the solitary, he could then transcend past and present. Transcending past and present, he could then enter into that which is neither dead nor alive. That which kills life does not die; that which gives life to life does not live. As a thing, it sends nothing forth without escort, meets nothing without welcome, destroys nothing without ruin, and completes nothing without fulfillment. Its name is *yingning*—tranquility through engagement. *Yingning* is that which is completed only after engagement.”

*Wai* means “to let go.” Things are what one requires morning and night, what is intimately tied to the self and hard to forget. To let go of them entirely—this is *du yi*. To let go of life is to cease hating death; ceasing to hate death, one is at peace with whatever one encounters, suddenly unobstructed, acting in accord with the moment—this is clarity at dawn. One moves in unison with the solitary. Clinging to life gives rise to death; hating death gives rise to life. Thus, without clinging and without hatred, one can transcend death and life. Allowing things to escort themselves, nothing is not escorted. Allowing things to welcome themselves, nothing is not welcomed. Allowing things to destroy themselves, nothing is not destroyed. Allowing things to complete themselves, nothing is not completed. One who merges with things—though things entangle, they entangle yet remain ever tranquil.

Nanbo Zikui said, “From whom did you alone hear this?”
He replied, “I heard it from the disciple of *Fumo*, the disciple of *Fumo* heard it from the grandson of *Luosong*, the grandson of *Luosong* heard it from *Zhanming*, *Zhanming* heard it from *Niexu*, *Niexu* heard it from *Xuyi*, *Xuyi* heard it from *Yu’ou*, *Yu’ou* heard it from *Xuanming*, *Xuanming* heard it from *Canliao*, and *Canliao* heard it from *Yishi*.”

If things entangle yet one alone remains unentangled, one is ruined.

Thus, entangled yet allowing it, there is nothing that does not bend to completion. *Xuanming* is so named because it is "non-being yet not nothingness." One who ascends through names to reach non-being must attain non-being beyond the realm of names. Therefore, though *Xuanming* is profound, it has not yet reached the ultimate, and so it is further entrusted to *Canliao*—this is the profound within the profound.

The principle of spontaneity is one that is cultivated through accumulated practice. Indeed, one ascends from the near to the distant, refines from the coarse to the subtle, and thus only after seven layers does one reach the name of non-being, and after nine layers does one doubt that non-being is the beginning.

Zisi, Ziyu, Zili, and Zilai said to one another, “Who can take non-being as the head, life as the spine, and death as the tailbone? Who knows that death and life, existence and nonexistence, are of one body—we shall befriend him!” The four looked at one another and smiled, their hearts in perfect accord, and so they became friends.

Soon after, Ziyu fell ill, and Zisi went to visit him. He said, “How wondrous! The Creator would make me like this, all crooked and bent.”

His back was hunched, his spine protruded, his five organs pressed upward, his chin tucked into his navel, his shoulders towered above his crown, and the growth on his neck pointed to the sky. The qi of yin and yang was in disarray, yet his heart remained at ease, untroubled. He hobbled to the well, peered into its depths, and said, “Alas! The Creator would again make me like this, all crooked and bent.”

He did not take it as an affliction. For one who follows the transformations of nature, there is no lament—only the world laments on his behalf.

Zisi said, “Do you resent it?”

He replied, “No, why should I resent it? If gradually my left arm were transformed into a rooster, I would use it to herald the dawn. If gradually my right arm were transformed into a crossbow, I would use it to hunt an owl for roasting. If gradually my tailbone were transformed into a wheel, and my spirit into a horse, I would ride it—why would I need another steed?

Moreover, to gain is a matter of timing; to lose is a matter of compliance. At peace with the time and in accord with compliance, sorrow and joy cannot enter. This is what the ancients called ‘suspended release,’ yet one who cannot release himself is bound by things. Furthermore, things have long been unable to overcome Heaven—why should I resent it?”

"Jìn" means gradual. When the body undergoes transformation and harmonizes with change, there is nowhere it does not adapt, and no adaptation that is impossible. What one encounters in the moment is called by the world ‘gain.’ Since time does not pause for even an instant, to comply and move on is called by the world ‘loss.’ If one cannot release oneself, then the myriad things bind one together.

Thus, one who can release is released from all things, while one who cannot release is released from nothing. Heaven cannot be without day and night—how can I be without life and death and resent it?

Soon after, Zilai fell ill, gasping as if near death. His wife and children gathered around, weeping. Zili went to see him and said, "Shoo! Away! Do not be startled by transformation!" Leaning against the doorframe, he spoke to him: "How wondrous is the Creator! What will it make of you next? Where will it send you? Will it make you into a mouse’s liver? Or an insect’s limb?"

Life and death are but waking and sleeping. By principle, one ought to sleep—one does not wish to be startled awake. To transform and die is fitting; there is no need to be alarmed by it.

Zilai said, "For a child, parents may command east or west, north or south, and one must obey. Yin and yang are no less commanding than parents. If they approach my death and I do not heed them, I would be defiant—what fault would lie with them? The great earth bears me with form, labors me with life, eases me with age, and rests me with death. Thus, to treat my life well is to treat my death well.

Now, when the great smith casts metal, if the metal leaps up and says, ‘I must become a *Mo Ye* sword!’ the smith will surely deem it an inauspicious metal. Now, if one acquires a human form and says, ‘I am human! I am human!’ the Creator will surely deem it an inauspicious person. If one takes heaven and earth as a great furnace and the Creator as a great smith, where could one go and not be acceptable?"

He then fell into a deep sleep and awoke with a start.

Since ancient times, some have been able to defy their parents’ commands, but none have been able to defy the changes of yin and yang or resist the cycle of day and night. Life and death are but day and night—nothing distant. When the time comes to die, it is not something to forbid. To stubbornly harbor resistance is to defy principle and hasten one’s death. The swiftness of death arises from one’s own defiance, not from death itself.

*Bi* refers to death; while alive, one regards death as *bi*. Principle is always present. "I am human! I am human!"—this is merely the wish to remain human, just as metal leaping up is known to be inauspicious, yet one cannot allow it to transform of its own accord.

The Dao of transformation encounters nothing it does not meet. Now, to encounter a human form—was this done deliberately? Life is not created deliberately; it arises naturally in time. To strive to possess it is delusion. People know that metal bound by attachment is inauspicious, so when one realizes one’s own nature is no different from metal, the attachments of the heart can be released. Once released, there is nothing that cannot be released.

Waking and sleeping remain as they are, without life and death burdening the heart.

Zisang Hu, Mengzi Fan, and Ziqin Zhang spoke to one another: "Who can join together without joining, act for one another without acting? Who can ascend to heaven and wander in the mists, roam without limit, forget life, and have no end?"

One who embodies heaven and earth and darkly merges with transformation—though hands and feet have different functions and the five organs distinct offices, they never join together yet all joints harmonize: this is joining without joining. They never act for one another yet inner and outer mutually aid: this is acting without acting. If one instead employs the mind to worry over hands and feet, or moves limbs to manage the five organs, the more one strives, the more inner and outer become exhausted.

Thus, one who takes the world as a single body has no partiality in its midst. Entrusting all things without exception, forgetting life, one forgets all—hence one can follow change and adapt to transformation, with nothing left incomplete.

The three looked at one another and laughed, their hearts in accord, and so became friends. Yet was this truly friendship? It was but a lodging of luminous intimacy, free from the nearness of affectionate attachment."

After a time, Zisang Hu died, his body yet unburied. When Confucius heard of it, he sent Zigong to attend the rites. Some wove funeral songs, others plucked the zither, and together they harmonized in song: *"Alas, Zisang Hu! Alas, Zisang Hu! You have returned to your true nature, while we remain as men!"* Zigong hastened forward and said, *"May I ask—is it proper to sing before the dead?"*

To weep when others weep is but a trace of custom. To equalize life and death, to forget sorrow and joy, to sing before the dead—this is the utmost of those beyond the world. The two looked at one another and laughed: *"How could he know the meaning of ritual!"*

One who knows the meaning of ritual must wander beyond to order what is within, guard the mother to preserve the child, and follow the heart’s truth without deviation. If one instead clings to reputation, is bound by form and convention, then filial piety cannot bear sincerity, kindness cannot bear substance, and father and son, brothers, harbor feelings and deceive one another—how could this be the great meaning of ritual?

Zigong returned and told Confucius: *"What manner of men are they? They cultivate no conduct, yet treat the body as external. They sang before the dead without change of countenance—there is no name for them. What manner of men are they?"*

Confucius said: *"They wander beyond the world, while I, Qiu, wander within it. Beyond and within do not reach one another, yet I sent you to offer condolences—how shallow I was! They are now companions of the Creator, roaming in the single qi of heaven and earth. To them, life is but an excrescent wart, death but the bursting of a sore. How could such as they know where life and death, before and after, reside? Borrowing from strange things, entrusting to a shared body, they forget liver and gall, leave behind ears and eyes. They turn and return, beginning and ending, without knowing head or tail. Vague and drifting beyond the dust and grime, carefree in the work of non-action—how could they fret over worldly ritual to perform for the eyes and ears of the crowd?"*

Zigong said: *"Then what Way does the Master follow?"*

There is an ultimate principle where beyond and within merge. There has never been one who attains the utmost of wandering beyond yet does not merge within, nor one who merges within yet does not wander beyond. Thus the sage constantly wanders beyond to merge within, is without mind to follow what is, and so though all day long he appears in form, his spirit and qi remain unchanged. Though he bends and rises amid ten thousand affairs, he is serene and at ease. To see form yet not reach spirit—this is the common burden of the world.

Thus when one sees him moving with the crowd, none can say he abandons things and leaves men behind; when one sees him embodying transformation and responding to affairs, none can say he sits in forgetfulness and attains self-possession. How could it be only the sage who is not thus? It must be said that the ultimate principle has no such thing.

Therefore, when Zhuangzi sought to clarify the lineage of the great tradition to awaken the world, had he simply praised Confucius in this way, some might have relied on what they saw to reject it. So he transcended the sage’s inner traces and lodged the beyond in those few disciples. One should forget what is lodged to seek the great meaning of his composition, and then the Way of wandering beyond to merge within will be clear and self-evident. Thus Zhuangzi’s book is a discourse that engages the world while transcending it.

Condolences are a near affair of the world within; to apply them to the world beyond is shallow. All is merged, thus there is no duality. Like a wart that hangs of itself, a growth that clings of itself—this is the temporary gathering of qi, not what one delights in. Like an abscess that bursts of itself, a sore that drains of itself—this is the natural dispersal of qi, not what one regrets. Life and death alternate without end; going along with them, there is nowhere one cannot go. Thus one does not know where victory or defeat lies.

*"Assuming"* means *"relying on."* Now life and death, gathering and dispersal, change without pattern—all are strange things. There is nothing strange that is not relied upon, so though what is relied upon differs, together they form a single body. Entrusting them to the five organs is still forgetting—what thing is worth recognizing? Without recognition from the start, one can freely follow the path of transformation, darkly merge with the waves of turning and returning, and not know where beginning or end reaches its limit. This is to wander merged in principle.

The *"work of non-action"* is not mere folded-hands silence; *"beyond the dust and grime"* is not hiding in mountains and forests. What he shows to the crowd is but their dust and grime—it is not the dark merging of the world beyond. Zigong had not heard of *"nature and the Way of Heaven,"* so he saw what he relied upon but not what he relied upon *for.*

What one relies upon *for* is no reliance—how could the world be aware of it? Confucius said: *"Qiu is a man punished by Heaven. Nevertheless, I share this with you."*

To take the mundane world as shackles is to clarify that what is prized lies beyond it. Those who wander beyond depend on what is within; those who depart from humanity conform to custom. Thus, those who possess all under Heaven do not take all under Heaven as their own. Only by relinquishing things can one enter the multitude; only by sitting in forgetfulness can one respond to affairs. The more one relinquishes, the more one gains. Should one abide in this ultimate state, then even if one wished to cast it aside, principle would naturally come of its own—this is what neither Heaven nor man can pardon.

Zigong said, "May I ask the method?"

Though bound by the world’s shackles, it is but something I share with you. This makes clear that I am ever beyond them.

Confucius said, "Fish come together in water; men come together in the Dao. Those who come together in water dig ponds to secure their sustenance; those who come together in the Dao find stability without affairs. Thus it is said: Fish forget one another in rivers and lakes; men forget one another in the arts of the Dao."

Asked about the meaning of wandering beyond while sharing within, Confucius said, "Fish come together in water; men come together in the Dao. Those who come together in water dig ponds to secure their sustenance; those who come together in the Dao find stability without affairs. Thus it is said: Fish forget one another in rivers and lakes; men forget one another in the arts of the Dao."

Zigong said, "May I ask about the extraordinary person?"

The means of their convergence may differ, yet in attaining affairs through non-affair, in sharing within from beyond form—thus securing sustenance and finding stability—none fail to follow this principle. All remain unaware of it, and so perfect non-action.

It is said: "The extraordinary person is extraordinary among men yet equal to Heaven. Thus it is said: The petty man of Heaven is the noble man of men; the noble man of men is the petty man of Heaven."

Those who are self-sufficient and forget one another—none under Heaven are otherwise. The perfected person is ever self-sufficient, and thus ever forgetful.

Asked where lies that which was earlier called "beyond form yet unyoked to custom," it is this: One who merges with the inner wanders in the outer. Only by wandering in the outer to merge with the inner, entrusting all things to their natural course, allowing each being’s nature to find its fullness so that the Dao of emperors and kings is realized—this is to be extraordinary among men yet equal to Heaven.

Spoken in terms of nature, there is no small or great among men; spoken in terms of human principle, those equal to Heaven may be called noble men.

Yan Hui asked Confucius, saying: "Mengsun Cai, when his mother died, wept without tears, felt no grief in his heart, and showed no sorrow in mourning. Lacking these three, he yet excelled in mourning throughout the state of Lu. Is it truly possible to gain such a reputation without substance? I find this deeply strange."

In the state of Lu, they observed his rites, while Yan Hui examined his heart. Confucius said: "Mengsun Cai has fully realized it—he has advanced beyond knowledge. Yet even in seeking simplicity, he finds no attainment, for he has already attained simplicity. Mengsun Cai knows not why he lives, nor why he dies. He knows not what to follow first, nor what to follow after. As if transformed into things, he awaits the transformations he does not know. Moreover, as he is about to transform, how could he know what is not yet transformed? As he is about to remain untransformed, how could he know what has already transformed? You and I are but dreamers who have not yet awakened!

Furthermore, he may take on startled forms, yet his heart remains undamaged; he may dwell in a new abode, yet his essence is not depleted. Mengsun Cai alone is awake—when others weep, he weeps too, for that is simply what is fitting for him. And yet, we merely share this understanding in our ears—how could we be certain that what I call ‘I’ is not truly ‘I’? Suppose you dream of being a bird soaring through the heavens, or a fish diving into the depths. How can we know whether the speaker now is awake or dreaming? When one attains perfect ease, laughter does not follow; when laughter is offered, it does not lead to forced cheer. Resting in spontaneity and departing with transformation, one enters the vast oneness of Heaven."

One who fully grasps the principle of life and death, responding fittingly to both inner and outer, moves in accordance with Heaven’s course—this is beyond the reach of mere knowledge. To select and choose between life and death without discerning their difference is like the progression of spring, autumn, winter, and summer. Having already simplified and found no distinction, there is nothing not at ease. With nothing not at ease, one does not weigh life and death in the mind but entrusts them to spontaneous transformation. Whatever one encounters, one is at peace. Not resisting transformation, life and death turn and merge with it as one, just as one forgets what one knows in the present—how could one anticipate what is unknown and fret over it in advance? Having transformed and come to life, how could one know the time before birth? Not yet transformed and facing death, how could one know what comes after? Thus, there is nothing to avoid or pursue, and one moves together with transformation.

Life and death are but waking and dreaming. Now, if in a dream one believes oneself awake, there is no way to prove that waking is not also a dream. If one cannot prove that waking is not a dream, then one also cannot prove that life is not death. Whether in life, death, waking, or dreaming, we know not where we stand. Yet in whatever one encounters, there is nothing not self-attained—why grieve over this while worrying about that? Transformation merely startles the form, so one does not allow life and death to burden the heart. The changes of the bodily frame are but daily renewals of one’s dwelling—one’s nature does not take them as death.

The one who is always awake meets nothing with resistance, so when others weep, he weeps too—this is simply what is fitting for him. Life, death, and transformation—all are mine. Since all are mine, what have I lost? Never having lost myself, what cause have I for sorrow? Without resistance, when others weep, he weeps; without sorrow, though he weeps, he is not grieved. Embracing all as mine, he harmonizes inner and outer, pervades past and present, and daily renews with transformation—how could he know where his true self resides? This means there is nowhere he does not find himself at ease.

When dreaming, one believes oneself awake—how then can one know that the present is not a dream? How can one know it is not waking? The transformations of waking and dreaming are boundless, so the changes of life and death are nothing to regret. Wherever one arrives is fitting, so one forgets the fitting—thus laughter does not follow. *Pai* means to shift or move on. In ritual, weeping must be mournful, laughter must be joyful—if sorrow and joy linger in the heart, one cannot move with ease. But Mengsun always abides in ease, so though he weeps, he is not grieved, and moves together with transformation. At peace with shifting and departing with transformation, he enters the silence and becomes one with Heaven."

From this point up to Zisi, their attainment is the same. The mourning they observe differs, so their songs and weeping are not alike.

Yi’erzi visited Xu You, who asked: "How has Yao aided you?" *Zi* here means to supply or assist. Yi’erzi replied: "Yao told me: ‘You must personally practice benevolence and righteousness and clearly distinguish right from wrong.’" Xu You said: "Why then have you come here? Yao has already branded you with benevolence and righteousness and mutilated you with right and wrong. How will you wander the boundless, carefree, and ever-shifting paths?"

"Such is how one will maim and cripple oneself with penal teachings, unable to wander again in the fields of self-attainment or the unbound paths. Yi’erzi said: 'Even so, I wish to wander within its fence.' He dared not seek to tread the central Way, but only wished to wander along its border. Xu You said: 'Not so. The blind have no part in the beauty of brows and eyes or the radiance of countenance; the sightless have no part in the splendor of blue and yellow, embroidered and patterned robes.'

Yi’erzi said: 'When Wu Zhuang lost her beauty, when Ju Liang lost his strength, when the Yellow Emperor lost his wisdom—all occurred within the forge and hammer’s span. How do we know the Maker of Things will not cease my branding and mend my mutilation, allowing me to mount completion and follow you, Master?'

This speaks of how the things of the world are not all self-complete; the principles of spontaneity also require smelting and forging to become vessels. Thus these three, too, having heard the Dao, afterward forgot their former pursuits. All this is figurative speech, meant to dispel the burdens of clouded action.

One who follows nature and goes straight forth—this is spontaneity. One who goes forth and harms nature, yet can change when nature is harmed—this, too, is spontaneity. How do we know that my nature is not fated to cease branding and mend mutilation, to mount the Way of completion and follow you? To cast me aside and refuse instruction—this would hardly be the Maker of Things’ perfection.'

Xu You said: 'Alas! It cannot be known.'"

"Let me speak to you in broad strokes: O my Master! O my Master! You pulverize the myriad things yet claim no righteousness; your beneficence extends to ten thousand generations yet claims no benevolence; you are older than high antiquity yet claim no seniority; you embrace heaven and earth, carving and sculpting all forms, yet claim no artistry. This is the realm in which you wander!"

All things are thus of themselves, with no partiality intervening—where then could benevolence and righteousness reside? They renew daily. It is spontaneity, and thus no artifice. To wander in non-action and take as teacher that which has no teacher.

Yan Hui said: "I have advanced."
By taking reduction as advancement.

Zhongni said: "What do you mean?"
He replied: "I have forgotten ritual and music."
He said: "Good, but not yet sufficient."

Benevolence is the trace of universal love; righteousness is the achievement of things. Love is not benevolence, yet the trace of benevolence is enacted; achievement is not righteousness, yet the achievement of righteousness appears. To retain benevolence and righteousness is insufficient to comprehend the origin of love and benefit in non-intention—thus to forget them is permissible. Yet merely forgetting their traces still falls short of profound penetration.

Another day, he again appeared and said: "I have advanced."
He asked: "What do you mean?"
He replied: "I have forgotten benevolence and righteousness."
He said: "Good, but not yet sufficient."

Ritual is the function of the physical form; music is the instrument of joy in life. To forget the instrument is not as profound as forgetting that which enables the instrument. Another day, he again appeared and said: "I have advanced!" He asked: "What do you mean?" He replied: "I have attained *zuowang* (sitting-forgetting)." Zhongni started and said: "What is *zuowang*?" Yan Hui said: "To discard the limbs and body, to cast off perception and intelligence, to depart from form and abandon knowledge, and to merge with the Great Thoroughfare—this is called *zuowang*." When nothing is not the same, then there is never discomfort; when there is never discomfort, what preferences or aversions could there be? Zhongni said: "To be the same is to have no preferences; to transform is to have no constancy. And you are indeed worthy! I, Qiu, shall follow after you."

One who is the same as transformation follows only where transformation leads, and thus has no constancy. Ziyu and Zisang were friends. When continuous rain fell for ten days, Ziyu said: "Zisang must be in dire straits!" He wrapped up rice and went to feed him. Arriving at Zisang’s gate, he heard sounds like singing, like weeping—plucking the zither and saying: "Father! Mother! Heaven!"

"Alas, man!"—his voice unable to bear the strain, he hastened to recite his verse.

Ziyu entered and asked: "Why do you sing this poem in such a manner?"

These two acted for one another without acting for one another. Now, wrapping rice to feed him was but following Heaven’s principle and happening of itself—it was not that he went because of acting for him. To suspect the presence of emotion is to rush forth and depart far from the principle.

He said: "I pondered what brought me to this extremity but found no answer. Would my parents wish me to be poor? Heaven covers without partiality, Earth bears without partiality—would Heaven and Earth single me out for poverty? I sought the one who caused this but could not find him! Yet to reach this extremity—such is fate!" These words declare that all things are natural, with no doer behind them.

---

*From the "Inner Chapters," "Responding as Emperor," Chapter Seven of the* Zhuangzi:

Nie Que questioned Wang Ni four times, and four times Wang Ni professed ignorance. Thereupon Nie Que leapt up in great joy and went to tell Master Pu Yi.

Master Pu Yi said: "Have you only now come to know this? The Lord of Yu did not match the Lord of Tai. The Lord of Yu concealed benevolence to entice men—he did gain men, yet never emerged from the realm of the unhuman. The Lord of Tai slept in tranquil ease, awoke in carefree leisure. Now he took himself for a horse, now for an ox. His knowledge was true to his nature, his virtue most genuine, and he never entered the realm of the unhuman."

One who is without intent and follows self-transformation is fit to be emperor. The Lord of Yu and the Lord of Tai were but traces of worldly affairs, not that by which traces are made. That by which traces are made is traceless—who in the world could name it? Never having been named, what victory or defeat could there be? Yet the traceless one rides the myriad changes, treads the ten thousand ages. The world has its level plains and perilous passes, so traces may fall short. Those who take what they love as the "human" and what they hate as the "unhuman" are merely bounded by the realm of right and wrong. One who can emerge from the realm of the unhuman must enter the realm where nothing is unhuman. Thus there is no gain, no loss, no right, no wrong—how could it be merely concealing benevolence to entice men! Being thus, what "human" or "unhuman" could there be? This may be called emerging from the realm of the unhuman. Following one’s own knowledge, thus the emotions are true. Following one’s own attainment, thus there is no falsity. Not entering the realm of right and wrong, one cuts off the age of the Lord of Yu.

---

Jian Wu met Jie Yu the Madman.

Jie Yu asked: "What did Ri Zhongshi tell you?"

Jian Wu said: "He told me that a ruler of men, issuing his own standards of laws, models, principles, and measures, who would dare not listen and be transformed?"

Jie Yu said: "This is deceptive virtue. In governing the world, it is like crossing the sea to dig a river or making a mosquito carry a mountain. Does the sage govern externally? He rectifies himself and then acts, ensuring he is truly capable of his task. Birds fly high to avoid the harm of arrows and snares; the field mouse burrows deep beneath the sacred mound to escape the calamity of fumigation and digging—yet are these two creatures lacking in knowledge?" To impose one’s own designs upon things is to cause things to lose their truth.

To entrust all things to their own nature is to accomplish without action; to impose one’s will upon the world with a single body is to leave no task complete and no burden borne. One must preserve only what lies within the bounds of one’s nature. Each fulfills its allotted span of life. One does not attempt what one cannot. Even birds and beasts each have their means of self-preservation—thus when emperors and kings leave them to their own devices without interference, they achieve completion on their own. Have you truly failed to see how these two creatures each preserve themselves without instruction?

Tian Gen wandered to Yin Yang and arrived at the banks of the Liao River, where he encountered an unnamed man and asked, “I beg to inquire how to govern the world.” The unnamed man replied, “Away with you! You are a vulgar man—why do you ask such an untimely question? I am about to become one with the Creator. When I weary of this, I will ride the boundless bird beyond the six directions and wander in the land of Nothingness, dwelling in the vast wilderness. Why would you trouble my heart with governing the world?”

To ask how to govern the world is not to rise from the Great Beginning nor to rest in the Dark and Obscure. Let each person act according to their own nature. "Boundless" refers to the myriad fragments. Riding the myriad fragments, one gallops through the ten thousand things, thus able to move freely without constraint or obstruction. To leave all things to their own sphere of self-realization is to achieve order without governance.

He asked the unnamed man again, “If you let your mind wander in simplicity, harmonize your qi with emptiness, follow things as they are without imposing private designs, then the world will be well-ordered.” To follow one’s nature without adornment is simplicity. To rest in stillness within one’s nature is emptiness. To let nature take its course is impartiality; to seek to augment it with personal desire is partiality. Partiality ultimately fails to sustain life, while following impartiality preserves it.

Yang Ziju visited Lao Dan and said, “Suppose there were a man swift, strong, and resolute, with keen perception and clear understanding, who studies the Dao without weariness—could such a one be compared to a enlightened king?” Lao Dan replied, “Such a one, in the eyes of the sage, is like a clerk or artisan, bound by skill and toil, exhausting his body and startling his mind. Moreover, the patterns of tigers and leopards attract hunters, and the agility of apes and monkeys invites capture. Could such a one be compared to an enlightened king?”

Yang Ziju, abashed, said, “I venture to ask about the governance of an enlightened king.” Lao Dan replied, “Such exertions cannot secure one’s own person—how could they compare to the sage-king? These are all cases of being ensnared by one’s own patterns and skills, not of riding emptiness to master the boundless.”

Lao Dan continued, “The governance of an enlightened king: his achievements cover the world yet seem not his own; he transforms and bestows upon the ten thousand things, yet the people do not rely on him. He remains nameless, allowing things to rejoice in themselves. He stands in the unfathomable and wanders in Nothingness.”

If the world lacked an enlightened king, none could attain self-realization. That they do so is truly the king’s achievement—yet his achievement lies in non-action and entrusting the world to itself. When all in the world act according to their own nature, it seems as though the enlightened king played no part. The enlightened king fully realizes the nature of things, so each person claims, “I am thus of myself,” unaware of their reliance on him. Though his achievements cover the world, he does not claim them as his own, so all things believe they have attained their own joy.

He dwells in the path of transformation, daily renewed and without fixed form. Identifying with the ten thousand things, he wanders in emptiness. If one cannot merge with things, one is constantly at odds with them—how then could one wander in emptiness?

In Zheng there was a divine diviner named Ji Xian, who knew the life and death, survival and demise, fortune and misfortune, longevity and early death of men, predicting them by year, month, or day with godlike accuracy. When the people of Zheng saw him, they all fled. Liezi met him and was enchanted. Returning home, he told Huzi, “At first I thought your Dao was supreme, but now I see there is something even greater.” He did not delight in hearing his own death date.

He claimed that Ji Xian’s attainment surpassed even yours. Huzi said, “I have shown you only its outward pattern, not yet its true substance. How then could you have grasped the Dao? A flock of hens without a rooster—how could they lay eggs? You present the Dao to the world with such insistence, and so you must be believed. That is why others can read your fortune.” He then said, “Bring him to me and let him observe me.”

This showed that Liezi had not yet embraced the Dao. Without embracing the Dao, one still has a mind; having a mind and clinging to one aspect, one must be believed by the world—thus others can read one’s fortune.

The next day, Liezi brought Ji Xian to see Huzi. Upon leaving, Ji Xian said to Liezi, “Alas! Your master is dead! He will not live. It will not be ten days! I saw something strange—like damp ashes.” Liezi entered, his tears soaking his lapels as he told Huzi. Huzi said, “Earlier, I showed him the pattern of earth—sprouting yet unmoving, still. This must be how he saw me sealing my virtue’s mechanism. Bring him again.”

Still and unmoving as a sprout, neither correcting itself nor standing upright, sharing the barrenness of withered wood and the lifelessness of damp ashes—this is the moment when the perfected person is without response. The perfected person moves like heaven, rests like earth, flows like water, and abides in profound stillness. Profound stillness and flowing water, heavenly motion and earthly rest—though they seem not to act, they are spontaneously so, all one in essence.

Now, when Ji Xian saw him dwelling like a corpse in seated oblivion, he declared him near death; when he glimpsed his spirit moving in accord with heaven, he took it for signs of life. Truly, if one responds without employing the mind so that principle naturally aligns with the mysterious tally, rising and falling with transformations while taking the world as measure, then one becomes master of things and follows the timeless without limit—thus beyond the diviner’s reckoning. This is the great meaning of "Responding to Emperors and Kings."

When the mechanism of virtue remains unactivated, it is called "sealed."

The next day, Liezi again brought Ji Xian to see Huzi. Upon leaving, Ji Xian said to Liezi, “Fortunate indeed! Your master has met me—he is recovering! Fully alive now! I see his sealed mechanism in motion!” Liezi entered and told Huzi. Huzi said, “Earlier, I showed him heaven and earth—names and realities did not enter, yet the mechanism arose from the soles of my feet. This must be how he saw my mechanism of goodness. Bring him again.”

*Mechanism* here means the same as *trigger*.

Now he himself realized that what he saw yesterday was the sealed mechanism in motion, which is why he had declared him near death. Within heaven and earth, the work of covering and bearing is manifest—compared to the pattern of earth, is this not a step beyond? This is the appearance of responsive resonance. When one entrusts oneself to natural covering and bearing, the heavenly mechanism mysteriously responds, and the adornments of fame and profit all become discarded things. The mechanism always arises from the utmost height, and when it moves and is good for the other, the other perceives it.

The next day, Liezi again brought Ji Xian to see Huzi. Upon leaving, Ji Xian said to Liezi, “Your master is unsettled—I can find no basis for divination. Try to settle him, and I will divine again.” Liezi entered and told Huzi. Huzi said, “Earlier, I showed him the Great Vacuity where nothing prevails—this must be how he saw my mechanism of balanced qi. The whirlpool formed by the *ni* fish is a deep pool; the stillness of still water makes a deep pool; the flow of flowing water makes a deep pool. There are nine names for deep pools—here we have three of them. Bring him again.”

Dwelling at the utmost height of the Great Vacuity, the mind drifts vast and still, merging all directions into profound unity—thus victory and defeat cannot find place within it. There is no movement that is not level, all blending into oneness. Those who peer at heaven through a tube see no bounds, and so it seems uneven. The deep pool is but the name for stillness and silence.

Water is ever without intent, yielding to external things; thus, whether flowing or still, whether the *ni* fish coils or the dragon leaps, it remains ever a deep pool, never losing its stillness and silence. The perfected person acts when employing it and ceases when setting it aside; though action and cessation differ, their profound silence is one. Thus, three differences are briefly shown to clarify this.

Though the currents shift through nine transformations, though order and chaos swirl in confusion, one who dwells at this height remains ever serene and self-content, drifting in forgetfulness of all doing.

The next day, Liezi again brought Ji Xian to see Huzi. Before he could steady himself, Ji Xian lost composure and fled. Huzi said, “Pursue him!” Liezi chased him but could not catch up. Returning, he reported to Huzi, “He has vanished—he is gone. I could not reach him.” Huzi said, “Earlier, I showed him what has never yet departed from my source. I met him with emptiness and followed his flow, so he knew not who or what I was. Taking me for drifting grass, taking me for shifting currents, he fled.”

Though transformations are ceaseless, their roots ever lie deep in hidden depths. Without intent, one follows the transformations of things, drifting unbound by any tether. As decay and dissolution shift like the world’s currents, there is nowhere one does not adapt. The perfected person is one, yet responds to the world’s changes and moves with the times—thus the physiognomist could find no place to fix his gaze, lost himself, and fled. This illustrates that one who would respond as emperor or king has no fixed method.

Thereafter, Liezi returned home believing he had never yet begun to learn. For three years he did not go out, cooked for his wife, and fed pigs as he would feed people, showing no partiality in his dealings. Carved and polished, he returned to simplicity, standing alone as a mere clod of earth. Though the world swirled in confusion, he remained thus to the end. He forgot distinctions of noble and base, attending only to what he encountered. He cast aside ornament and sought substance, shedding external embellishments. Though active, his true nature never scattered.

Let things each fulfill their own ends. Be not the host of fame, the storehouse of schemes, the bearer of tasks, or the master of knowledge. Embrace the boundless and wander without trace. Exhaust what you have received from Heaven, yet perceive no gain—it is emptiness alone! The perfected person’s mind is like a mirror: it neither sends forth nor welcomes, responds yet does not store, and thus can master things without harm. Following things, each finds its proper name. Let things each devise their own plans, entrust tasks to each, and without intent, let each master its own knowledge.

Following the self-so of the world, one may gallop through the ten thousand things without limit. Entrusting oneself to things, one leaves no tracks. When one has enough, one stops—perceiving gain, one does not know when to stop. Without emptiness, one cannot bear the weight of all realities. Reflecting things without emotion, one responds when they come and ceases when they go. Things arrive and are mirrored; the mirror does not act with intent, so though the world be vast, there is no weariness of spirit.

The emperor of the Southern Sea was named Sudden, the emperor of the Northern Sea was named Fleeting, and the emperor of the Central Realm was named Chaos. Sudden and Fleeting often met in the land of Chaos, who treated them very well. Sudden and Fleeting plotted to repay Chaos’s virtue, saying, “All people have seven apertures for seeing, hearing, eating, and breathing—only this one lacks them. Let us try to bore them for him.” Each day they bored one aperture, and on the seventh day, Chaos died. Those who act ruin things.

Xiang Xiu and Guo Xiang’s *Commentary on the Zhuangzi*
*Zhuangzi, Inner Chapters, “Free and Easy Wandering”*

Though small and great differ, when released into the field of self-contentment, things follow their nature, affairs match their capacities, and each fits its allotted place—free and easy wandering is one. How could there be victory or defeat among them?

In the Northern Darkness there is a fish, its name is Kun. The Kun’s greatness—no one knows how many thousand *li* it spans. It transforms into a bird, its name is Peng. The reality of the Peng and the Kun, I have not yet fathomed. The great purport of the *Zhuangzi* lies in free and easy wandering, non-action and self-contentment. Thus it exhausts the extremes of small and great to clarify the fittingness of one’s allotted nature. Those of far-reaching vision should grasp its essential convergence and let go of its incidental lodgings, not needing to quibble over every detail with the living. As long as it does not harm the grand aim, all may be roughly understood.

The Peng’s back—no one knows how many thousand *li* it spans. Roused to flight, its wings are like clouds hanging from the sky. This bird, when the sea surges, will journey to the Southern Darkness. The Southern Darkness is the Celestial Pond. Only a dark sea suffices to bear its body, only ninety thousand *li* suffice to support its wings. Is this a fondness for the strange?
