Simply, great things must arise from great places, and great places must give rise to such great things—this principle is naturally so, not to be feared as a loss. Why trouble the mind over it?

*Qi Xie* is a record of marvels. It says: “When the Peng journeys to the Southern Darkness, it strikes the water for three thousand *li*, then spirals upward on the whirlwind for ninety thousand *li*. With wings so vast, it is difficult to lift, so it must beat the whirlwind to ascend—only ninety thousand *li* suffice to sustain itself. With such wings, how could it suddenly rise and descend a few fathoms? All this is unavoidable, not a matter of delight. It departs on the six-month wind.”

The great bird departs for half a year before resting at the Celestial Pond; the small bird flies for half a morning before darting to the elm or sandalwood to alight. Though their capacities differ, in following their nature they are one. The wild horses of the air, the motes of dust—all living things are blown by one another’s breath. These are but what the Peng relies upon to fly.

The "wild horses" are wandering *qi*. Is the deep blue of the sky its true color? Or is it so distant that it has no end? From above, the Peng sees the earth as we see the sky—thus it halts and sets its course southward, for it knows not the distance in *li*, but only that it has strength enough to go. If water is not deep, it cannot bear a great boat. Pour a cup of water into a hollow in the floor, and a mustard seed becomes a boat; place a cup there, and it sticks fast—for the water is shallow and the boat too large. This makes clear why the Peng flies so high: its wings are vast. A small creature requires little, so a great one cannot make do with less. Thus there is a limit to principle, a fixed bound to things; each suffices for its task, and their fulfillment is one.

If one forgets the self and pursues life beyond what is fitting, undertaking what one’s strength cannot bear and acting against one’s nature, then even wings spanning the sky cannot escape exhaustion, nor can a sudden flight avoid distress. If the wind is not deep, it cannot bear great wings. Only when the wind lies ninety thousand *li* below does it now ride the gale; only when it bears the weight of heaven and brushes aside all obstacles does it now set its course south. Yet this is not a love of height or longing for distance—without accumulated wind, there would be no passage.

This is the Peng’s freedom.

The cicada and the fledgling dove laugh at it, saying: *"We burst forth and fly, darting to the elm or sandalwood to rest, and if we fall short, we simply drop to the ground—why go ninety thousand *li* to the south?"* If one is content in one’s nature, then even the great Peng has no cause to value itself above the small bird, nor does the small bird envy the Celestial Pond—for its desires are already fulfilled. Though great and small differ, their freedom is one.

One who journeys to the near wilds returns in a day with a full belly; one who travels a hundred *li* must pound grain the night before; one who goes a thousand *li* must gather provisions for three months. The farther the destination, the more provisions are needed—just as the greater the wings, the deeper the *qi* must be. What do these two creatures know? (The two creatures refer to the Peng and the shrimp.) Comparing great to small equalizes their differing aims. Why do their aims differ? Not because they choose to, but because it is naturally so. It is natural—not by effort.

This is the essence of freedom. Small knowledge does not reach great knowledge; short years do not reach long years. Each thing has its nature, each nature its limit—like years and knowledge, how could mere striving attain them? From here to Liezi, examples of long and short years and knowledge are given, each true to its own measure, none sufficient to surpass the other. Only the person free of dependence, who forgets self and others, who obscures these differences—only such a one, whose freedom is without merit, can unite great and small. For if there were great and small, then even the Peng and the sparrow, the minister and the wind-rider, would be bound by things. In life and death, only the one without life or death is limitless; if there were life and death, then even the great *chun* tree and the mayfly, Pengzu and the morning mushroom, would be equal in brevity.

Thus, to wander without great or small is to be without end; to be lost in neither life nor death is to be without limit. But if freedom is bound to a direction, then even if released to roam, it will reach an end—for it is not yet free of dependence. How do we know this is so? The morning mushroom knows not the waxing and waning of the moon; the cricket knows not spring and autumn—these are short years. South of Chu there is the *Mingling* tree, for which five hundred years are a spring, five hundred years an autumn; in high antiquity there was the great *zhuang* tree, for which eight thousand years are a spring, eight thousand years an autumn—these are long years. Yet Pengzu alone is famed for his longevity; for common people to compare themselves to him is truly pitiable! The disparity in years and knowledge is so vast—compared to what common people find lamentable, this is even more lamentable. Yet people do not grieve this, for each nature has its limit. If one knows this limit, then even the slightest difference cannot be bridged—what in the world is there to grieve?

Things do not long for what is small because of its smallness, nor must the small envy the great. Thus, the difference between great and small has its fixed measure, beyond the reach of envy or desire—so the burden of envy and desire can be cut off. Grief arises from burden; when burden is gone, grief departs. When grief departs, life and nature are at peace—this has never been otherwise.

This is what Tang asked Ji about. Tang’s question to Ji also spoke of each thing having its limit, and that following it brings harmony—thus Zhuangzi affirmed his question.

Beyond the barren north lies the Dark Sea, the Celestial Pond. There is a fish there, thousands of *li* wide, whose length is unknown—its name is Kun. There is a bird there, its name Peng, its back like Mount Tai, its wings like clouds hanging from the sky. It rides the whirlwind and the ram’s-horn gale to ascend ninety thousand *li*, piercing the clouds and bearing the blue sky, then sets its course south, bound for the Southern Darkness.

The marsh sparrow laughed at it, saying: "Where is it off to now? I leap and bound upward, but descend after only a few *ren*, soaring among the mugwort and wormwood—this is the perfection of flight. And where is *it* off to now?" This is the distinction between small and great. Each attains its nature as perfection and exhausts itself as the limit. Earlier, it was said that the two creatures differ, and so their destinations differ—one soars to the Celestial Pond, the other reaches only the elm and sandalwood—each is content with its form and finds sufficiency, unaware of why it is so. Now, speaking of the distinction between small and great, each has its natural endowment. This is not something attainable through striving or admiration. Each rests in its own nature, untroubled by their differences, and so the point is reiterated.

Thus, those whose knowledge suffices for one office, whose conduct matches one village, whose virtue aligns with one lord, or whose merit wins one state—such men regard themselves as this sparrow does, content within their own bounds, like a bird self-satisfied in its domain. Yet Song Rongzi would still laugh at them. Because he has not yet attained equality, he finds cause to laugh. Moreover, though the whole world praises him, he is not encouraged; though the whole world blames him, he is not disheartened. He has clearly settled the division between inner and outer, regarding himself as inner and all else as outer. He distinguishes the boundary between honor and disgrace, honoring himself and disgracing others. This is the limit. He cannot surpass it. Yet in his dealings with the world, he is not restless. He is sufficient unto himself, and so remains at ease in the world. Even so, he has not yet established the ultimate. He can affirm himself, but cannot be without something to affirm.

Liezi rode the wind and traveled, lightly and skillfully. After fifteen days, he returned. In his pursuit of good fortune, he was not restless. He naturally rode the wind, not seeking knowledge through restless effort. Though he avoided walking, he still depended on something. Without wind, he could not travel—this is necessarily dependence. Only one who accomplishes all without reliance is free from dependence.

As for one who rides the constancy of Heaven and Earth, and harnesses the transformations of the six *qi*, roaming the boundless—what does *he* depend on? Heaven and Earth are the collective names for the myriad things. Heaven and Earth take the myriad things as their body, and the myriad things must take spontaneity as their constancy. Spontaneity is that which is not done yet naturally is. Thus, the great Peng’s ability to ascend, the marsh sparrow’s ability to descend, the tree stump’s ability to grow, the morning mushroom’s ability to be short—all these are what spontaneity enables, not what effort enables. What is not done yet naturally is, this is constancy. Therefore, to ride the constancy of Heaven and Earth is to follow the nature of the myriad things; to harness the transformations of the six *qi* is to roam the paths of change. Proceeding thus, where is there an end? What is harnessed and ridden—what more is there to depend on? This is the carefree wandering of one of utmost virtue, who merges self and other in mystery.

If there is dependence, then even Liezi’s lightness and skill cannot enable him to travel without wind. He must obtain what he depends on before attaining carefree wandering—how much more so for the great Peng! Only one who merges with things and follows great change can be without dependence and always penetrate. Yet even those who depend on something, if they do not lose what they depend on, are equal to the great penetration. Thus, whether dependent or independent, there is nothing that cannot be equalized. When each rests in its nature, the heavenly mechanism unfolds of itself, receiving without awareness—then there is nothing that cannot be distinct. Yet even independence is insufficient to distinguish dependence, let alone the great and small of those who depend!

Thus it is said: The Perfect Man has no self; having no self, he follows things and so attains. The Spirit Man has no merit; things have never thanked spontaneity for their lives, yet must rely on acupuncture and stone—when principle reaches its limit, traces vanish. Now, following without assisting, one becomes one with the ultimate principle, and so has no merit. The Sage has no name. The Sage is merely the name for things attaining their nature—this is insufficient to name how they attain it.

Yao offered the world to Xu You, saying: "The sun and moon have risen, yet the torch still burns—is it not difficult for it to shine? The timely rain has fallen, yet irrigation continues—is it not laborious for it to moisten? If you stand forth, the world will be well-governed, yet I still occupy the throne. I see myself as lacking. Please take the world." Xu You replied: "You govern the world, and the world is already well-governed. One who can make the world well-governed does not govern the world. Thus, Yao governs by not governing, not by governing and thereby governing. Now, Xu You clearly sees that once governed, there is nothing to depend on, yet the governance actually comes from Yao. Thus, his words 'you govern' should be forgotten to seek what they imply. Yet some say that one who governs and thereby governs is Yao, while one who does not govern yet enables Yao to govern is Xu You—this is far from the truth. Governance arises from non-governance; action emerges from non-action. Taking from Yao is sufficient—why borrow from Xu You? If one claims that only by folding one’s arms in the mountains and forests can one be called non-active, this is why the teachings of Zhuangzi and Laozi are rejected by those in power. Those in power necessarily dwell in the realm of action and do not return—this is the reason. Yet if I were to replace you, would I do so for the sake of a name? A name is but the guest of reality."

Shall I then be but a guest? One who takes up the burden of self responds to things, while one who follows things moves with them without opposition. Thus, Yao had no counterpart in the world, while Xu You stood as an equal to Qi and Xie. How can this be so? One who merges with things cannot be separated from them. Therefore, with a mind devoid of intent, responses arise spontaneously, following only what is sensed—adrift like a boat untethered, neither east nor west being its own. Thus, wherever one goes, one shares all with the people, and wherever one turns, one becomes the lord of the world. To rule in this way is like the world elevating itself—such is the virtue of the lord. But if one stands alone, loftily atop a mountain’s peak, this is not the natural inclination of human feeling, for to cling to oneness is but a partial preference. How can one claim this exclusively? Such a one is but a single thing among the common, an outer minister to Yao. If an outer minister were to replace the inner lord, this would be to bear the name of lordship without its true responsibility.

The wren nests in the deep forest, yet needs no more than a single branch; the mole drinks from the river, yet takes no more than its fill. Each nature has its limit—if that limit is satisfied, then the rest of the world’s wealth is superfluous. Return to your repose, my lord—I have no use for the world! If you have no use for it, why does Yao alone possess it? Truly, one who embraces openness has no fixed direction, and so the world delights in elevating him without weariness.

Though the cook does not prepare the meal, the invocator does not step beyond the altar to take his place. Cook and invocator each abide in their proper office; birds and beasts, the myriad things, each find sufficiency in what they receive; Yao and Xu You each rest in their allotted place—this is the ultimate reality of the world. Each attains their reality—what more is there to do? It is enough to attain it oneself.

Thus, though the paths of Yao and Xu You differ, in their freedom they are one. Jian Wu asked Lian Shu: *"I heard Jie Yu’s words—they were vast yet without measure, sweeping away without return. I was startled and terrified by them, like the Milky Way stretching endlessly. They were so extreme, so far from human feeling!"* Lian Shu said: *"What did he say?"* Jian Wu replied: *"On the distant Guye Mountain dwells a spirit-person, their skin like ice and snow, their grace like a maiden’s. These are but borrowed words. The spirit-person is what we now call the sage. Though the sage may sit in the ancestral hall, their heart is no different from one in the mountains and forests—how could the world recognize this? They see only the yellow canopy and jade seal and think these enough to bind their heart; they see them traversing mountains and rivers, sharing in the people’s affairs, and think these enough to weary their spirit. How could they know that the most perfect remains unharmed?

Now, to speak of one of royal virtue and lodge them on this mountain is to show that the world has no way to recognize them. Thus, they are placed beyond the farthest reaches, beyond the bounds of sight and hearing. The maiden does not let the outer harm the inner. They eat no grains, drinking the wind and dew. Though all eat grains, the spirit-person alone is so—this makes clear that the spirit-person is not formed by grains but is endowed with the wondrous qi of nature. They ride the clouds, mount flying dragons, and wander beyond the four seas. Their spirit is concentrated, so that things suffer no blight and the harvests ripen. Yet I deem this mad and do not believe it!"*

One whose body embodies spirit and dwells in the numinous, who exhausts principle and reaches the subtlest mysteries—though they sit in stillness within the quiet hall, their mystery pervades beyond the four seas. Thus, they ride the Two Modes and harness the Six Abandonments, moving among the multitudes yet driving the ten thousand things. If nothing is not compliant, then floating clouds become their mount; if no form is not borne, then flying dragons become their steed. They cast off the self and attain spontaneity, though seemingly indifferent, they need nothing. Sitting in forgetfulness, moving in forgetfulness, forgetting even the act of forgetting—thus their movement is like dragging withered wood, their stillness like a heap of dead ashes. This is what is meant by their spirit being concentrated. When their spirit is concentrated, what is not concentrated attains spontaneity.

The world judges by what it sees and draws conclusions—how could it ever believe this?

Lián Shū said: "Indeed. The blind cannot partake in the spectacle of patterns, the deaf cannot partake in the sound of bells and drums. Is it only the body that suffers blindness and deafness? Knowledge, too, has its own. To fail to reach the ultimate subtlety of perfect speech and dismiss it as madness unworthy of belief—this is the deafness and blindness of knowledge. Such words are but the prattle of a child. To say that what Jiē Yú spoke of is naturally sought by things, and that those who are deaf and blind to knowledge deny its possibility—this man, this power, would rather merge with the ten thousand things as one, seeking to bring order to a disordered world. Who would wear themselves out taking the world as their concern?

The heart of the sage reaches the ultimate convergence of the Two Modes and exhausts the subtle numbers of the ten thousand things. Thus, they embody transformation and harmonize with change. There is nowhere they cannot go, pervading the ten thousand things so that nothing is not so. The world, in its chaos, seeks me, but I have no mind. If I have no mind, why should I not respond to the world? Yet one who embodies the mysterious and exhausts the subtlest—it is by not acting that they harmonize with the natures of the ten thousand things, shaping the transformations of the world, and so attain the fame of Yáo and Shùn. Who would wear themselves out with toil and anxious thought, taking affairs as their affair, and only then succeed?

This person, no thing harms them. To be at ease with what harms is to render harm powerless; when harm is powerless, things do not harm them. Great floods may rise to the heavens without drowning them; great droughts may melt metal and stone, scorch earth and mountains without heating them. Wherever they go, they are at ease—every place is fitting. Life and death bring no change to them, much less drowning or scorching! Thus, the perfected person is not entangled by calamity and disaster—not by avoiding it, but by following principle straightforwardly, naturally meeting with fortune. Their dust and chaff, the dregs they discard, are what would mold Yáo and Shùn. Who would take things as their concern?

Yáo and Shùn are but names for worldly affairs; those who act for names are not true names. Thus, Yáo and Shùn—were they merely Yáo and Shùn? They must have possessed the reality of the spirit-person. What the world calls Yáo and Shùn is merely the dust and chaff of their names. The people of Sòng carried ceremonial caps to Yuè, but the Yuè people, with shorn hair and tattooed bodies, had no use for them. Yáo governed the people of the world and brought order to the realm, yet when he went to see the Four Masters on the distant slopes of Miǎo Gū Shé Mountain, north of the Fén River, he was suddenly bereft of his world. Yáo’s uselessness to the world was like the Yuè people’s uselessness to the ceremonial cap. Yet one who abandons the world is truly revered by the world. Though the world revered Yáo, Yáo never possessed the world. Thus, he was suddenly bereft of it, his mind wandering in the farthest reaches of the obscure. Though he sat as if enthroned above the ten thousand things, he had never ceased to be carefree.

The Four Masters are but a lodging for words, to show that Yáo was not confined to being Yáo. In reality, Yáo was obscure; his traces were but Yáo. To view the obscure from the traces is to see inner and outer as separate realms—no wonder it seems strange! The world sees only Yáo as Yáo—how could it recognize his obscurity?"

Huìzǐ said to Zhuāngzǐ: "The King of Wèi gave me seeds of a great gourd. I planted them, and they grew to hold five *dàn*. When I used it to hold water, its strength could not support itself. When I split it to make a ladle, its size was too vast to hold anything. It was not that it wasn’t resoundingly large—I smashed it because it was useless."

Zhuāngzǐ said: "You are truly inept at using what is great. There was a man of Sòng skilled in making a salve that prevented chapped hands. For generations, his family made their living by washing silk floss. A traveler heard of it and offered a hundred gold pieces for the formula. The family gathered and deliberated: 'For generations, we’ve washed silk floss and earned only a few coins. Now, in one morning, we can sell this skill for a hundred gold pieces—let’s give it to him.' The traveler obtained it and persuaded the King of Wú. When Yuè raised a rebellion, the King of Wú sent him as a general. In winter, he fought a naval battle with the Yuè and crushed them, gaining land and a fief. The ability to prevent chapped hands was the same, yet one used it to gain a fief, while another could not escape washing silk floss—the difference lay in how it was used. Now you have a gourd of five *dàn*—why not consider making a great float and drifting upon rivers and lakes, instead of worrying that its size is too vast to hold anything? You still have the heart of a clump of mugwort!"

Mugwort is not the path of one who is truly penetrating. This passage speaks of how things each have their proper use. If one finds their proper use, where could one not be carefree?

Huìzǐ said to Zhuāngzǐ: "I have a great tree—people call it the *chū*. Its great trunk is swollen and gnarled, not fitting the inked line; its small branches are twisted, not fitting the compass or square. It stands by the road, but carpenters do not glance at it. Now your words are vast but useless—just what everyone rejects."

Zhuāngzǐ said: "Have you never seen a wildcat or weasel? They crouch low to lie in wait, pouncing on their prey. They leap east and west, not avoiding high or low..."

They are caught in traps and snares and die in nets. Now consider the yak: its size is like clouds trailing across the sky. This creature is great indeed, yet it cannot catch mice. You have a great tree and fret that it is useless—why not plant it in the land of Nothing Whatever, the vast and boundless wilds? Wander idly at its side, roam carefree beneath its shade. No axe will fell it prematurely, no thing will harm it. Useless? Where then could it be troubled?"

For things great and small, if they miss their proper measure, the principle of benefit and harm is the same. When each finds its proper use, all things are carefree.

---

In *The Inner Chapters: "On the Equality of Things,"* Master Nanguo Ziqi sat leaning against his armrest, gazing at the sky and exhaling deeply, his response seeming as if he had lost his counterpart. Yan Cheng Ziyou stood in attendance before him and asked, "What is this? Can the body truly be made like withered wood, and the heart like dead ashes? Yet the one who leans on the armrest today is not the same as the one who leaned on it before?"

All things deem themselves right and others wrong, praise themselves and despise others—none are exempt. However, though right and wrong differ, self and other are equal. When heaven and humanity are united, self and other are equal, then externally there is nothing with which to rejoice, and in response, the body dissolves as if it has lost its match. Dead ashes and withered wood are valued for their stillness and lack of emotion. Those who follow their natural course and forget right and wrong embody only their innate nature—what else could there be? Thus, when still, they stand like a withered tree; when moving, they sway like a dry branch. Sitting, they are like dead ashes; walking, like drifting dust. The forms of movement and stillness are not what I can unify; their state of effortless self-possession is not what I can duplicate.

Ziyou had seen those who leaned on armrests before, but none like Ziqi. "I have lost my 'I,'" he said, "and in forgetting myself, what in the world is there left to recognize?" Only by forgetting both inner and outer can one transcend and attain all.

Ziqi said, "Yan, how excellent that you ask! Just now, I lost my 'I'—did you realize it? You have heard the *human pipes* but not the *earth pipes*; you have heard the *earth pipes* but not the *heavenly pipes*!"

*Pipes* refers to flutes. Flutes vary in length, their notes differing in pitch, producing myriad sounds—some short, some long, some high, some low. Though the sounds are myriad, the measure they receive is one; thus, there is no place to interpose judgments of superiority or inferiority. How much more so with the winds and things of the world: different sounds share the same nature, each taking its own course, and so the *pipes of heaven and earth* are revealed.

Ziyou said, "May I ask the method?"

Ziqi replied, "The great clod breathes forth *qi*, and its name is wind. It does nothing, yet when it arises, the myriad hollows roar in anger. Have you alone not heard its long sighs? The *great clod* is nothingness itself. As for the breathing forth of *qi*, how could there be a thing that does it? The *qi* simply breathes forth of itself. When things are born, none fail to arise of themselves—thus the body of this self-arising is vast, and so it is named the *great clod*.

The towering forests and jagged cliffs, the great trees with hollows as wide as a man’s embrace—some resemble noses, some mouths, some ears, some pegs, some rings, some mortars, some depressions, some pools. Some hiss, some whistle, some scold, some inhale, some cry, some wail, some moan, some shriek. Those ahead sing *yu*, and those behind sing *yong*. A gentle breeze elicits a small harmony; a whirlwind, a great one. When a fierce gale subsides, all hollows fall silent. Have you alone not seen them swaying—*tiaotiao*, *diaodiao*?"

The wind does nothing, yet when it arises, the myriad hollows all rage and produce sound. The long sighs are the sounds of a great wind. The stirring of a mighty wind. Here, the various hollows are briefly likened to different things. Here, the diverse sounds of the myriad hollows are briefly enumerated. Though the notes of the sounds vary endlessly—singing and responding, great and small—none fail to match what they receive and each fits its place. *Subsides* means ceases. When a fierce wind blows, the hollows are full; when it ceases, they are empty. Though full and empty differ, in each attaining its proper state, they are the same. *Tiaotiao* and *diaodiao* describe the swaying of things. Though the sounds differ, so too do the movements of their forms. Yet though their movements differ, in attaining equality, they are one—how could *tiaotiao* alone be right and *diaodiao* alone be wrong?

Ziyou said, "The *earth pipes* are simply the myriad hollows; the *human pipes* are the various bamboo flutes. May I ask about the *heavenly pipes*?"

Ziqi said, "The *heavenly pipes* blow through the myriad differences, yet each follows its own course. All take what is their own—who is it that stirs them to anger?"

These are the *heavenly pipes*. For the *heavenly pipes*, is there yet another thing apart? They are simply the hollows, bamboo flutes, and all that joins with living things, converging to form a single heaven. That which is *wu* (nothing) cannot generate *you* (being); that which is *you* before it is born cannot be the source of life."

Then who is it that gives life to life? It arises of itself, a clod that lives by itself. It lives by itself—it is not I who give it life. Since I cannot give life to things, and things cannot give life to me, then I am self-so. Being self-so, it is called "heavenly-so." Heavenly-so is not made; thus it is spoken of as "heaven." Speaking of it as "heaven" clarifies its self-so nature—how could it mean the azure vault? Yet some say the *heavenly pipes* compel things to follow them. But heaven itself cannot possess itself—how could it possess things? Thus "heaven" is simply the collective name for the myriad things. None is uniquely heaven—who then masters and compels things? Therefore, things each live of themselves, with no source from which they emerge—this is the Dao of heaven. All things attain themselves—who stirs them to anger, making them so? This further clarifies the *heavenly pipes*.

The great knowledge is leisurely, the small knowledge is leisurely. The great words blaze, the small words chatter. In sleep, their spirits intermingle; in waking, their forms open. They engage and contend, daily waging war in their hearts. Some are vague, some are guarded, some are secretive. Small fears make them tremble; great fears make them numb. Their outbursts are like the release of a crossbow—this is what it means to judge right and wrong. Their restraint is like a sworn oath—this is what it means to cling to victory. Their decline is like autumn and winter, speaking of their daily decay. What they drown in cannot be restored. Their weariness is like a sealed letter, speaking of their aging and stagnation. The heart near death cannot be revived. Joy and anger, sorrow and delight, worry and sighing, change and fear, frivolity and ease, seduction and posturing—pleasure emerges from emptiness, steam gives rise to mushrooms. Day and night they alternate before us, yet none knows where they sprout. Enough, enough! Morning and evening we attain this—is this not how we come to be? This is the difference in knowledge. This is the difference in speech. This is the difference between waking and sleeping. This is the difference in interaction. This is the difference in fear and trembling. This is the difference in movement and stillness. Their decay and daily decay are like this. Their drowning and irreversible course are like this. Their weariness, sunk in desire, aging and growing ever more stagnant, are like this. Their pursuit of profit, fear of harm, secret scheming to achieve their aims, are like this. These are the differences in nature and disposition. These are the differences in events and changes.

From this point onward, we briefly enumerate the boundlessness of the *heavenly pipes*; from this point downward, we clarify the self-so nature of boundlessness. Things each are self-so, not knowing why they are so yet being so—thus though their forms differ endlessly, their *so-ness* is endlessly the same. Day and night alternate, the old giving way to the new. Heaven and earth, the myriad things, transform and daily renew, advancing with time—what gives rise to them? They are simply self-so. This speaks of their self-generation. Without that, there is no "I"; without "I," there is nothing to take. This is close, yet one does not know what makes it so. As if there were a true master, yet no trace of it can be found. It can be acted upon and trusted, yet its form is unseen—it has essence but no form. That is self-so. Self-so gives birth to me; I give birth to self-so. Thus self-so is simply my own self-so—how could it be distant? All things, in their multiplicity, are simply thus of themselves, not made to be so by one another. Therefore, leaving them to themselves, principle arrives of itself.

The myriad things, the myriad dispositions, differ in their inclinations and aversions—it is as if there were a true master making them so. Yet when one seeks the traces of this true master, one never finds them. Thus it is clear that all things are self-so, with nothing making them so. Now consider a traveler: he trusts that he can walk, yet does not see the form that enables him to walk. His disposition matches the thing, so the form is not separately visible. The face, the nine orifices, the six viscera—all are complete and present. With whom should I be intimate? Do you delight in all of them? Or do you have favorites? Are they all like servants and concubines? Are servants and concubines insufficient to govern one another? Do they take turns being lord and subject? Or is there a true lord present? Whether one seeks and attains its essence or not, it neither adds to nor detracts from its truth. Entrust it to self-so, and all things are preserved. They simply exist of themselves. If one delighted in all, that would imply partiality. With partiality, one cannot preserve all things completely. Thus they exist without delight, live without being made. If one were partial to all, ambition would exceed one’s proper role, superiors and inferiors would encroach upon one another, and none would be servants or concubines. If those with the talents of servants and concubines do not accept the tasks of servants and concubines, they are lost.

Thus the principle of lord and subject, superior and inferior, inner and outer, hands and feet, is the natural pattern of heaven—how could it be the doing of a true person? Servants and concubines simply each fulfill their proper role—this is not insufficient for mutual governance. Mutual governance is like hands, feet, ears, and eyes, the four limbs and hundred parts, each with its office and all mutually serving one another. Those whom the times deem worthy become lords; those whose talents do not match the age become subjects. Like heaven’s natural height and earth’s natural lowness, the head’s natural position above and the feet’s natural position below—is there any alternation? Though there is no error in their proper places, they are necessarily proper of themselves. Leaving them to themselves, they are thus of themselves—this is not artificial.

All who attain their true nature and employ their self-so nature, though they be mere servants, still do not heed slander or praise but rest content in their work. Thus whether one knows or does not know, all are self-so. But if one opens the path of vain hopes, with inferiors encroaching upon superiors, things lose their truth, people forget their roots—then between slander and praise, one’s bearing is lost and confused. Once one receives this completed form, one does not perish until its end. Locked in mutual struggle and wear, one’s actions rush forward like a gallop, yet nothing can stop them—is this not lamentable? Toiling one’s whole life without seeing success, wearily exhausted yet not knowing where one is headed—how can this not be sorrowful?

If people call this "not dying," what benefit is there? The form dissolves, and the heart follows it—can this not be called great sorrow? Is human life inherently so deluded? Am I alone deluded, or are there others who are not?

Each nature has its allotted portion, so the wise guard their knowledge to the end, while the foolish cling to their ignorance until death—who can alter their nature midway? The myriad beings swirl in confusion, clashing in opposition and accord, each trusting their own biases and indulging their impulses, none able to turn back. This—what the common people deem sorrowful—is indeed sorrowful. Yet the common people never regard it as sorrowful, for it is the nature of their nature. Since all things are so by nature, what is there to lament?

Human desires know no bounds, and those who know contentment are few. Thus, having attained this, they do not stop but pursue that instead. All toil their entire lives, never sating their ambitions, ceasing only in death. Therefore, their success is never seen. All things wear out their bodies in pursuit of what they love, until they are exhausted and spent. Yet they do not know where this love leads! In truth, it is no different from death. When heart and form race together, weary yet unable to turn back, this is a sorrow greater than what common people grieve. But since common people never grieve over this, what they grieve is not worth grieving.

All the matters above occur without knowing why they occur—hence, they are deluded. Those who do not know do not know how they know, yet they know of themselves. The living do not know how they live, yet they live of themselves. Though the myriad things differ, in that their living does not arise from knowing, there is no difference—thus, none in the world are not deluded.

If one follows the completed heart and takes it as one’s teacher, who is without a teacher? Why must there be those who know the alternation and yet choose for themselves? Even the foolish do so! To have right and wrong before the heart is complete is like arriving in Yue today when you set out yesterday. Thus, to take what is not as what is—even the divine Yu could not comprehend it, so how can I?

The heart sufficient to govern the body’s use is called the "completed heart." If one takes one’s completed heart as one’s teacher, then each person has their own teacher. Each person having their own teacher, they entrust themselves to it and it fits. To use the completed to replace the incomplete is not knowledge—it is the heart’s self-attainment. Thus, even the foolish take their completed heart as their teacher, unwilling to employ what they deem short and abandon what they deem long.

If you arrive in Yue today, how could you have arrived yesterday? Before the heart is complete, how could right and wrong arise? Those who clarify right and wrong cannot be without them, so the perfected person follows both. In principle, there is no right or wrong, yet the deluded take it as existing—this is taking what is not as what is. Once the deluded heart is formed, even sages cannot dispel it, so one entrusts it to its natural state without forcing knowledge.

Words are not the wind’s blowing; speakers have something to say. Yet what they say is not yet settled. Is there truly speech? Or has there never been speech? Do they differ from the chirping of fledglings, or is there distinction? Or is there no distinction? Each has something to say, so they differ from the wind. I deem this right, and they deem it wrong; what they deem right, I deem wrong—so it is not settled. Not settled because of the bias of their perspectives.

Do we take it as speech? Yet it is insufficient to settle anything. Do we take it as no speech? But one already speaks from one’s own position. Speech and the chirping of fledglings reach the same end—whether there is distinction or not is truly unsettled. The world’s sentiments need not be the same, yet their words cannot differ, so right and wrong swirl in confusion, with no way to settle them.

Where does the Dao hide to have truth and falsity? Where do words hide to have right and wrong? Where does the Dao go and not remain? Where do words remain and yet cannot? The Dao is hidden in petty achievements, words are hidden in florid embellishment. Thus arise the right and wrong of the Confucians and Mohists, each affirming what the other negates and negating what the other affirms. To affirm what they negate and negate what they affirm, nothing is better than illumination.

The Dao is nowhere absent! Why is it hidden to have truth and falsity, and why do the names of right and wrong arise in profusion? All remain. All are permissible. Petty achievements and florid embellishment naturally obscure the Dao, yet the Dao cannot be obscured. Thus, truth and falsity, right and wrong, operate in florid embellishment and cease in proper alignment, appear in petty achievements and vanish in great wholeness.

Confucians and Mohists mutually affirm and negate, yet all in the world are Confucians and Mohists. Thus, the hundred schools arise, each prizing their own views, never stepping beyond their bounds. Those who have right and wrong are what the Confucians and Mohists affirm; those who have no right and no wrong are what they negate. Now, to affirm what the Confucians and Mohists negate and negate what they affirm is to illuminate the absence of right and wrong. To illuminate the absence of right and wrong, nothing is better than using the Confucians and Mohists to mutually clarify each other. Mutually clarifying each other, what they affirm is not right, and what they negate is not wrong. Not wrong, then there is no wrong; not right, then there is no right.

Nothing is not "that," nothing is not "this." From "that," one does not see; from self-knowledge, one knows. Thus it is said: "That emerges from this, and this also depends on that." This is the theory of "that" and "this" arising together. Yet even as they arise, they die; even as they die, they arise. Even as they are permissible, they are not; even as they are not, they are permissible. Following right, following wrong; following wrong, following right. Therefore, the sage does not proceed thus but illuminates it by the heavens—this too is following "this." All things deem themselves right, so nothing is not right.

All things deem each other "that," thus nothing is not "that." Nothing is not "that," then under heaven there is no "this"; nothing is not "this," then under heaven there is no "that." Without "that" and without "this," this is the profound unity.

The partiality of things lies in that none see what the other sees, but only know what they themselves know. Knowing what they themselves know, they deem themselves right. Deeming themselves right, they regard the other as wrong. Thus it is said: "That emerges from this, and this also depends on that"—"that" and "this" arise through mutual dependence.

The changes of life and death are like the progression of the four seasons—spring, autumn, winter, and summer. Though the states of life and death differ, in each finding peace in what they encounter, they are one. Now, the living deem life as life, while the dead deem life as death—thus there is no life. The living deem death as death, while the dead deem death as life—thus there is no death. Without life and without death, without right and without wrong, the debates of Confucians and Mohists are what I cannot align with; yet when each obscures their own distinctions, these are what I cannot oppose.

The one who embraces openness follows the right and wrong of the world yet remains without right or wrong. Thus, not proceeding by the path of right and wrong yet ensuring no harm from right or wrong is simply to illuminate their natural state without deviation. This is also "that," and "that" is also "this." "That" has its own right and wrong, and "this" has its own right and wrong—is there truly a "that" and "this"? Or is there truly no "that" and "this"? Where "that" and "this" find no counterpart, this is called the *pivot of the Dao*. When the pivot is found at the center of the ring, it responds to the infinite. "This" is one infinity, and "not-this" is another infinity. Thus it is said: nothing compares to illumination.

I, too, am deemed "that" by "that." "That," too, deems itself right. "This" deems itself right and "that" wrong; "that" deems itself right and "this" wrong—each contains within itself one right and one wrong. Now, if one wishes to call "that" "that," yet "that" still deems itself right; if one wishes to call "this" "this," yet "this" is still deemed "that" by "that." Thus, the presence or absence of "that" and "this" remains undetermined.

A *counterpart* is an opposition. "That" and "this" oppose each other, yet the sage harmonizes both. Thus, the one without intent merges with things and has never set up oppositions under heaven. The *pivot* is the essential point. Abiding in this essential point and meeting the profound extremity, one responds to the boundless. Right and wrong alternate endlessly, following one another without cease—thus they are called a *ring*. The center of the ring is empty; now, if one takes right and wrong as the ring and attains its center, there is no right and no wrong. Without right and without wrong, one can respond to right and wrong. Since right and wrong are infinite, the response is also infinite.

Under heaven, none do not deem themselves right and none do not deem others wrong. Thus, one right and one wrong proceed endlessly. Only the one who traverses emptiness and attains the center, vast and without attachment, rides upon it in wandering. To use a finger to illustrate that a finger is not a finger is not as good as using a non-finger to illustrate that a finger is not a finger; to use a horse to illustrate that a horse is not a horse is not as good as using a non-horse to illustrate that a horse is not a horse. Heaven and earth are one finger; the myriad things are one horse.

For one to deem oneself right and others wrong is the common sentiment of "that" and "this." Thus, if I use my finger to illustrate "that" finger, then "that" finger alone becomes a non-finger to my finger—this is using a finger to illustrate that a finger is not a finger. If, in turn, "that" finger is used to illustrate my finger, then my finger becomes a non-finger to "that" finger—this is using a non-finger to illustrate that a finger is not a finger. To clarify the absence of right and wrong, nothing compares to reciprocal illustration. Through reciprocal illustration, "that" and "I" are alike in deeming ourselves right and equal in deeming each other wrong. Equal in deeming each other wrong, then under heaven there is no right; alike in deeming ourselves right, then under heaven there is no wrong.

How can this be made clear? If "this" were truly right, then under heaven there would be no one to deem it wrong. If "not-this" were truly wrong, then under heaven there would also be no one to deem it right. Now, with right and wrong lacking a master, they are confused and chaotic—it is clear that these petty distinctions merely reflect each one’s biased views converging in uniformity. Looking up and looking down, none are not thus. Therefore, the perfected person knows that heaven and earth are one finger and the myriad things are one horse. Thus, vast and greatly at peace, heaven and earth and the myriad things each rest in their proper place, united in self-contentment, without right and without wrong.

Is it permissible? Is it not permissible? What is permissible to oneself is called permissible; what is not permissible to oneself is called not permissible. The Dao is completed through its practice; things are deemed so through their naming. There is that by which it is permissible, that by which it is not permissible, that by which it is so, and that by which it is not so. How is it so? It is so in being so. How is it not so? It is not so in not being so. How is it permissible? It is permissible in being permissible. How is it not permissible? It is not permissible in not being permissible.

Things inherently have that by which they are so; things inherently have that by which they are permissible. There is nothing that is not so; there is nothing that is not permissible. Thus, take a stalk and a pillar, an ugly woman and Xi Shi, the expansive, the strange, the deceitful, and the bizarre—all are united as one. In their division, they are completed; in their completion, they are destroyed. All things are without completion and destruction, united as one. There is nothing that is not completed; there is nothing that is not so. Each is so in its own way; each is permissible in its own way.

A stalk lies horizontally while a pillar stands vertically; an ugly woman is repulsive while Xi Shi is beautiful. What is meant by "equalizing"? Surely it is not to equalize forms or unify standards! Thus, take the vertical and horizontal, the beautiful and ugly, the expansive and bizarre—each is so in its own way, each is permissible in its own way. Though principles may differ in ten thousand ways, their nature is equally attained. Thus it is said: the Dao unites as one.

Things may be deemed dissolution by one and completion by another. What I call completion, another may call destruction. Completion and destruction arise from self-regard, blind to the other. Thus, there is no completion or destruction, just as there is no right or wrong. Only the enlightened know that all is united as one, and though they do not employ this knowledge, they lodge it in the ordinary. Following what is so, they rest in it without knowing why—this is called the Dao.

The enlightened are not confined to one perspective; suddenly, they forget themselves and entrust themselves to spontaneous use. In spontaneous use, all things flow smoothly and find their own fulfillment. "Few" means "exhaustion." The ultimate principle is exhausted in self-fulfillment. The enlightened follow without contrivance. Do they know that following is good and thus follow? They do not know why they follow—they simply follow, and so it is called the Dao.

To strain the mind in pursuit of unity while failing to recognize its sameness is called "morning threes." What is "morning threes"? The monkey keeper distributed chestnuts, saying, "Three in the morning and four in the evening." All the monkeys were enraged. He then said, "Very well, four in the morning and three in the evening." All the monkeys were pleased. The names and realities remained unchanged, yet joy and anger were put to use—this, too, is following what is so. Therefore, the sage harmonizes with right and wrong and rests in the celestial balance—this is called "walking two paths."

Does the enlightened one strain the mind to achieve unity? If one labors the mind to force unity, it is unreliable and no different from those who fail to achieve it. They are as deluded as the monkeys, clinging to what they prefer and deeming themselves right. Without partiality, one entrusts all to spontaneous equilibrium and leaves it at that. Entrusting the world’s right and wrong—

The ancients’ knowledge reached its limit. Where did it reach? Some held that things had never yet begun to exist—this is the utmost, the exhaustion, beyond which nothing can be added! Next were those who held that things existed but had never yet been bounded. Next were those who held that boundaries existed but right and wrong had never yet arisen. The manifestation of right and wrong is the diminishment of the Dao; the diminishment of the Dao is the formation of attachment. Is there truly completion and diminishment? Is there truly no completion and diminishment? With completion and diminishment, there is Zhao’s zither-playing; without completion and diminishment, there is Zhao’s not playing the zither. Zhao Wen’s zither-playing, Shi Kuang’s wielding the baton, and Hui Shi’s leaning on the catalpa—these three were nearly at the height of wisdom, and so their deeds are recorded at life’s end.

Yet their attachment to their pursuits set them apart from others. Their attachment sought to illuminate them, but what they sought to illuminate was not meant to be illuminated—thus they ended in the obscurity of "hard and white." And Zhao Wen’s son ended his life weaving the threads of his father’s art, achieving nothing. If this can be called achievement, then though I have achieved nothing, I too may be said to have achieved. If this cannot be called achievement, then neither things nor I have achieved anything.

Thus, the dazzling confusion of doubt is what the sage contemplates. Not employing this knowledge but lodging it in the ordinary—this is called "illuminating through obscurity." This is to forget heaven and earth, to leave the myriad things behind, to look outward without examining the cosmos, to look inward without perceiving one’s own body. Thus, one is vast and unburdened, moving with things and responding to all without hindrance.

Though one may not yet have forgotten all, one can still forget self and other. Though one may not yet have forgotten self and other, one can still forget their right and wrong. Without the Dao’s diminishment, emotions become partial and attachment takes form. Failing to forget attachment and relinquish self-interest, one cannot achieve mystical unity with self and other. This is not perfection.

Being and non-being—these one cannot know, even unto the end.

The myriad sounds cannot all be enumerated. Thus, when one blows pipes or plucks strings, though the fingers may move intricately, many sounds are still lost. Those who grasp the flute or strike the zither seek to manifest sound, yet in manifesting sound, sound is lost; in not manifesting sound, sound remains whole. Thus, to seek completion and thereby diminish it—this is how Zhaowen plays the zither. Not to seek completion and thus remain undiminished—this is how Zhaowen does not play the zither. "Nearly" means "exhaustively." These three masters all sought to clarify what they themselves did not understand, thus exhausting their knowledge and depleting their thoughts, their bodies wearied and their spirits fatigued. Some leaned on their staffs and dozed, others rested against a catalpa tree and closed their eyes. They relied on their vigor to endure, for without it, they would have succumbed long ago.

These three masters each cherished only what they themselves understood, believing themselves distinct from the common crowd. They sought to display their understanding to others, desiring that all should share their preferences. This is like playing the reed organ to an ox. Since others ultimately do not understand, their own arts and methods end in obscurity. Zhaowen’s son, in turn, carried on his father’s legacy, yet in the end achieved nothing. Though these three sought to enlighten others, those others ultimately did not understand, and so they accomplished nothing in their lifetimes. If these three can be called accomplished, then even my own lack of accomplishment could be called accomplishment. All things illuminate themselves yet do not illuminate others. If, because others are not illuminated, one deems them unaccomplished, then all things would be without accomplishment.

Thus, the sage does not display this to dazzle that, nor abandons the self to pursue things. Following along, they allow each to fulfill its capacity, and so all things achieve completion without omission. Now, these three masters sought to display their own preferences to others—is this not delusion? The sage is without self. Thus, when faced with the dazzling confusion of doubt, they chart and delimit it; when confronted with vast deception and strangeness, they unify and harmonize it. They allow the myriad differences to rest in what they find restful, so that the multitude do not lose what they deem right. Thus, the sage is not employed by things, and the use of all things is fulfilled. When all things employ themselves, who is right and who is wrong? Therefore, even in the face of unbridled transformations and strange deviations, bending to follow them and entrusting them to their own use, though their uses may differ in ten thousand ways, all remains clearly self-evident.

Now, suppose I were to speak here—how would I know if it is of the same kind as this? Or not of the same kind? Whether of the same kind or not, all are of the same kind when taken together, and thus no different from that. Nevertheless, let me attempt to speak: There is a beginning; there is that which precedes the beginning; there is that which precedes what precedes the beginning. There is being; there is non-being; there is that which precedes non-being; there is that which precedes what precedes non-being. Suddenly, there is being and non-being, yet one does not know which of the two—being or non-being—is truly so. Now, I have already spoken, yet I do not know if what I have called "spoken" is truly spoken or truly unspoken.

If I say there is no right or wrong, how would I know if this is of the same kind as those who speak of right and wrong or not? If I wish to call it the same kind, then I take non-being as right, while they take non-being as wrong—thus, it is not the same kind. Yet though this right and wrong differ, they are still not free from right and wrong, and so are of the same kind as that. Therefore, whether of the same kind or not, all are of the same kind when taken together, and thus no different from that. If one wishes to avoid such great dissimilarity, nothing is better than having no mind. Having dispelled right and wrong, one must then dispel the act of dispelling. Dispelling the dispelling again and again until there is no dispelling left—only then will there be no dispelling and nothing not dispelled, and right and wrong will depart of themselves. The ultimate principle is without words; to speak is to fall into classification. Thus, I tentatively entrust these words to this purpose.

If there is a beginning, there is an end. To speak of no end or beginning and to equate life and death—those who unify them do not compare to those who do not unify them and yet find them equal of themselves. This is to forget their unity. If there is being, beauty and ugliness, right and wrong are all present. If there is non-being yet one does not know that there is no non-being, then right and wrong, love and hate still linger in the heart. Even knowing there is no non-being, one may still not be without knowledge. To forget all knowledge entirely—only then does one suddenly begin to comprehend nothingness. In comprehending nothingness, heaven and earth, the myriad things, self and other, right and wrong—all become clearly and firmly so.

To say there is no right or wrong is still to make a statement. Not knowing whether this statement is being or non-being, one then becomes utterly free of the slightest speck of dust in the heart. Nothing in the world is greater than the tip of an autumn hair, yet Mount Tai is small; nothing lives longer than a child who dies young, yet Pengzu is short-lived. Heaven and earth are born together with me, and the myriad things and I are one. Since we are already one, can there be speech? Since we have already called it one, can there be no speech? One and speech make two; two and one make three. Proceeding from this, even the most skilled mathematician cannot arrive at a number, much less an ordinary person! Therefore, to go from non-being to being, reaching three—how much more so to go from being to being! Do not proceed thus; follow what is already so!

If one opposes things by form, then Mount Tai is greater than the tip of an autumn hair. But if each abides by its own nature and things reach their ultimate, then great form is not in excess, and small form is not insufficient. If each is sufficient in its nature, then the tip of an autumn hair is not merely small in its smallness, nor is Mount Tai merely great in its greatness. If sufficiency in nature is deemed greatness, then nothing in the world is greater than the tip of an autumn hair. If sufficiency in nature is not deemed greatness, then even Mount Tai can be called small. Thus, it is said: Nothing in the world is greater than the tip of an autumn hair, yet Mount Tai is small. If Mount Tai is small, then nothing in the world is great; if the tip of an autumn hair is great, then nothing in the world is small. Without small or great, without long life or early death, the cicada does not envy the great *chun* tree and rejoices in its own contentment; the marsh pheasant does not prize the celestial pond and finds glory in its own sufficiency.

If one is content with Heaven’s natural endowment and at peace with one’s nature and destiny, then even heaven and earth are not long-lived enough to outlast me, for they arise together with me; the myriad things are not distinct enough to differ from me, for they attain together with me. If heaven and earth arise together with me, why should they not be as one? If the myriad things attain together with me, why should they not be identical? The myriad things in their myriad forms all find sufficiency in themselves, and their sufficiency is one. Once one has attained this oneness, there is no principle left to articulate.

Names and terms arise from a lack of clarity. Some things cannot clarify their own oneness and thus pursue others through comparison, so they coin a term to correct them. Once they have named this "oneness," they have already introduced speech. Yet to speak of oneness with words is to divide it, for oneness is not speech; thus, oneness and speech become two. Oneness is one, yet speech divides it again. With one and two, can we not call it three? To express oneness with a single word still yields three—how much more so when tracing its tributaries, where every thing has its own name? Even with skilled calculation, none can enumerate them.

Thus, those who name oneness do not differ from others, while those who forget oneness attain it without speech. Each abides by what it can do—this is the truest way. The Dao has never had boundaries, nor has speech ever had constancy; yet for the sake of this, distinctions arise. Let me speak of these distinctions: there is left and right, hierarchy and duty, division and debate, competition and contention—these are called the eight virtues.

Beyond the six directions, the sage acknowledges but does not discourse; within the six directions, the sage discourses but does not debate; regarding the *Spring and Autumn Annals* and the aspirations of former kings, the sage debates but does not contend. Thus, division implies what is undivided; debate implies what is undebated.

One may ask: "Why is this?" The sage embraces it, while the multitude debates to display themselves. Thus, it is said: debate arises from what is unseen. In profound obscurity, nothing is absent. When "this" and "that" are spoken, right and wrong have no fixed ground. The Dao is unbounded, so the myriad things freely occupy their allotted domains, each differing in what suits them. Every thing has its principle, every affair its fittingness. They are grouped by kind and distinguished by category. Pursuing side by side is called competition; opposing debate is called contention. Briefly distinguishing them, we have these eight virtues.

What lies beyond the six directions refers to what is outside the inherent nature of things. Though principle exists even there, since it lies beyond the bounds of inherent nature, it does not move the sage, who therefore does not discourse on it. To discourse on it would be to lead the myriad things to learn what they cannot. Thus, the sage does not discuss what lies beyond, and the eight distinctions dissolve into self-sufficiency. One presents one’s nature and rests in it, following its completed traces to congeal at the ultimate of rightness, without clinging to one’s own rightness to condemn others.

Each thing divides itself; each affair distinguishes itself. To desire to impose one’s own divisions is to fail to see their self-distinction. The sage harbors no such intent. Not seeing their self-debate, he debates what he knows to show them.

The great Dao is not named; great debate does not speak; great benevolence is not benevolent; great integrity is not modest; great courage does not harm. When the Dao is made manifest, it is no longer the Dao; when speech is eloquent, it falls short; when benevolence is constant, it is not all-encompassing; when integrity is pure, it is not trustworthy; when courage is harmful, it fails. These five, though incomplete, nearly approach the proper direction.

Entrusting things to their own names, there is nothing to name. They distinguish themselves. Existing without attachment, they endure on their own. The utterly sufficient one is not affected by things’ comings and goings, so there is no place for modesty or fullness. Without obstruction, one can go anywhere without peril. To use this to illuminate that is to lose both. One cannot reach the self-division of things.

Things have no constant attachment, and constant attachment is never all-encompassing. Brightly pure integrity is merely the greed of the fame-seeker, not true integrity. Harmful, contrary courage is detested by all under heaven, leaving no ground to stand. These five all injure what is fitting by acting, unable to rest in their original nature and endlessly seeking what lies outside. Seeking what cannot be sought is like trying to learn the square from the round or a fish longing to be a bird. Though one may aspire to phoenixes and emulate the sun and moon, the closer one draws to them, the farther one strays from reality. The more one learns, the more one loses one’s nature. Thus, when things are equalized, the burden of partial preferences is cast off.

To know to stop at what one does not know—this is perfection. Who knows the debate that does not speak, the Dao that is not spoken? If one can know this, it is called the Heavenly Storehouse. Pour into it without filling it; draw from it without depleting it; yet one does not know whence it comes—this is called preserving the light.

What one does not know lies beyond the bounds of inherent nature. Thus, to stop within what one knows is to reach perfection. Vast and unconstrained, one entrusts all to it. The mind of the perfected one is like a mirror: it responds but does not store, so it remains open without fluctuation of fullness or emptiness. The coming of the ultimate principle leaves no trace. Allowing it to clarify itself, its light is never dimmed.

Thus, long ago, Yao asked Shun: "I wish to attack Zong, Kuai, and Xu’ao, yet when I face south, I cannot put it aside. What is the reason?" Shun said: "Those three lords dwell among the mugwort and reeds. Why can you not put it aside? In ancient times, ten suns appeared together, and the myriad things were all illuminated—how much more so for one whose virtue surpasses the sun!" Yao’s way of peaceful acceptance was not yet vast, so he listened to court affairs without joy. Seeking to lodge the principle of luminous equality in a great sage, he posed this strange question to elicit an answer.

What things find peaceful has no baseness, so the mugwort and reeds are the wondrous abode of those three lords. When the doubled brightness ascends the heavens, the six directions are all illuminated, and there is no mugwort or reed that is not bathed in light.

The sun and moon, though impartial in their illumination, still leave some places unreached—yet virtue knows no such lack. Now, to force the aspirations of the mugwort and reeds and compel them to follow one’s own will—how could this expand the supreme Dao? Thus does the spirit remain unresolved.

If all things flourished according to their nature, each resting in what it finds peaceful, whether distant or near, hidden or deep, left to itself in perfect ease—then all would attain their fullest potential. In that case, nothing would be amiss for them, and nothing would bring disquiet to me.

Nie Que asked Wang Ni: "Do you know what all things agree to be right?"
Wang Ni replied: "How could I know?"
What they agree upon is not necessarily right, and what they differ on is not uniquely wrong—thus neither can correct the other, and there is no use for such knowledge.

"Do you know what you do not know?"
"How could I know?"
If one knew what one did not know, that would itself be a form of knowledge. With knowledge, one could not entrust things to their own rightness.

"Then are things without knowledge?"
"How could I know? Though let me try to speak of it: How do I know that what I call knowledge is not ignorance? How do I know that what I call ignorance is not knowledge? Now, let me ask you this: When people sleep in damp places, their waists ache and they become half-paralyzed—is that the case for loaches? When they dwell in trees, they tremble with fear—is that the case for monkeys? Of these three, which knows the proper place to dwell? People eat domesticated animals, deer eat grass, centipedes relish snakes, owls and crows favor mice—of these four, which knows the proper flavor? Monkeys mate with monkeys, deer couple with deer, loaches swim with fish. Mao Qiang and Xi Shi are considered beautiful by people—yet when fish see them, they dive deep; when birds see them, they fly high; when deer see them, they bolt away. Of these four, which knows the true beauty of the world? From my perspective, the beginnings of benevolence and righteousness, the paths of right and wrong, are tangled and confused—how could I discern their distinctions?"

The multitude knows nothing—only then is one vast and unburdened by preferences. Precisely because they do not know, I dare not speak with certainty; I merely offer tentative words. Fish swim in water, a capacity shared by all aquatic creatures, and all call this "knowing." Yet from a bird’s perspective, what was just called "knowing" becomes "not knowing." The dung beetle’s knowledge lies in rolling its ball, while those who mock the beetle prize storax resin. Thus, what is commonly called "knowing" cannot be taken as an absolute standard. What is called "not knowing" is simply a matter of difference—it is still a kind of knowing, unique to its own domain. Since I do not know what is correct, I tentatively ask you.

Here, I briefly cite three examples to illustrate the differing advantages of the myriad things. Here, I briefly cite four examples to show that beauty and ugliness have no fixed standard. Here, I briefly cite four examples to demonstrate that what the world favors varies endlessly. To condemn what differs is to have no way of knowing whether what is shared is truly correct. What benefits one may harm another, and since "one" and "another" in the world are boundless, right and wrong have no constant end. Therefore, only by refraining from debate and allowing each to follow its own nature can all things be freely and fully realized.

Nie Que said, "If you do not know benefit and harm, does the Perfected Person likewise not know benefit and harm?" Not yet having mastered this "not knowing," he still suspects that the Perfected Person ought to know.

This knot remains unresolved.

Wang Ni said, "The Perfected Person is divine! Though great marshes burn, they cannot make him hot; though the Milky Way freezes, they cannot make him cold. Though swift thunder splits mountains, it cannot harm him; though whirlwinds shake the seas, they cannot startle him. One such as this rides the clouds and qi, mounts the sun and moon, and roams beyond the four seas. Life and death bring no change to him—how much less the mere tips of benefit and harm!"

Without intent, yet nothing is not accorded. When spirit is whole and faculties complete, and the body merges with things, though one traverses the most extreme transformations, one has never ceased to be oneself. Thus, nothing lingers or lodges in the breast.

One lodges in things and moves—it is not the self that acts. Having taken change as one’s body, life and death are as one. Day and night pass without life or death—how much less can benefit and harm disturb the mind?

Ju Quezi asked Chang Wuzi, "I have heard from the Master that the sage does not engage in affairs, does not pursue profit, does not avoid harm, does not delight in seeking, does not follow the Dao. Without words, they speak; with words, they are wordless—and roam beyond the dust and grime. The Master considered these careless words, but I take them as the practice of the wondrous Dao. How do you think I should regard them?"

Chang Wuzi said, "Even the Yellow Emperor would have been perplexed by such words—how could Confucius know them! Moreover, you are far too hasty in your reckoning. Seeing an egg, you demand the crowing of the rooster at dawn; seeing a slingshot, you seek the roasted owl. I have spoken to you carelessly—why not listen carelessly in turn?

"To stand beside the sun and moon, to embrace the cosmos, to let things meet of themselves, to set aside confusion and disorder—why rank them in order of honor? The multitude toil to and fro; the sage appears foolish and dull. They blend the myriad ages into a single purity. All things are thus, and in this they are mutually enfolded.

"Affairs come of themselves, and principle responds of itself—one does not follow and engage with them. Proceeding straightforwardly, one avoids nothing and seeks nothing. Seeking brings no delight; taking directly brings no anger. This is the singular attainment.

"All that bears a name is not what I call it—they each name themselves. Thus, there is no 'that' with words and no 'this' without words. All that is not true nature is dust and grime.

"Things have their spontaneity; principle has its ultimate. Following it directly, one merges obscurely of itself—no words are needed. Thus, to speak is careless, and to hear is to be perplexed. Even the Yellow Emperor could not prevent all things from harboring attachments and being perplexed to the end. Therefore, the sage entrusts things to the realm beyond dust and grime, mysteriously uniting with what lies beyond sight and hearing. Illumined by Heaven, they do not oppose with calculation; released to spontaneity, they do not force clarity.

"Now, Ju Quezi, upon hearing careless words, you immediately take them as the practice of the wondrous Dao. This is no different from seeing an egg and demanding the rooster’s crow or seeing a slingshot and seeking the roasted owl. Those who cannot rest in their time and follow the natural order, who probe change and seek transformation, who contemplate death while alive, who cling to 'this' to debate 'that'—all are those who oppose with calculation.

"To speak is to be careless—so I have spoken carelessly. But if you listen to careless words as if they were true, you are again too hasty in your reckoning. So listen carelessly—why not?

"To take life and death as day and night is the metaphor of standing beside the sun and moon. To take the myriad things as a single body is the analogy of embracing the cosmos. Because there is that which is deemed base, honor and lowliness arise, and confusion and disorder proliferate, with none able to set them right. Each is self-certain in its own corner.

"Thus, for the Dao of effortless self-union, nothing is better than setting it aside and not speaking, entrusting it to spontaneity. To be vast and boundless is to be without waves or shores. To gallop through the realm of 'this' and 'that' is to be impure. Purity is to be without admixture.

"To survey the myriad ages and blend their changes—ordinary people call this 'mixed.' Thus, they toil in body and startle the mind, shunning 'that' and clinging to 'this.' Only the great sage, without attachment, proceeds straightforwardly and becomes one with change. In constant oneness with change, they ever roam in singularity. Though they blend countless ages, a thousand differences and ten thousand distinctions, when the Dao moves through them and completes them, past and present are one. When things name them, the myriad things are as one.

"Nothing is not so; no time is not complete—this may be called purity. Nothing is not so. 'Enfolded' means accumulated. Accumulating 'this' over the myriad ages makes the myriad ages one 'this.' Accumulating 'so' over the myriad things makes the myriad things all 'so.'

"Thus, one does not know where life and death, before and after, reside; where 'that' and 'I,' victory and defeat, go. How do I know that delighting in life is not delusion? Life and death are one, yet one delights only in life—desiring to oppose change. Thus, one does not know it is delusion.

"How do I know that hating death is not like a youth lost and unable to return home? Young and losing one’s former home is called 'youth lost.' The youth lost settles into where they are and does not know to return to their homeland. How do I know that life is not such a youth lost? How do I know that death is not a return home—and yet one hates it?

"Li Ji was the daughter of the border guard of Ai. When the state of Jin first obtained her, she wept until her tears soaked her collar. But when she reached the king’s palace, shared his bed, and ate fine meats, she later regretted her tears. How do I know that the dead do not regret having sought life?

"Within a single lifetime, emotions change like this. On that day, one does not know the other—how much less can one know the change of life and death! 'Sought' means 'desired.'

"One who dreams of drinking wine wakes and weeps; one who dreams of weeping wakes and goes hunting. While dreaming, one does not know it is a dream. Within the dream, one may even divine the dream—only upon waking does one know it was a dream."

Moreover, only after the great awakening does one know that all this was but a great dream. Yet fools believe themselves already awake, priding themselves on their keen discernment. "A lord! A herdsman!" How rigid they are! Even you and I, Confucius, are but dreams—when I say you are dreaming, I too am dreaming. Such words are called *diaogui* (strange and paradoxical). Even if a great sage should appear once in ten thousand generations to understand their meaning, it would be as if one encountered him at dawn or dusk.

This is the transformation of waking and dreaming. If circumstances change, emotions differ, and so the wishes of life and death cannot be the same. Thus, when alive, one delights in life; when dead, one delights in death. Though life and death differ, in each attaining their own wish they are one—what is there to cling to? From this perspective, at the moment of death, one does not know it as death but simply follows one’s own inclination. The dreamer who within a dream divines that dream differs not at all from the waking man. In the moment of encounter, nothing is lacking—why then, while alive, should one worry about death?

The great awakened one is the sage. The great awakened one knows that those who harbor anxieties and concerns remain unawakened. Fools, lost in their great dream, believe themselves awake, smugly distinguishing what they favor as rulers and what they despise as herdsmen, delighting in the biased views of their own faction—how narrow-minded they are! Unable to forget words and attain spiritual understanding, they are not truly awakened. Extraordinary discourse is beyond the comprehension of ordinary men, hence it is called *diaodang zhuogui* (startling and paradoxical), and they fail to recognize its profound liberation.

Those who can shed all attachments and harmonize life and death are exceedingly rare. Even if you and I were to debate, and you defeated me, or I defeated you—would you truly be right? Would I truly be wrong? If I defeated you, and you did not defeat me, would I truly be right? Would you truly be wrong? Is it possible that one of us is right? Or that one of us is wrong? Or that both are right? Or that both are wrong? You and I cannot know for certain. People are inherently shrouded in obscurity—whom could I ask to judge rightly? If I asked one who agrees with you to judge, since he agrees with you, how could he judge rightly? If I asked one who agrees with me to judge, since he agrees with me, how could he judge rightly? If I asked one who differs from both you and me to judge, since he differs from us, how could he judge rightly?
