If I asked one who agrees with both you and me to judge, since he agrees with us, how could he judge rightly? Thus, you, I, and others all cannot know for certain—should we then await another?

You and *er* both mean "you." Without knowing, one speculates; without seeing, one debates. Debating is insufficient for self-assurance because it opposes things. Debating all day remains shrouded in obscurity, and in the end, none can be rightly judged—thus, one must leave it to self-correction. Agreement does not suffice as proof, nor does disagreement as grounds for rejection. If what you hold to be right were truly right, then no one in the world could contradict it; if what you believe to be wrong were truly wrong, then there would be no basis for anyone to affirm it. Now, people affirm what they agree on and reject what they differ on—with agreement and difference both present, right and wrong have no fixed ground.

Thus, right and wrong arise from the love of debate and rest in the *tianjun* (Heavenly Equalizer). Entrust them to the two alternatives and let them resolve themselves. Each corrects itself. Relying on another is insufficient to correct oneself, so no one in the world can correct another—thus, one must leave it to self-correction to reach the ultimate.

The *tianni* (Heavenly Boundary) is the natural division. Right and wrong, affirmation and negation, self and other oppose one another—hence, there is no debate. Without debate, one harmonizes with the *tianni*, abiding in one’s natural division alone, without relying on another for correction. The mutual dependence of opposing voices is like their non-dependence. Harmonizing with the *tianni* and following the boundless transformation, one exhausts the years.

"What does it mean to harmonize with the *tianni*?" It means: "Is it so? Is it not so? So or not so. If what is right were truly right, then its difference from what is not right would require no debate; if what is so were truly so, then its difference from what is not so would require no debate." Forgetting years, forgetting righteousness, one roams beyond all bounds—thus, one lodges in the boundless. The debate over right and wrong is but the transformation of voices. The mutual dependence of these transforming voices is insufficient to correct one another, so they are as if non-dependent. Harmonizing with the natural division and following the infinite transformation, pursuing this path, the realm of right and wrong naturally dissolves, and the essence of life and destiny is fully realized.

Forgetting years, one harmonizes life and death; forgetting righteousness, one transcends right and wrong. When right and wrong, life and death dissolve into one, this is the ultimate principle. The ultimate principle flows without limit—thus, those who lodge in it cannot be exhausted.

The shadow’s companion asked the shadow, "Earlier, you were moving; now, you are still. Earlier, you were sitting; now, you are rising. Why are you so without constancy?"

The shadow replied, "Do I depend on something to be thus? Does what I depend on also depend on something to be thus? Do I depend on the scales of a snake or the wings of a cicada? How would I know why it is so? How would I know why it is not so?"

The "shadow’s companion" is the faint shade beyond the shadow. This speaks to the natural workings of the celestial mechanism, where sitting and rising occur without dependence. Those who attain without dependence—who can fathom their cause and demand their reason? If one insists on tracing their dependence and seeking their origin, such inquiry would stretch endlessly, ultimately reaching the state of no-dependence, where the principle of spontaneous transformation becomes clear. If one were to depend on the scales of a snake or the wings of a cicada, the lack of constancy would not be hard to understand. The reason it is not understood now is precisely because it does not depend on such things but transforms spontaneously.

Some in the world say the shadow’s companion depends on the shadow, the shadow on form, and form on the Creator. Let us ask: Does the Creator exist or not? If not, how could it create things? If so, it would not suffice to account for the multitude of forms. Only by recognizing that the multitude of forms arise from themselves can one begin to speak of creation. Thus, within the realm of existing things, even the shadow’s companion does not fail to transform spontaneously in profound obscurity. Therefore, the Creator has no master, and each thing creates itself. Each thing creates itself without dependence—this is the true nature of heaven and earth.

Thus, self and other are mutually entailed, form and shadow arise together; though profoundly united, they do not depend on one another. Understanding this principle will lead all things to return to their root within themselves without relying on externals. Externally, nothing is to be rejected; internally, nothing is to be prized. Therefore, all things emerge serenely without knowing why they are born, and all attain their nature without knowing how they attain it. Now, the shadow’s companion’s relation to the shadow is like saying they arise together without dependence. Thus, though all things gather and together complete heaven, each stands distinctly and sees itself alone. Therefore, the shadow’s companion is not controlled by the shadow, nor is the shadow moved by form, nor is form transformed by nothingness. Whether transformed or not, so or not so, following others or acting on one’s own—all arise spontaneously. How could I know their cause?

Thus, to follow without aiding is to allow root and branch, inner and outer, to flow unimpeded and attain their nature, leaving no trace. If one clings to proximate causes and forgets their spontaneity, seeking externals and losing the inner master, then attachment and esteem arise. Though one may wish to equalize and harmonize, what is esteemed already resides in the heart—how can there be any leveling?

Once, Zhuang Zhou dreamed he was a butterfly, fluttering and joyful, a butterfly content with itself. He did not know he was Zhou. Suddenly he awoke, and there he was, palpably Zhou. Did Zhou dream he was a butterfly, or did the butterfly dream it was Zhou? Between Zhou and the butterfly, there must be a distinction. This is called the transformation of things. Delighting in one’s own joy, acting with ease and pleasure—when he dreamed he was a butterfly and did not know he was Zhou, it was no different from death. Yet wherever he was, he was content. Thus, those who cling to life while alive will surely cling to death when dying. From this, we see that those who grieve over death while alive are mistaken.

Speaking from Zhou’s perspective, he called it waking, but it may not have been waking. Now, not knowing the butterfly is no different from the dream’s not knowing Zhou. Each suits its momentary contentment, so there is no way to prove the butterfly does not dream it is Zhou. Some dream in a brief nap and experience a hundred years—there is no way to prove that this hundred years is not a dream in a nap. Time does not pause for even a moment, and the present does not endure. Thus, yesterday’s dream has already transformed by today. The change between life and death is no different—why trouble the heart over it?

To be so absorbed in one is to be unaware of the other—this is like dreaming of being a butterfly. To take it from a human perspective, in a single lifetime, the present does not know the future—this is like Lady Li. Yet fools secretly believe they know that life is joyful and death is bitter, never having heard of the transformation of things.

*From the "Inner Chapters" of the Zhuangzi, "The Secret of Caring for Life"*

My life has limits, but knowledge is without bounds. To pursue the boundless with the limited is perilous! And to do so while claiming to be wise is perilous indeed! In doing good, avoid seeking fame; in doing evil, avoid approaching punishment. Follow the central path as your constant guide—this can preserve the body, fulfill life, nurture one’s kin, and complete one’s years.

Life exists to be nurtured, so nurturing life is the ultimate principle. Yet if nurturing exceeds its limit and harms life, it is no longer the true way of nurturing life. The endowment each receives has its own limit. Lifting heavy weights and carrying light ones while the spirit and qi remain unperturbed—this is the limit of one’s strength. Those who crave fame and compete for victory, even if they break their backs, will never satisfy their desires—this is the boundlessness of knowledge. Thus, the pursuit of knowledge arises from imbalance and vanishes in profound spontaneity.

Profound spontaneity means following one’s utmost capacity without adding even a hair’s weight. Therefore, though bearing ten thousand jun, if it matches one’s ability, one suddenly forgets the weight on one’s body. Though responding to ten thousand affairs, one remains oblivious to their presence. This is the true way of nurturing life. To seek boundless knowledge with a limited nature—how can one not be exhausted? Already exhausted by knowledge and not knowing to stop, then using knowledge to remedy it—this is nurturing that harms, truly perilous.

Forgetting good and evil and abiding in the center, allowing all things to act on their own, merging obscurely with the most fitting—thus, punishments and fame keep their distance, and perfect principle resides within. Taking the central path as one’s constant, nurturing one’s kin appropriately—if one attains the center and moves in profound spontaneity, then nothing is impossible. Nurturing life does not seek to exceed one’s portion; it is merely about fulfilling principle and completing one’s years.

When Cook Ding carved an ox for Lord Wenhui, where his hand touched, his shoulder leaned, his foot stepped, his knee pressed—*hua!*—the sounds rang out. The blade’s advance—*huo!*—was flawless, matching the rhythm of the "Mulberry Grove" dance, attaining the harmony of the "Jingshou" melody.

Lord Wenhui exclaimed, "Excellent! How has your skill reached such heights?" He spoke of how Cook Ding seized every advantage to apply his craft, leaving no joint unloosed, fully exhausting principle—both perfectly suiting the ox’s structure and harmonizing with rhythm and measure.

Cook Ding set down his blade and replied, "What your servant delights in is the Dao, which advances beyond mere skill. When I first began to carve oxen, all I saw was the whole ox. After three years, I no longer saw the whole ox. Now, I encounter it with spirit rather than sight; my senses halt while my spirit moves. I rely on Heaven’s patterns, cleaving great gaps, guiding the blade through great cavities, following what is inherently so. Even the finest network of sinews and bones has never once impeded me—how much less the great thigh bones! A good cook changes his blade every year, for he cuts; an ordinary cook changes his blade every month, for he hacks. Your servant’s blade has endured nineteen years, carving thousands of oxen, yet its edge remains as if freshly whetted. For where the joints have spaces, and the blade has no thickness, entering with what has no thickness into those spaces—how vast is the room for the blade to move! Thus, after nineteen years, my blade’s edge is still as if newly whetted from the grindstone.

Yet whenever I come to a complex cluster, I see the difficulty, grow cautious, focus my gaze, slow my movements, and move the blade with utmost subtlety—*huo!*—and the ox falls apart like clods of earth. I stand holding the blade, look around in satisfaction, then clean and put it away." Here, he simply lodged the Dao within his skill—what he truly delighted in was not skill itself.

Though he has yet to perceive the spaces within the principle—(3)
He simply perceives those spaces within the principle.
The office of scrutiny lies abandoned;
he lets his mind flow freely, following the principle’s grain. (4)
He does not cut across it.
Where boundaries arise, he merely strikes along them to part the way.
Joints dissolve, cavities open—he guides them to separate.
The blade is never applied recklessly.
The marvel of his skill lies in constantly gliding the edge through emptiness,
never once brushing against the slightest obstruction.

*Gū*—when the blade twists against a great bone, it galls the edge,
for it has not struck the space within the principle.
To hit bone is to break the blade.

*Xíng*—the whetstone.

Tangled clusters form where sinews and bones interlace.
He no longer fixes his gaze on other things.
His hand moves slowly.
When he finds the proper point, little force is needed.
The principle is cleaved without trace of the blade, as if earth crumbles away.

*Yì zú róng yù zì dé*—this is ease of foot, ample leisure, and self-possession.

He wipes the blade and sheathes it.

Prince Wenhui said: "Excellent! From the words of Cook Ding, I have gained insight into nurturing life."
Since the blade may be nurtured, so too may life be nurtured.

Gongwen Xuan saw the Right Master and was startled, saying: "What manner of man is this? How did he become one-legged? Was it Heaven’s doing?"

"Was it man’s doing?"
He replied: "Heaven’s, not man’s. Heaven’s life bestows this solitary state, while man’s form bears companionship. From this we know it is Heaven’s work, not man’s."

*Jiè* is the term for partial amputation. What knowledge cannot remedy is Heaven’s doing. To defy what one knows is man’s doing. Partial amputation is called *solitary*. If a master’s knowledge, confined to one household, cannot preserve both feet, then this is what knowledge cannot remedy. Were the Right Master’s knowledge to insist on preserving both, his spirit would inwardly falter and his body outwardly decay—how could it be merely partial amputation! Walking with both feet is called *companionship*. The appearance of companionship never arouses doubt that it is not fate. Since companionship is fate, we know that solitude is likewise not of our own making. Thus, one who grasps the essence of life does not strive for what life cannot achieve; one who grasps the essence of fate does not strive for what fate cannot remedy. To preserve one’s natural state is all.

A marsh pheasant takes ten steps for a single peck, a hundred steps for a single drink, yet it does not seek to be kept in a cage. Though its spirit may flourish, this is not well-being. *Qí* means to seek; *fán* is what cages the pheasant. To move freely between heaven and earth, to wander in the realm of self-possession—this is the subtlety of nurturing life. Why seek to enter a cage and submit to care? What begins in comfort and never ceases to be comfortable is to forget comfort. The pheasant’s spirit flourishes, its will and qi overflow with ease, and it roams the clear, open land, unaware that it is doing well.

When Lao Dan died, Qin Shi went to mourn him, crying out three times before leaving. A disciple said: "Was he not your friend?"
"Yes."
"Then is such mourning fitting?"
People mourn, and so he mourned; people wail, and so he wailed. They found it strange that he did not linger at the door to observe the transformation, yet cried out three times.
He said: "At first I thought he was a man, but now I see he was not."

When I entered to offer condolences, I saw elders weeping for him as if for their own children, and the young weeping as if for their own mothers. Those who gathered there must have been moved to speak when no words were sought, to weep when no weeping was sought. This is to flee from Heaven and betray one’s nature, to forget what one has received—ancients called this "the punishment of fleeing from Heaven." When he came, it was the Master’s time; when he departed, it was the Master’s compliance. To rest in one’s time and abide in compliance is to keep sorrow and joy from entering—ancients called this "the Great Release."

The perfected person has no personal attachments and merely joins the crowd in lamentation, so such conduct is fitting. The nature one receives from Heaven has its own proper measure; it cannot be escaped, nor can it be augmented. To flee from Heaven’s principle and amplify worldly emotions, to let sorrow and joy pass through the heart, is to bind the spirit in suffering, no different from flogging. Was this not a kind of punishment? The enlightened of old held such views.

Time brings life of itself; principle demands death. Sorrow and joy arise from loss and gain. But the sage who embodies mysterious penetration and harmonizes with change is at peace in every moment, at home in every circumstance. Merged with the transformations of creation, there is nowhere that is not oneself—what gain or loss, what life or death could there be? Thus, to follow what one has received is to leave no space for sorrow or joy to intrude.

To take the bound as suspended is to see the unbound as the Great Release. In this release, the truth of one’s nature and destiny is realized—this is the essence of nurturing life. A finger may be exhausted in serving as kindling, but the fire is transmitted, and its end is unknown. "Exhausted" means "ended"; "kindling" is like the previous fuel. The finger may reach the end of the previous fuel’s principle, yet the fire is passed on and does not expire. The heart that receives nurturance continues life without cessation. This reveals that nurturing life is the very means by which life endures.

Time does not return, and the present does not pause. Thus, a person’s life is but a single breath, a single moment of attainment. The previous breath is not the present breath, so nurturance continues life; the former flame is not the later flame, so the fire is transmitted through the fuel. The fire is transmitted and life continues—this is the culmination of nurturance. How could the world know that life is exhausted only to be reborn?

From *Zhuangzi*, "Inner Chapters," "The Human World" (Chapter Four):
Yan Hui sought an audience with Confucius and asked to be sent on a mission.

Yan Hui said, "Where are you going?"
He replied, "I am going to the state of Wei."
Yan Hui asked, "What will you do there?"
He replied, "I have heard that the lord of Wei is in his prime, yet his conduct is willful. He treats his state lightly and does not recognize his faults. He recklessly expends his people’s lives, and the dead are measured by the state as if filling a marsh—like reeds, the people have nowhere to turn. I once heard you say, ‘Leave a well-governed state; go to a disordered one. The physician’s gate is crowded with the sick.’ I wish to apply what I have heard and reflect on how to act, hoping that the state may yet be cured."

One who mingles with others cannot depart from them. Yet the vicissitudes of the human world differ with each generation, and only those who are without fixed intent and do not impose their own will can adapt to change without bearing its burdens. He does not share the people’s desires. For one who rules others, every action rides upon the people—one outburst of anger brings corpses and flowing blood, one moment of joy fills the roads with carriages and caps. Thus, the ruler’s use of the state cannot be taken lightly. None dare admonish him. He recklessly drives them to their deaths. The entire state is delivered to the brink of death, countless as the fallen, regarded as mere grass and weeds. They have nowhere to turn.

Confucius said, "Alas! You are likely to go and be punished! The Dao does not tolerate杂乱.杂乱 leads to excess, excess to disturbance, disturbance to worry, and worry cannot be remedied. The utmost person of old first established it within themselves before establishing it in others. If what is established within oneself is not yet settled, how can one attend to the conduct of a violent ruler? Their Dao is insufficient to save that affliction. It is fitting to find the right person. If one does not find the right person, then even if a hundred physicians guard the illness, it will only deepen doubt and fail to bring a single cure. To respond to things without an empty mind and to exhaust one’s thoughts in confronting difficulties—this shows that what is established within oneself is not yet settled. Only by setting aside knowledge to nurture the true, entrusting the wondrous to the talents of the multitude, attributing merit and fame to others while keeping worries and concerns far from oneself, can one then proceed to the conduct of a violent ruler.

Do you also know whence virtue’s dissipation and knowledge’s emergence arise? Virtue dissipates for the sake of fame, and knowledge emerges from contention. Fame is that by which people vie; knowledge is the instrument of contention. Both are inauspicious tools, not means to fully realize one’s conduct. Virtue’s dissipation stems from pride in fame; knowledge’s reckless emergence stems from contention over goodness. Even Jie and Zhi, in what they prized and cherished, sought nothing beyond fame and goodness. Fame and wisdom are what the world employs. Yet when fame arises, contention follows; when wisdom is used, strife flourishes. Thus, only by abandoning fame and knowledge can one’s conduct be fully realized.

Moreover, thick virtue and steadfast trust have not yet reached others’ *qi*; fame without contention has not yet reached others’ hearts. To forcibly display the language of benevolence and righteousness, compass and square before a violent ruler is to make others resent what they perceive as your beauty—this is called ‘bringing disaster upon others.’ One who brings disaster upon others will surely have disaster reflected back upon them. You are likely to bring disaster upon yourself. If you merely delight in the worthy and despise the unworthy, what need is there to seek distinction? If you do not instruct, the king and dukes will surely exploit others to vie for victory. Your eyes will be dazzled, your complexion will become placid, your mouth will scheme, your demeanor will take shape, and your heart will conform. This is like using fire to save fire, water to save water—it is called ‘increasing excess.’ Following the beginning without end, you will likely, with insincere grand words, meet your death before the violent ruler!

When one offers a luminous pearl at night, few do not grasp their swords—this is because they do not understand. Now, your virtue and trust, along with your fame of non-contention, are things they do not understand. To forcibly apply the standards of benevolence and righteousness to them, they will say you seek to destroy others to establish yourself. Thus, the utmost person does not exhaust their will in ordering the world but responds to things with an empty mind. Sincerity and trust are manifest in heaven and earth; non-contention flows freely among the myriad things. Only then do the myriad things return to embrace them, heaven and earth do not oppose them, their virtuous reputation spreads and the world responds in harmony, their illustrious conduct shines and the six directions all answer. Only then can one traverse cold and heat, navigate order and chaos, without clashing with the dragon’s reversed scales.

If your approach is not trusted, they will say you contend for fame and turn to harm you. If you can delight in the worthy and despise the foolish, hear righteousness and submit, you will be a enlightened ruler. If you are an enlightened ruler, you will not suffer from a lack of worthy ministers, and your going will not be extraordinary. If it is not so, you will surely be harmed. Thus, to go with intent is to have no place to go; to respond without intent is to have response come of itself—then there is nowhere you cannot go. You need only remain silent and wordless. If you speak, the king and dukes will surely exploit their authority as rulers to engage in verbal sparring, using clever speech to reject admonition and adorn their faults. Their words will be quick and persuasive, dazzling the eyes. You will be unable to distinguish yourself from them, with no time to save or extricate yourself, and will end up releasing yourself to follow them. Unable to save them, you will only strengthen their power.

Clinging to the usual and refusing to change, they will not trust you. To admonish without trust, even earnest words will bring harm. Moreover, in ancient times, Jie killed Guan Longfeng, and Zhou killed Prince Bigan—both were those who cultivated themselves and humbly comforted the people, yet by submitting to those below, they offended those above. Thus, their rulers used their cultivation to cast them out. These were lovers of fame. In ancient times, Yao attacked Cong, Zhi, Xu, and Ao, and Yu attacked Youhu. Their states became wastelands of the dead, and they themselves were executed. Their use of arms did not cease, their pursuit of fame and substance was unending—have you alone not heard of this? Fame and substance are things even the sages cannot overcome—how much less can you!"

"Even so, if you must have some way, come and tell me of it."

Guan Longfeng and Prince Bigan, though dwelling below, took on the cares of those above—this was not their proper duty. They did not wish to let ministers gain the reputation of surpassing their rulers. Tyrannical rulers do not merely seek to indulge their desires; they also seek fame, though what they seek is not the true Dao.

Rulers who covet fame and lust after desires—even Yao and Yu could not transform them. Thus, the people joined together to attack them, yet you would go empty-handed to transform them with the Dao?

Yan Hui said, "If I maintain a proper demeanor yet empty my mind, and strive to be single-minded, would that suffice?"

"To rectify one’s form while emptying one’s heart—this is to speak with humility and without duplicity."

He said, "That will not suffice."

Yan Hui asked, "Why not? If one’s yang qi is full to overflowing, with shifting colors and an unsteady countenance—common people dare not oppose it. One seizes upon what moves others to seek compliance with one’s own heart. This is called a gradual virtue that does not take shape—how much less can great virtue do so! One will cling rigidly without transformation, outwardly conforming while inwardly scorning—how could this ever succeed?"

This speaks of the Lord of Wei: his overbearing yang nature swells within and flaunts itself without, reaching the height of arrogance. His moods shift unpredictably, and none dare defy him. Such extreme obstinacy—when others act to please him, he tramples them, then suppresses and humiliates them, seeking ease and indulgence to satisfy his extravagant heart. His words, whether few or many, admit no yielding or concession. Thus, he clings to his original intent. Outwardly conforming while inwardly scorning—this is merely the earlier "proper demeanor, empty mind, and single-minded striving," which is insufficient to transform him.

"Then let me be inwardly upright and outwardly yielding, achieving success by emulating the ancients. To be inwardly upright is to follow Heaven. One who follows Heaven knows that the Son of Heaven and oneself are both children of Heaven. Why should one alone seek that others praise one’s words or seek that others not condemn them? Those who act thus are called childlike—this is what it means to follow Heaven. To be outwardly yielding is to follow others. Raising the hands, kneeling, and bowing—these are the rites of a minister. If all others do so, how dare I not? To do what others do, so that none find fault—this is what it means to follow others. To achieve success by emulating the ancients is to follow the past. Though my words may instruct, their substance is fitting and true, for they belong to the ancients, not to me. Thus, though upright, one avoids harm—this is what it means to follow the ancients. Would this suffice?"

Yan Hui further expounded these three principles. All things, whether noble or base, share the same attainment of life. Thus, whether good or not good, one leaves it to impartial judgment—seeking nothing from others. Following Heaven’s principle, one extends from one’s nature and entrusts it to fate, proceeding as straightforwardly as an infant. Outwardly, the form bends and yields, adapting to what human affairs demand. Success in the present is measured against the past. Though this is common teaching, it carries the essence of admonition and reproof. By lodging uprightness in antiquity, one avoids bringing harm upon oneself.

Confucius said, "Alas! How can this suffice? There are too many policies and laws, yet they lack harmony. Though one may be steadfast, one remains blameless—but this is all there is to it. How can it ever reach transformation? It is still to follow one’s own mind. The principle admits no duality, yet by enforcing these three measures to govern, one fails to merge with affairs. Though not grand in scope, one may yet avoid censure. Blame may be absent, but transformation is not. Wielding these three methods to suit circumstances is not to act without intention, entrusting all under Heaven to fate."

Yan Hui said, "I have no further way to advance. I venture to ask for your guidance."

Confucius said, "Purify yourself, and I will tell you. To act with intention—is this easy? Those who deem it easy are unsuited for the vastness of Heaven."

Yan Hui said, "My family is poor, and for months now I have drunk no wine and eaten no meat. May this be called purification?"

Confucius replied, "This is the purification for sacrifice, not the purification of the mind."

Yan Hui said, "I venture to ask what the purification of the mind is."

Confucius said, "Unify your will. Do not listen with the ears but listen with the mind; do not listen with the mind but listen with qi. The ears are limited to hearing, the mind to matching signs. Qi is that which is empty and awaits things. Only the Dao gathers in emptiness. Emptiness is the purification of the mind."

To act with intention in the mind is indeed not easy. Those who deem intentional action easy have not seen its fittingness. To discard heterodoxies and rely on singularity—abandon the senses, set aside the mind’s intentions, and accord with the self-attainment of qi and nature: this is to be empty and await things. Empty the mind, and the supreme Dao will gather in your embrace.

Yan Hui said, "Before I had yet received this teaching, there truly was a Yan Hui. Having received it, there has never been a Yan Hui—may this be called emptiness?"

He had not yet practiced the purification of the mind, and so his self remained.

The Master said, "Perfect! I shall tell you this: if you can enter and wander within its enclosure without being moved by its name, then when it enters, you will respond; when it does not enter, you will cease. Without gate, without governance—abiding in the single dwelling of inevitability—then you are near.

Having attained the service of the mind’s purification, you will be without self. The realm where the unmoored heart finds itself is where one should stop at the real. It is like the notes of the pentatonic scale: they respond without intention, and thus it is called resonance. To respond without intention is to follow the other without forcing a reply. Let things be as they are—this is to be without gate. Entrusting the world to its natural ease—this is to be without governance. *Du* means to govern. Inevitability is the necessity of principle, the dwelling of utmost singularity that meets the tally of necessity. Principle is exhausted in this.

Cutting off tracks is easy; leaving no trace upon the earth is difficult. Being used by others is easy to feign; being used by Heaven is difficult to feign. We have heard of flying with wings, but never of flying without wings; we have heard of knowing with knowledge, but never of knowing without knowledge. Behold that empty chamber: in the vacant room, whiteness arises, and auspiciousness comes to rest. If one does not come to rest, this is called sitting in motion. If one follows the ears and eyes, turning inward while abandoning the mind’s knowledge, then even ghosts and spirits will come to dwell—how much more so men! This is the transformation of the myriad things, the pivot of Yu and Shun, the ultimate course of Fuxi and Jiqu, and how much more so for those who are scattered!

Not acting is easy; to act without treading upon the earth is impossible. Non-doing is easy; to act without harming one’s nature is unattainable. What the eyes and ears grasp is coarse, and thus easily deceived. But the response of spontaneity is subtle, and thus difficult to feign. The less one loses of the real, the less one is incomplete; the more one loses of the real, the more one is incomplete. The recompense for loss and gain has never failed to match its measure. To go against Heaven and feign falsity—is this not difficult?

Words must have their instruments to accomplish their tasks. Now, without the dwelling of utmost emptiness, how can one have the reality of transforming things? To see something as if it were nothing—this is the vacant room. In the vacant room, pure whiteness arises alone. Where auspiciousness gathers is in utmost emptiness and stillness. If one does not rest in what is fitting, does not meet the ultimate, then even in the days of responding while sitting, one races without cease. Thus, the outer enemy has not yet arrived, yet one is already exhausted within—how can one transform things?

If one closes the ears and eyes and naturally attains, then the mind’s knowledge is externalized. Therefore, to follow one’s nature and move directly, going nowhere without darkness—how could one be burdened by the obscure and hidden, let alone the entanglements of the human world? Things have no nobility or baseness; none fail to communicate through the mind’s knowledge and the ears and eyes. Thus, what the world calls knowledge—is it desired in order to know? What it calls seeing—is it done in order to see? If knowledge and sight could be obtained by desire and effort, then could one desire worthiness and obtain worthiness, or strive for sagehood and attain sagehood? Certainly not.

Yet the world does not know that knowledge knows itself, and so seeks to know by desiring knowledge; it does not see that seeing sees itself, and so seeks to see by desiring sight. Not knowing that life lives itself, it then strives to live by contriving life. Thus, seeing the eyes, one demands the clarity of Li Zhu; hearing the ears, one demands the acuity of Shi Kuang. The spirit races within, the ears and eyes are exhausted without, the body is ill at ease, and one is no longer in darkness with things. Not being in darkness, how can one harmonize with the changes of the human world or respond to the rhythms of every generation?

Lord Gaogao of Ye was about to be sent as an envoy to Qi. He asked Confucius, saying, 'The king’s charge to me, Zhu Liang, is most weighty. Qi’s treatment of envoys is generally respectful but not urgent. Even a common man cannot be moved—how much less a feudal lord! I am deeply fearful. You once told Zhu Liang: "In all matters, whether small or great, few do not rely on the Dao to achieve joyful completion. If the matter is not completed, there will surely be the calamity of human judgment; if it is completed, there will surely be the calamity of yin and yang. Only those with virtue can achieve completion or non-completion without calamity." My meals are coarse and not refined, and my cook has no desire for coolness. Yet this morning I received the charge, and by evening I am drinking ice—is this not the heat within me? I have not yet reached the truth of the matter, yet I already have the calamity of yin and yang! If the matter is not completed, there will surely be the calamity of human judgment—this is a double burden. As a minister, I am not equal to this task. Please tell me what to do!'

The weight of the mission is because he seeks something. He fears that Qi will merely return empty respect and not urgently respond to his request. In matters great or small, few fail to achieve joyful completion through words—this is what Confucius once told Zhu Liang. Those who rely on completion for joy will be angered if it is not achieved. This is something even the King of Chu cannot avoid. Though the human calamity may be averted, joy and fear still war in the breast, and ice is already congealed in the five viscera. Only those with virtue can entrust success or failure to the other without troubling the heart!

Facing the fire without thinking of coolness shows the frugality of his fare. That his fare is frugal yet he drinks ice from inner heat is truly due to the difficulty of the matter, not the excellence of the food. Before the matter is completed, he only fears it will not be. If it truly fails, then fear will congeal within, and the net of punishment will spread without.

Confucius said, 'There are two great precepts under Heaven: one is fate, the other is duty. Your love for your parents is fate—it cannot be untied from the heart. A minister’s service to his lord is duty—there is nowhere he can go under Heaven that is not his lord, nowhere to escape within the earth.'"

These are called the great precepts. Thus, in serving one’s parents, to be at ease without choosing the place is the utmost filial piety; in serving one’s lord, to be at ease without choosing the task is the height of loyalty. In serving one’s own heart, sorrow and joy do not alter what lies before it—knowing the inevitable and accepting it as fate is the perfection of virtue.

For a minister on the brink, there are indeed things he cannot avoid. Acting with the truth of the matter and forgetting himself, how could he have leisure to delight in life and dread death? The Master’s conduct is thus! Naturally bound fast, it cannot be undone.

When a thousand gather, if none is taken as master, disorder will lead to dissolution. Thus, many worthy men cannot have many lords, and without worthies there cannot be no lord—this is the Dao of Heaven and man, the inevitable principle. If a lord could be fled and parents untied, there would be no need for precepts.

To know the inevitable as fate and accept it is to be without sorrow or joy—how could one’s bearing be altered? Thus, in profound obscurity, one takes what one encounters as fate and does not interpose the heart between them, merging utterly with what is most fitting without joy or sorrow within. Even in serving ordinary men, there is nowhere one does not find accord—how much more so with lord and parents!

Matters have their inevitabilities, principles are ever-pervading. Thus, to entrust them is to accomplish them, and where matters are accomplished yet the self is not preserved—such has never been. Why then trouble the heart over the self? Principles are without obstruction, so one should entrust what one encounters and proceed directly.

If one’s faith in the Dao is not steadfast and likes and dislikes remain in the heart, unable to proceed with what is most fitting while scheming for life and fearing death, I have never seen such a one succeed in his affairs.

Let me repeat what I have heard: In close dealings, trust must be mutually confirmed; in distant dealings, loyalty must be conveyed through words. Words must sometimes be transmitted. To transmit words of twofold joy or twofold anger is the most difficult thing under Heaven.

For twofold joy invariably produces excessive praise, and twofold anger invariably produces excessive blame. All excess is falsehood; falsehood breeds distrust, and distrust brings calamity to the transmitter. Thus, the model saying goes: *"Transmit the constant truth, do not transmit excessive words—then you will be nearly whole."*

Those near at hand can verify, so their trust is confirmed through direct contact. Those afar must convey intent through words. Words of joy and anger, if they exceed reality, should be transmitted so that neither side loses the mean—hence the difficulty. *Excess* means going beyond. Words of joy and anger always exceed what is fitting. Suspecting the words are not his, one assumes the transmitter fabricates. Distrust arises. If excessive words are transmitted, it seems like wild falsehood. The recipient doubts, and the transmitter is arbitrarily blamed for exaggeration or understatement.

Even if one hears momentary excesses, do not transmit them. Instead, convey the constant truth and capture the sincerity of the intent—then you will be nearly whole.

Moreover, those who contend with skill and strength begin in openness but invariably end in secrecy; when excess prevails, many strange tactics arise. Those who drink wine with ritual begin in order but invariably end in chaos; when excess prevails, many strange pleasures arise. All things are thus: they begin in sincerity but invariably end in baseness. Their inception is simple, but their conclusion must be vast.

Words are like wind and waves; actions are like gain and loss. Wind and waves are easily stirred; gain and loss are easily imperiled. Thus, anger arises without cause, and artful speech with biased words. When beasts die, they do not choose their cries—gasping and disordered, they give rise to vicious hearts. Scrutiny carried too far must evoke unworthy responses, yet one does not know it is so. If one does not know it is so, who knows where it will end?

Thus, the model saying goes: *"Do not alter commands, do not urge completion. Going beyond is adding to them."* Altering commands and urging completion endanger affairs. Beauty takes time to perfect; evil, once done, cannot be undone—can one not be cautious?

Originally, all enjoyed the game. When the desire to win arises, it secretly gives rise to harming others. No longer following principle, distinctions of rank are lost, and toasts proceed without order. Drunkenness and excess prevail, reaching every extreme. Trouble arises from simplicity, affairs from the minute—this is the inevitable course.

Words are like wind and waves; thus, acting on them is like gain and loss. If one leaves wind and waves unacted upon, then reality is not lost. When affairs attain their reality, danger can be pacified and turmoil settled.

The arising of anger has no other cause—it always comes from artful words exceeding reality and biased speech missing the mark. Like wild beasts driven into a corner, their cries urgent and their breath spent, harmony does not come, and their breath is disordered. Suddenly enraged, they both give rise to vicious sores in mutual opposition.

To be broad and accommodate all things, all things will gather to you. Scrutiny carried too far breeds petty and miserly hearts without one’s awareness. Thus, the great man lets things roam freely in the realm of self-attainment, neither begrudging others’ abilities nor exhausting their joy. Therefore, the fellowship of the four seas can be preserved whole.

If one does not realize this, how can one know where fortune and misfortune equally lead?

Transmit the other’s reality. Entrust its natural completion. Adding to it is not entrusting reality—this endangers affairs. Beauty is completed by entrusting its timely transformation, like planting—it cannot be done in a single morning. To urge completion of what one dislikes is to invite regret and failure.

Moreover, to ride things with the heart and entrust the inevitable to nurture the center—this is perfection. What need is there to act in repayment? It is best to deliver one’s mandate—this is the difficult part.

To lodge the mind in things is to entrust the inevitable course of principle. The fullness of the Mean is thus preserved—this is the utmost in engaging with things. One should entrust the reality of what is to be repaid—why interpose one’s intent in the matter?

Delivering one’s mandate directly is easiest, yet applying joy and anger to the heart makes it difficult.

When Yan He was about to serve as tutor to the crown prince of Duke Ling of Wei, he asked Qu Boyu, saying: “There is a man here whose virtue is heaven-sent for destruction. If I deal with him without restraint, it will endanger our state; if I deal with him with restraint, it will endanger myself. His knowledge suffices only to recognize others’ faults, not their causes. In such a case, what should I do?”

The nature of petty men is such that if you guide them by rules, they resent you; if you indulge their excesses, they throw the state into chaos. Unaware that the people’s faults arise from themselves, they blame the people and never reform. Qu Boyu said: “An excellent question! Be on guard, be cautious—rectify yourself! In form, nothing is better than compliance; in heart, nothing is better than harmony. Yet these two have their perils. Compliance must not become submission, harmony must not become display. If in form you comply and submit, you will be overthrown, destroyed, collapse, or stumble. If in heart you harmonize and display yourself, you will become a reputation, a name, an omen, a calamity.

Let him be an infant, and you be an infant with him; let him be without boundaries, and you be without boundaries with him; let him be without limits, and you be without limits with him—thus guiding him to enter flawlessness. Through repeated encounters and convergence, both serve to rectify yourself. In form, do not oppose; in harmony, do not conform. Compliance in form is following; submission is merging with him. Harmony is aided by rightness; display is self-promotion.

If you merge with him, you will topple and not support him, perishing together. Thus, you must model yourself on heaven and earth, merely avoiding petty distinctions. If you display your harmony, you will earn a reputation for tolerance, but aiding his reputation will make him resent your superiority and recklessly spawn calamities. Therefore, you must remain obscure as if in darkness, blending like dust in light—then you cannot be drawn near or kept distant, cannot be benefited or harmed. Do not set small edges against his scales.

Do you not know the mantis? It brandishes its arms to block a chariot’s wheel, unaware of its incapacity—such is the beauty of its talent. Beware, be cautious! To accumulate your strengths and beauties to challenge him is perilous indeed! The mantis’s brandished arms are not without beauty, yet blocking the chariot’s wheel is simply no match. Now, with knowledge insufficient to cope, you seek to forcefully take on this task—this is the mantis brandishing its arms. To accumulate your talents, boast of your beauties, and challenge this man is the path to danger.

Do you not know those who raise tigers? They dare not feed them living creatures, for fear of their killing rage; they dare not feed them whole creatures, for fear of their rending rage. They time their hunger and satiety, understanding their angry hearts. Tigers and humans are of different kinds, yet tigers fawn on those who raise them—this is compliance. Thus, those they kill are those who oppose them. They fear that a killing intent will provoke rage. If you make the tiger bite and tear its food, it will use force and grow angry. Know the cause of its anger and comply with it. Compliance with principle makes even different kinds grow affectionate; opposition to nature makes even the closest turn to arms.

Those who love horses use baskets to collect their dung and conch shells to collect their urine. Yet if a mosquito or gadfly alights, and they strike it untimely, the horse will break its bit, shatter its chest-plate. Love can reach a point where it causes loss. Can one not be cautious?”

The dung and urine are most base, yet one uses precious vessels to contain them—this is the utmost in loving horses. Servants bustle about tending to the horse. Though they seek to relieve its distress, they catch the horse unawares. Striking when it is unprepared, they startle it to this extreme. The intent was to remove harm, yet in haste they strike, leading to destruction and breakage, losing the very reason for their love. Thus, in dealing with things in the world, one must be cautious at the juncture of compliance and opposition.

When Master Carpenter Shi traveled to Qi, he reached Quyuan and saw an oak that was a sacred tree. Its greatness could shade thousands of oxen; its girth measured a hundred spans. Its height towered over the mountains, and only after rising eighty feet did it bear branches. Those that could be made into boats numbered more than ten at its side. Spectators gathered like a marketplace, yet the master carpenter did not glance at it and continued on his way without pause. His disciple, having gazed his fill, ran to catch up with Master Shi and said, “Since I took up the axe and followed you, Master, I have never seen timber so fine as this. Why do you refuse to look and walk on without stopping?” He replied, “Enough! Say no more. It is but a useless tree.”

"To use it for a boat would cause it to sink; for a coffin or outer coffin, it would quickly rot; for vessels, it would swiftly break; for doors or windows, it would ooze resin; for pillars, it would be eaten by worms—this is a worthless tree. Having no use, it has thus attained such longevity."

Not counted among the useful, it is called a "scattered tree." Master Carpenter Shi returned home, and the sacred oak appeared to him in a dream, saying: "With what would you compare me? Would you liken me to the so-called 'cultivated trees'? Those such as hawthorns, pears, oranges, and pomelos—the fruits of the vine and melon—once their fruit ripens, they are stripped, and once stripped, they are shamed. Their great branches are broken, their small branches are torn. This is how their abilities bring suffering to their lives. Thus, they do not complete their Heaven-ordained years but perish midway, struck down by the common world. All things are like this.

Yet I have sought to be of no use for a long time! Nearly dying, only now have I attained it—this is my great use. If I had been useful, could I have grown so vast? Moreover, you and I are both things—why would you judge me so? And you, a nearly dead 'scattered man,' how could you understand a 'scattered tree'?"

All useful trees are called "cultivated trees." All things harm themselves through their own usefulness. Many have sneered at me, but only you, Master Carpenter Shi, now perceive this. The accumulation of uselessness is the great use for preserving life. If one is useful, one will surely be felled in time.

This was in jest with Master Carpenter Shi. He awoke and interpreted his dream. His disciple asked: "If you seek the useless, then why does it serve as a sacred tree?" He replied: "Hush! Say no more!"

It merely lodges there by chance! It takes this as the scorn of those who do not know it. Had it not become a sacred tree, it would nearly have been felled! Moreover, what it preserves differs from the multitude, yet you compare it by righteousness—is this not far off the mark?"

He still suspected that the tree took pride in being a sacred tree, rather than simply seeking the useless. The sacred tree merely came to lodge there—it was not that the tree sought to become one. This tree, then, took the sacred tree as one who did not know it and thus brought disgrace and affliction—how could this be an honor? The tree itself took its uselessness as its use, so even if it were not a sacred tree, it would still ultimately avoid the harm of being felled. It preserved itself by not preserving, while the multitude preserved by preserving. Benefiting others and nurturing things, restraining people from wrongdoing—these are the duties of the sacred tree. But the useless one, serene and unobtrusive, allows all talents to be used; each self-using thing attains its proper place without interference—this is how the useless preserves life. You praise it as a sacred tree—how could it come close to that?

Nanbo Ziqi wandered in the hills of Shang and saw a great tree, unlike any other: its shade could shelter a thousand chariots, and travelers sought refuge beneath its foliage. Ziqi said, "What tree is this? It must possess extraordinary timber!" Looking up at its slender branches, he saw they were gnarled and unfit for beams or rafters; looking down at its great roots, he saw they were split and could not be made into coffins. Tasting its leaves, his mouth was blistered and wounded; smelling it, he was struck with a madness that lasted three days. Yet its branches provided shade enough to shelter a thousand chariots. Ziqi said, "This is truly a useless tree, and yet it has grown so vast! Alas, the spirit-like person—such is their uselessness."

When a ruler is useless to the hundred officials, the hundred officials manage their own affairs: the clear-sighted see for him, the keen-eared hear for him, the wise plan for him, the brave defend for him. What does he do? He remains darkly silent. And when all talents do not lose their proper place, then the useless becomes what all talents ultimately rely upon. Thus, the world delights in exalting him without tiring, riding upon the ten thousand things without harm.

"In Song there was a place called Jing, where catalpa, cypress, and mulberry thrived. Those with trunks the size of an arm’s embrace were felled by those seeking pegs for monkeys; those three or four spans around were felled by those seeking high and famous beams; those seven or eight spans around were felled by the families of nobles and wealthy merchants seeking side planks. Thus, they did not live out their natural years but were cut down midway by axes—this is the calamity of being useful. Therefore, when exorcists release one from misfortune, they discard the ox with a white forehead, the pig with an upturned snout, and the person with hemorrhoids, deeming them inauspicious and unfit for crossing the river. The exorcists know this well—these are what they consider ill omens. Yet this is precisely what the spirit-like person regards as the greatest auspice. Those with usefulness cannot help but cling to it. When exorcists perform their rites, they cast aside these three and meticulously select only the finest sacrificial animals before daring to use them. The exorcists, too, know that the useless remain whole. To preserve life is what the world calls auspicious, yet exorcists deem the useless inauspicious and refuse to use them—yet it is precisely through this inauspiciousness that life is preserved, making it the greatest auspice. The spirit-like person, without intention, follows things as they are. Thus, what the world calls the greatest auspice, the spirit-like person does not oppose.

There was one called Zhili Shu, whose chin was hidden in his navel, his shoulders higher than his head, his topknot pointing to the sky, his five organs above, his thighs beside his ribs. By mending needles and washing clothes, he earned enough to feed himself; by winnowing and sifting grain, he could feed ten people. When the authorities conscripted soldiers, Zhili waved his arms and wandered among them; when great labor was demanded, Zhili, citing his chronic illness, was exempted from service; when the authorities distributed grain to the sick, he received three bushels and ten bundles of firewood. Though his body was deformed, he could still nourish himself and live out his natural years—how much more so one whose virtue is deformed! Relying on his uselessness, he did not hide himself. Exempt from corvée labor, he did not serve; when gifts were given, he accepted them. The spirit-like person is of no use to things, yet things each attain their own use, attributing merit and fame to the multitude of talents, merging with things without a trace. Thus, he avoids the harms of the human world, abides in the substance of constant beauty—this is one whose virtue is deformed.

Confucius traveled to Chu, and the madman Jieyu wandered by his gate, singing:
*Phoenix! Phoenix!*
*How has virtue declined?*
One should follow the times and move forward, fully attaining the harmony of the moment. The rise and fall of the world are too trivial to notice—hence, "How has it declined?" The future cannot be awaited; the past cannot be pursued. One must simply do what is fitting in the present. When the world has the Dao, the sage achieves; when the world lacks the Dao, the sage lives. Entrusting oneself to what is so, principle naturally arises. Arising and achieving are not of my doing—how could one change one’s course for order or chaos? Those who seek order pursue achievement, thus abandoning achievement and not failing; those who face chaos seek life, thus forgetting life and not dying. In the present age, one merely avoids punishment. Without looking ahead or behind, one fully meets the present moment, merging darkly with the times to attain perfect harmony, thus preserving oneself and avoiding the names of punishment.

Happiness is lighter than a feather, yet none know how to bear it. Feet can walk—let them roam; hands can grasp—let them hold. Let the ears hear what they hear, the eyes see what they see; know to stop where knowledge ends, and act only where ability lies. Use what is self-using, do what is self-doing, indulge one’s nature without the slightest concern for what lies beyond—this is the utmost ease of non-action."

No one has ever preserved their nature and life through action, nor has anyone whose nature and life were whole not attained good fortune. Thus, good fortune is nothing other than the wholeness of which we spoke earlier—it is not borrowed from external things, so how could it bear the weight of a goose feather? To move in accord with one’s nature, never exceeding its bounds, is the utmost ease in the world. To lift what lifts itself, to bear what bears itself, is the utmost lightness in the world.

Yet knowing injures one’s nature by its boundlessness, and the heart disturbs the true by its likes and dislikes. Thus, people abandon this utmost ease of non-action to pursue the utmost difficulty of action, forsaking the utmost lightness of self-lifting to take up the utmost weight of bearing others. This is the common affliction of the world.

Disaster is heavier than the earth, yet none know how to avoid it. If one acts within one’s nature, even bearing ten thousand *jun* brings no sense of weight. If one relies on external things, even a weight no more than a *zhu* may prove unbearable. Acting inwardly is good fortune, and thus good fortune is supremely light. Acting outwardly is disaster, and thus disaster is supremely heavy. That disaster is supremely heavy yet none know how to avoid it—this is the world’s great delusion.

Enough, enough! To govern others with virtue is perilous, perilous! To draw lines on the ground and make others follow them—such traces cannot be concealed. To possess something oneself and then impose it upon things is to fail to merge with them.

Thus, the great person does not dazzle others with their own brightness but lets others find their own clarity; they do not impose their virtue upon others but allow others to attain their own virtue. In this way, they pervade and unite the myriad things, darkly merging self and other, indistinctly becoming one with the world, so that inner and outer share the same good fortune.

"Deluded yang, deluded yang—do not harm my path."
"Deluded yang" means lost yang. If one abides in solitude without being swayed by externals, one’s path remains whole.

"Crooked, crooked—do not harm my feet."
If one’s conduct is perfectly curved, it is self-sufficient.

Mountain trees bring ruin upon themselves; fat is consumed by its own flame. Cinnamon is edible, so it is felled; lacquer is useful, so it is cut. People all know the use of what is useful, but none know the use of what is useless. What is useful serves others’ purposes; what is useless preserves one’s own life. Those who carve their flesh for the world are known to the world. But to allow the people to preserve their wholeness while both self and other find harmony—this is a subtlety so profound that none sense its presence within.

*From the "Inner Chapters" of the Zhuangzi, "Virtue’s Perfect Correspondence" (Chapter Five):*

In Lu there was a man named Wang Tai who had lost a foot, yet those who followed him were as numerous as those who followed Confucius. His virtue was full within, and things responded to him without; inner and outer merged darkly, so that he trusted his fate as if it were a tally, forgetting his bodily form. His disciples were as many as Confucius’s.

Chang Ji asked Confucius, "Wang Tai is a man who has lost a foot, yet those who follow him divide Lu equally with you. He stands without teaching, sits without discussing. Those who go to him empty return full."

Had there not been a teaching beyond words, a shaping of the heart without form? What manner of man is this? Each finds sufficiency in himself. Yet it is strange that though his form is maimed, his heart is full. When the heart is whole, it leaves the body behind, forgets the five viscera, and wanders alone—none under heaven can part from it.

Confucius said, "This Master is a sage. I am but a latecomer who has not yet gone to him! I shall take him as my teacher—how much more should those who are not my equal! Why limit it to Lu? I shall lead all under heaven to follow him." When spirit is whole and the heart complete, the body merges with things. One who merges with things cannot be distanced by all under heaven—how could it be merely one state?

Chang Ji said, "He is a man who has lost a foot, yet he surpasses the common run by far. If this is so, how does he employ his heart uniquely?"

Confucius said, "Life and death are great indeed, yet he does not change with them. Though heaven and earth collapse, he will not be lost with them. He examines what is without reliance and does not shift with things; he commands the transformations of things while guarding their root." Though people change daily, the change of life and death is the greatest of changes. He moves with change, so life and death do not alter him. This is following it. It is understanding the constancy of nature and fate, taking transformation as fate without opposition, never departing from the ultimate rightness.

Chang Ji said, "What does this mean?"

Confucius said, "Viewed from what makes them different, the liver and gallbladder are as distant as Chu and Yue. Viewed from what makes them the same, the myriad things are all one. One who is like this does not know what the ears and eyes deem fitting but roams the heart in the harmony of virtue. He sees what things share and does not see what they lose; he regards the loss of a foot as discarding dirt."

When the nature of pleasure and suffering differs, the feelings of beauty and ugliness diverge. Though what is deemed beautiful varies, all share in having something beautiful. Each deems beautiful what it deems beautiful, so the myriad things are one in beauty. Each affirms what it affirms, so all under heaven is one in affirmation. If one distinguishes things by their differences, then nothing under heaven is not different. Yet one who gazes with vast clarity governs heaven and earth, contains the myriad things, and knows that differences are not truly different. Thus, by following what is shared, nothing under heaven is not the same. Knowing further that sameness is not truly possessed, by following what is without, right and wrong, beauty and ugliness—nothing is not without.

To affirm oneself and negate others, to find beauty in oneself and ugliness in others—from those of middling understanding down to crawling insects, none are not thus. Yet this is merely clarity about oneself and not clarity about others. As for those of profound penetration and merging, they use all under heaven to illuminate all under heaven. If all under heaven says, 'I am not,' then it illuminates that there is nothing not so. If it says, 'That is right,' then it illuminates that there is nothing right. Without right and without wrong, all blends into one. Thus, one can ride change and follow transformation, oppose things without fear. Having forgotten all that is fitting, nothing is not entrusted. To entrust all and not attain—this has never been. To attain all and not be in harmony—this too has never been heard of. Therefore, the heart is released into the space of the Dao and virtue, drifting without anything amiss, vast and without anything unsuitable.

Embodying the wondrous heart of the utmost principle, one can be without difference from any thing. Without difference from any thing, then in life and death, change and transformation, there is nowhere that is not oneself. Thus, life is my time, death is my following; time is my gathering, following is my scattering. Though gathering and scattering differ, I am always myself. Thus, life is merely my moment—there has never been gain. Death too is myself—there has never been loss. The change of life and death is but one. Having seen its oneness, one sheds all ties, darkly merging self and other, taking life and death as waking and sleeping, the bodily form as an inn. Departing life is like removing sandals, severing a foot like discarding dirt—I have never seen anything that could entangle his heart.

Chang Ji said, "He cultivates himself. Through his knowledge, he attains his heart; through his heart, he attains his constant heart. What can things do to surpass him?"

Wang Tai was suspected of not having forgotten knowledge to preserve himself, of not having relinquished the heart to attain self-possession. Yet to attain one’s constant heart is to be level in one’s comings and goings. The suspicion was that he could not remain level in his comings and goings when encountering things, and thus constantly compelled things to come to him.

Confucius said, "No one mirrors themselves in flowing water, but in still water. Only stillness can still the many that seek stillness. Rooted in earth, only the pine and cypress stand upright, ever green through winter and summer. Rooted in heaven, only Yao and Shun stand upright, at the head of all things. Fortunate to have rectified their lives, they rectify the lives of all. The proof of preserving one’s beginning lies in fearlessness—one brave warrior can charge into nine armies. Those who seek fame and can bind themselves to it are already thus, let alone those who govern heaven and earth, store all things, merely lodge in the six senses, take ears and eyes as mere images, unite knowledge with what is known, and whose hearts have never died! He will choose a day to ascend on high, and people will follow him as a matter of course. How would he deign to make things his concern?"

The stillness of water that allows it to serve as a mirror is not stillness sought for the sake of mirroring. Thus, when Wang Tai gathered a following, the people came to him of their own accord—how could he have drawn them to follow him? If he had acted to make it so, he could not have abided in the stillness of all things. The pine and cypress are uniquely endowed with the qi of nature’s harmony, and thus stand as the finest of trees—not because they acted to attain it. Those who uniquely receive the upright qi of nature are exceedingly rare: below, only the pine and cypress; above, only the sages. Thus, all that are not upright come seeking rectification. If all things were whole and green, the pine and cypress would hold no distinction; if all people were self-rectified, none would admire the great sage and hasten to him. Fortunate to be upright of themselves, they do not rectify in order to rectify others. They do not relinquish fame yet bear all things without constraint, darkly embodying all without exception. This is what is meant by "an inn." People use ears and eyes, yet they do not depend on ears and eyes. When knowledge moves with transformation, there is nowhere it does not darkly merge—this is the oneness of knowledge. When the heart flows with life and death, there is no time that is not life—this is the heart that has never died. To choose is merely to avoid losing the meeting; such a person makes no choices, but follows heaven’s course and moves with the times. Thus, those who borrow from him are led to esteem him. His serenity preserves his wholeness.

Shentu Jia, a man without feet, studied alongside Zichan of Zheng under Bohun Wuren. Zichan said to Shentu Jia, "If I go out first, you stay; if you go out first, I stay." The next day, they again sat together in the same hall. Zichan said to Shentu Jia, "If I go out first, you stay; if you go out first, I stay. Now I am about to go out—can you stay? Or not? Moreover, when you see the one in power, you do not avoid him. Do you consider yourself equal to the one in power?" He was ashamed to walk alongside one without feet. Pressing the matter, he sought to ensure Shentu Jia would not walk beside him. Always prizing his own position, he bluntly declared Shentu Jia equal to the one in power, as if this alone proved his insolence.

Shentu Jia said, "In the Master’s gate, are there truly those in power like this? You delight in your position and put others after yourself. I have heard: ‘When a mirror is bright, dust does not settle; if it settles, it is not bright. Long dwelling with the worthy leaves no faults.’ Now, what you take as greatest is the Master, yet you still speak thus—is this not a fault?" This was a discussion of virtue, not a calculation of rank. He mocked Zichan’s pride in his position, his desire to precede others. To serve a clear teacher yet retain such a stingy heart—this was the true fault.

Zichan said, "Given your condition, you still contend with Yao in goodness. By your virtue, are you not sufficient to reflect on yourself?" He meant that with such a maimed form, Shentu Jia did not examine himself but instead slighted those in power, comparing himself to the virtuous. By his virtue, he was not enough to make up for the fault of his maimed form.

Shentu Jia said, "Those who describe their faults, claiming they should not have lost what they lost, are many; those who do not describe their faults, claiming they should not have kept what they kept, are few. Knowing there is nothing to be done and accepting it as fate—only those with virtue can do this. Wandering within Yi’s drawn bow: the center is the mark, yet missing it is fate. Many with whole feet mock my incomplete feet; I bristled with anger, but when I came to the Master’s place, I turned back, at peace. Did the Master wash me with goodness? Or did I awaken myself? I have wandered with you for nineteen years, yet you have never known I was without feet. Now you wander with me within the form, yet seek me outside the form—is this not a fault?" He often recounted his own faults, claiming many believed he should not have lost what he lost.

Yi was a master archer of ancient times. Wherever his bow and arrows reached was called the "drawn bow’s range." When profit and harm contend, the world becomes a field of archers like Yi. Those who do not abandon themselves, forget knowledge, and flow with things—all wander within Yi’s drawn bow. Even if one ventures forth like Zhang Yi or dwells in seclusion like Shan Bao, they cannot escape the mark; whether one hits or misses it depends solely on fate. Yet each clings to their own encounters, unaware that fate unfolds of itself. Thus, those who escape harm from bow and arrow deem themselves skillful, rejoicing in their superiority. But when they cannot escape, they blame their own errors, grieving in spirit and shame—such are those who fail to grasp the nature of fate.

Our lives are not of our own making. Within a single lifetime, across a hundred years, all our sitting and rising, moving and pausing, inclinations and aversions, emotions and abilities—all that we possess, all that we lack, all that we do, all that we encounter—are not truly ours; they arise by principle alone. To impose joy and sorrow upon this is to go against nature and lose the Way. All such mockery stems from ignorance of fate.

Seeing the perfected one’s knowledge of fate and abandonment of form, he cast aside his former anger and returned to equilibrium. Was it because the Master cleansed me with the virtuous Dao? Or was I able to reflect upon myself? Thus, he forgot his form and shed his burdens. For when form is forgotten, virtue resides within. Now you wander with me in virtue, not in bodily form, yet you seek my outward adornments—is this not a fault?

Zi Chan, struck with realization, changed his expression and said, "Say no more!" Having awakened, he grew weary of excessive words.

The heel is the cheek. In Lu there was a man named Shushan Wuzhi who had lost his toes; he came to see Confucius by dragging his heels. Confucius said, "You were not careful, and previously you suffered such misfortune. Though you come now, what can be done?"

When a person is born, the principle arises of itself. Simply do nothing and let it grow naturally—this is to value the body and know one’s duty. If one forgets this natural arising, becomes cautious, and acts with self-importance, this is to use the body lightly and fail to know one’s duty. Thus, the five organs attack one another within, and hands and feet are maimed without.

Wuzhi said, "I only failed to know my duty and used my body lightly, which is why I lost my foot. Now that I have come, what remains of what I valued still exists, so I strive to preserve it whole. Heaven covers all without exception; Earth bears all without exception. I took you, Master, to be like Heaven and Earth—how could I have known you would still be like this?"

To cast aside self-importance and caution, to let life unfold naturally—this is to strive for wholeness. To reproach another for lack of care is to fall short of Heaven and Earth.

Confucius said, "I am indeed narrow-minded! Sir, why not come in? Please share what you have heard."

Hearing what he had heard, he departed, preserving his non-action. Wuzhi left.

Confucius said, "Disciples, take heed! Though Wuzhi is maimed, he still strives to study and remedy his past misdeeds—how much more should one of whole virtue do so!"

One of whole virtue is born and forgets life. Wuzhi said to Lao Dan, "Is Confucius not yet a perfected person? Why does he persist in treating you as a teacher with such earnestness? He seeks to be known for marvels, uncanny illusions, and strange wonders, unaware that the perfected person regards these as his own shackles."

He found it strange that Confucius should again study under Lao Dan. One without attachment learns as others learn. Yet the ancients studied for themselves, while those of today study for others—this flaw has led them to act as others act.

One who teaches others to attain self-realization follows what is constant; one who abandons the self to imitate others and chases externals seeks an extraordinary reputation. Such extraordinary reputation arises from what is constant.

Thus, study is not for the sake of marvels, yet marvels inevitably arise from study. Ritual is not for the sake of ornate display, yet ornate display inevitably arises from ritual. This is the necessary principle, which the perfected person can do nothing about—hence he regards it as his shackles.

Lao Dan said, "Why not simply lead him to see life and death as a single thread, the permissible and impermissible as one continuum, and thus free him from his shackles? Would that be possible?"

He wished to guide him directly to the obscure truth, hoping to leave no trace.

Wu Zhi said, "Heaven has punished him—how can it be undone!"

Now, Confucius was not without profound insight. Yet by the natural principle, where there is movement, a shadow follows; where there are words, echoes respond. To comply with things is to establish a name and trace, though one who complies with things does not act for the sake of name. Not acting for the sake of name is perfection, yet one cannot ultimately escape name—who then can free himself from it? Thus, name is the shadow and echo; shadow and echo are the shackles of form and sound. When this principle is understood, name and trace may be discarded. When name and trace may be discarded, attachment to externals may be severed. When attachment to externals may be severed, one’s nature and life may be preserved whole.

Duke Ai of Lu asked Confucius, "In Wei there is an ugly man named Ai Taituo. When men dwell with him, they grow attached and cannot leave; when women see him, they plead with their parents, saying, 'Rather than be another man’s wife, I would be his concubine'—and this has happened more than ten times without cease. He has never been heard to initiate, only to harmonize with others. He holds no lordly position to save others from death, no wealth to fill others’ bellies; moreover, his ugliness startles the world. He harmonizes but does not initiate, his wisdom does not extend beyond his immediate sphere, yet men and women alike flock to him—there must be something extraordinary about him. I summoned him to observe, and indeed, his ugliness startled the world. When he dwelt with me, before a month had passed, I took notice of his character; before a year had elapsed, I trusted him. As the state had no chief minister, I offered him the governance. He responded with reluctance, as if declining; I felt ashamed, yet in the end I entrusted him with the state. Soon after, he left me and departed. I grieved as though I had suffered a loss, as though there were no one left to share the joy of ruling this state. What manner of man is he?"

Ugliness is but outward deformity. Yet all things flock to him not by force or favor. They come not seeking sustenance, nor drawn by comeliness of form. No summons brings them, no calculation beyond their nature guides them. One whose talents are whole harms nothing in the world: entering the herd, he does not disturb the beasts; joining the flock, he does not scatter the birds. Thus he becomes a refuge for the ten thousand things.

Before a month had passed, his profound depth was already perceived. Entrusted with the governance of the state, neither favor nor disgrace could unsettle his spirit. When others declined, he too declined.

Confucius said: "Once, while traveling in Chu, I saw young boars nursing from their dead mother. After a moment’s gaze, they abandoned her and fled. They saw not themselves in her, nor recognized their kind. What they loved in their mother was not her form, but that which animated her form. When warriors fall in battle, their burials are not adorned with military regalia; when a man’s feet are severed, his shoes are no longer cherished. In both cases, the root is lost. The consorts of the Son of Heaven are chosen without cutting their nails or piercing their ears; once wed, they are not sent on further errands. If even bodily wholeness suffices to command such reverence, how much more one whose virtue is whole!"

Now, Aitai Tu never spoke yet was trusted, achieved nothing yet was cherished, was offered a state yet feared only that he might refuse—surely he is one whose talents are whole and whose virtue remains unmanifest."

They nursed from her milk. The living are drawn to their kind by talent and virtue; when these depart in death, the living flee, having lost their kind. Thus one whose inner power is profound resembles a newborn babe: wherever he goes, all treat him as an infant, and none in the world would harm him. This is because he retains his kind and reveals his true nature.
