If the heart shares a kindred nature, even dissimilar forms will not provoke hostility; if that nature is lost, even forms as close as mother and child cannot secure the bond.

That which animates the form is talent and virtue. Military regalia are the trappings of valor; when a warrior dies in battle, valor is gone—where then should regalia be placed? Shoes are cherished for the sake of the feet; when the feet are lost, the shoes are no longer loved. Both lose their root. The Son of Heaven’s consorts remain unaltered in body, and once wed, they are not sent away, for their bodily wholeness is preserved. How much more revered is one whose virtue is whole!

Duke Ai asked: "What is meant by *talents whole*?"
Confucius replied: "Life and death, survival and ruin, poverty and wealth, worthiness and unworthiness, slander and praise, hunger and thirst, cold and heat—these are the changes of affairs, the workings of fate. They alternate before us day and night, yet knowledge cannot fathom their beginnings. Thus they should not disturb inner harmony nor enter the sacred chamber of the mind. Let them flow smoothly without obstruction, never departing from joy. Let day and night be seamless, so that the heart ever abides in springtime, meeting all things with the vitality of the season. This is what is called *talents whole*."

The principle is inherently right—it cannot be escaped. Thus, human life is no mistake; what life possesses is no delusion. Though heaven and earth are vast and the myriad things numerous, what I encounter is precisely this—even the gods of heaven and earth, the sages of the state, could not alter it with all their strength and wisdom. Therefore, what one does not encounter cannot be encountered; what one encounters cannot but be encountered. What one does not do cannot be done; what one does cannot but be done. Entrust it to fate, and it will naturally be fitting.

Fate moves, affairs change, ceaselessly day and night, pressing forward without retreat, lingering without pause. Thus, one whose talents are whole follows what is encountered and entrusts it to fate. The beginning cannot be fathomed by knowledge, nor can the past be detained by emotion. Therefore, those who understand that fate must unfold and affairs must change—how could they scheme for the end or cling to the new and long for the old? Even with the utmost wisdom, they cannot fathom it. The past has gone—what can I do about it? If one knows that nature and fate are inherently right, then though life and death, poverty and success shift in a thousand transformations, one remains serene and at ease, harmony and principle abiding within.

The *spirit’s abode* is the dwelling of the essence. The perfectly content do not let cares and troubles disturb their spirit—they pass by like something external. If one’s harmonious nature remains undisturbed and the spirit’s abode is at ease, then though one traverses the greatest changes, one does not lose their joy. Silently, one entrusts all to fate. This is what the myriad beings rely upon, transforming together with the four seasons.

"What is meant by *virtue not manifest*?"
"The level is the fullness of still water. It can serve as a model: inwardly preserved, outwardly undisturbed. Virtue is the cultivation of perfect harmony. *Virtue not manifest* means things cannot depart from it."

There is no greater levelness in the world than still water. Utterly impartial, it is the standard by which all under heaven aligns. When affairs are accomplished and things are harmonized, this is called *virtue*. When no affair fails to be accomplished and no thing fails to be harmonized, this is *virtue not manifest*. Thus, all under heaven delight in exalting it without tiring.

Another day, Duke Ai told Minzi: "At first, I thought that facing south as ruler of all under heaven, holding the people’s guiding principles and worrying over their deaths, I was supremely enlightened. Now, hearing the words of the perfected person, I fear I lack their substance, have lightly used my person, and lost my state. I and Kong Qiu are not ruler and subject—we are friends in virtue!"

Those who hear of the wind of *virtue whole*—even Duke Ai—would wish to cast off their bodily form and forget nobility and baseness.

Yin Qi, the hunchbacked, lipless man, persuaded Duke Ling of Wei, who was so pleased that he compared whole-bodied men and found their necks too slender. The man with a great goiter persuaded Duke Huan of Qi, who was so pleased that he compared whole-bodied men and found their necks too slender. Thus, virtue has its excellence, and form has what may be forgotten. If one does not forget what should be forgotten but forgets what should not be forgotten, this is called *true forgetting*.

Partial emotion, once set in one direction, makes the ugly seem fine and the fine seem ugly. If one’s virtue excels in following things, things forget their ugliness; if it excels in opposing things, things forget their fineness. Living, they are loved; dead, they are discarded. Thus, virtue is what the world does not forget; form is what principle does not retain. Therefore, to forget form is not true forgetting; to forget virtue while clinging to form—that is *true forgetting*.

Thus, the sage wanders freely, yet knowledge becomes a snare, covenants become glue, virtue becomes a bond, craft becomes commerce. The sage does not scheme—what use is knowledge? Does not carve—what use is glue? Has no loss—what use is virtue? Does not trade—what use is commerce? These four are the *heavenly nourishment*. *Heavenly nourishment* is the *heavenly sustenance*. Having received sustenance from heaven, what need is there for man?

One has the form of a human but not the emotions of a human. Having the form of a human, one mingles with others; lacking human emotions, right and wrong do not adhere to one’s person. How small—this by which one belongs to humanity! How great—this by which one alone completes one’s heaven! Wandering in the realm of self-attainment, released without fail—this is *talents and virtue whole*. These four arise naturally from one another; their principle is already complete. Since it is naturally complete, the sage has no use for their own efforts. Words arise from nature and are endowed by it. Once endowed by nature, its principle is sufficient. Thus, though one may ponder deeply to avoid difficulty or clearly admonish to evade misfortune, nothing happens without reason—all are the convergence of heaven and earth, the direction of the supreme principle. What must be pondered is not my pondering; what must not be pondered is not my not pondering. Some ponder and avoid it; some ponder and do not avoid it; some do not ponder and avoid it; some do not ponder and do not avoid it. All this is not of me—what is there to do? Entrust it, and it comes of itself.

Their appearance is like that of a human. Like gathers with like, kind separates from kind—this is the Dao of nature. Without emotion, one entrusts all to things. Their appearance is like that of a human. Without emotion, they are vast and unburdened by anything. To be unburdened by anything is what those with emotions cannot achieve—thus, without emotion, one alone completes heaven.

Thus, virtue has its excellence, and form has what may be forgotten. If one does not forget what should be forgotten but forgets what should not be forgotten, this is called *true forgetting*.

Huizi said to Zhuangzi: "Are humans truly without emotion?"
Zhuangzi said: "Yes."
Huizi said: "If a human has no emotion, how can they be called human?"
Zhuangzi said: "The Dao gives them their appearance, heaven gives them their form—how can they not be called human?"

Human life is not born of emotion.

What life knows—how could it be what emotion knows? Thus, those with emotion strive for detachment and vastness yet cannot achieve it, while detachment and vastness are attained through freedom from emotion. Those with emotion strive to become worthy and sagely yet cannot achieve it, while the worthy and sagely become so through freedom from emotion. Is it merely that the worthy and sagely are utterly remote, and detachment and vastness are difficult to emulate? Even the dullest, the deaf, the blind, the crowing of roosters, the barking of dogs—though they may have emotion in their striving, they ultimately cannot succeed. Whether distant or near, even if separated by but a hair’s breadth, in the space between brow and eye, they can never attain it. Thus, when the myriad things are considered, one returns to the self: ears and eyes cannot substitute for one another to accomplish their tasks, nor can hands and feet take over one another’s duties to achieve their work.

When an infant first comes into the world, it does not seek milk with its eyes, nor turn its ears toward light, nor grasp objects with its feet, nor crawl with its hands. How could it be that the hundred limbs lack fixed offices, that the bodily form has no inherent master, and that emotion alone governs them? Huizi said: "If one is called human, how can they be without emotion?" He did not understand that the bodily form is not of emotion. Zhuangzi said: "This is not what I mean by emotion. What I call freedom from emotion is that one does not internally harm the body with likes and dislikes, but constantly follows nature without adding to life." If right and wrong are taken as emotion, then one without right or wrong, without likes or dislikes—though possessing bodily form—is merely human in name; where then could emotion reside? Proceeding directly where it is fitting, without deviation—this is not emotion. Stopping where it is fitting—this too is not emotion.

Huizi said: "If one does not augment life, how can they possess a body?"
He did not understand that life generates itself, and principle suffices of itself.

Zhuangzi said: "The Dao bestows its appearance, Heaven bestows its form—do not internally harm the body with likes and dislikes.
Now you estrange your spirit, exhaust your essence, lean against a tree chanting, rest upon a withered catalpa in slumber.
Heaven has selected this form, yet you clamor about ‘hard and white.’"

The principle of life is already self-sufficient within form and appearance; simply follow it, and the body endures.
The emotions of likes and dislikes do not augment life but only suffice to harm the body, for life has its allotted portion.
If the spirit does not rest within the bounds of its nature, it becomes estranged; if essence does not halt at the limit of self-generation, it becomes exhausted.
Thus, when walking, one leans against a tree chanting; when sitting, one rests upon a catalpa sleeping—this speaks of the self-entrapment of those with emotion.
It means that all your actions—estranging the spirit, exhausting essence, leaning against trees, resting upon catalpas, chanting and sleeping—are what the world calls emotion.
Yet to say it is ‘Heaven’s selection’ makes clear that what is called emotion does not arise from emotion—how much less anything else!
Thus, though the myriad things and myriad forms, their activities and preferences, all arise within the absence of emotion, what use is emotion between them?"

---

*From the "Inner Chapters" of the Zhuangzi, "The Great Master" (Chapter Six)*

To know what Heaven does and what man does—this is perfection!
To know what Heaven does is to be born of Heaven; to know what man does is to use what one’s knowledge knows to nourish what one’s knowledge does not know, thus fulfilling one’s Heaven-appointed years without dying prematurely—this is the fullness of knowledge.
Though Heaven and Earth are vast and the myriad things abundant, what they honor and take as master is without intention.

To know what Heaven and man do is to know that all is natural.
Thus, one inwardly releases the body and outwardly

Thus, though they do not regard heat as heat, they never rush into fire; though they do not regard wetness as wetness, they never tread into water; though they do not regard death as death, they never lose their lives. For life—does it live itself into being? For completion—does it complete itself into completion? Therefore, one who entrusts all to the natural and arrives at all without exception is a true person. How could they impose fixed intentions upon what they encounter?

The true persons of old slept without dreaming, woke without worry, ate without relishing flavor, and breathed deeply. The true person’s breath reaches the heels, while the breath of ordinary people reaches only the throat. Those who are cramped and submissive choke on their words as if retching. The deeper one’s cravings and desires, the shallower one’s Heavenly mechanism. They harbor no deliberate thoughts, content with whatever they encounter. What is proper to eat is simply eaten—this arises from the root. When qi is not balanced and flowing, one must deeply root and settle to the utmost, only then returning to oneness and desirelessness.

The true persons of old did not delight in life nor detest death. They did not rejoice at departure nor resist return. They came and went with effortless ease, neither forgetting their origin nor seeking their end. They received and rejoiced in it, forgot and returned to it—this is called not harming the Dao with the heart, not aiding Heaven with human effort. This is the true person, one who embodies transformation. Serene and entrusting all to the natural, they lodge in the utmost principle, thus coming and going without difficulty. Beginning and end, change and transformation—all are forgotten. How could they merely reverse forgetfulness of life while still probing the meaning of death? They do not question what they receive; whatever they encounter, they find it fitting. Their return is not by way of recognition—it is the utmost.

At birth, humans are tranquil—this is Heaven’s nature. Moved by things, they stir—this is the desire of nature. When the things that move people are endless and human pursuit of desire knows no bounds, then Heavenly principle is extinguished. The true person knows that using the heart turns one from the Dao, and aiding Heaven harms life, so they do not act thus. Such a one’s heart is forgotten, their countenance still, their brow serene. Chill as autumn, warm as spring, their joy and anger flow with the four seasons, harmonizing with things yet no one knows their limit. Where they dwell, they settle into their will. Though they act, they do not disrupt their tranquility.

Their brow serene—this is the appearance of great simplicity. To kill is not for the sake of might. Those who embody the Dao and accord with change share the warmth and severity of cold and heat, yet never hold it in mind. Still, they bear the semblance of warmth and severity, the rhythm of life and death, so we ascribe to them joy and anger. Without intent toward things, they do not violate what is fitting for them; with nothing unfitting, none know their limit.

Thus the sage in waging war may overthrow a state yet not lose the people’s hearts. Beneficence flows through ten thousand generations, yet not from love of others. One who delights in connecting with things is no sage; one who has favorites is not humane; one who follows the seasons is not worthy; one who does not penetrate benefit and harm is no gentleman; one who acts for fame yet loses the self is no scholar; one who destroys the self yet is not true is no servant of others.

Like those such as Hu Bujie, Wu Guang, Boyi, Shuqi, Jizi, Xuyu, Ji Ta, and Shentu Di—these serve the service of others, follow the fitting of others, yet do not follow their own fitting. They cause the fall of what the people’s hearts desire to fall, thus they do not lose the people’s hearts. The bright sun ascends the sky, illuminating the six directions—this is not from love of others.

Thus the sage abiding in the world is warm as the spring sun’s self-harmony, so those who receive its moisture do not give thanks; chill as the autumn frost’s self-descent, so those who wither do not bear resentment. The sage has no joy—simply nothing obstructs, and things naturally flow. Utmost benevolence has no favorites, entrusting principle to preserve itself. One who follows Heaven’s timing does not compare to one who forgets timing and naturally accords with it. Unable to unify the paths of right and wrong and pursue benefit while avoiding harm, one injures virtue and burdens what is fitting.

A true scholar abandons fame and attains the self, so fame matches reality and fortune responds to the person. Losing one’s nature and forcing compliance with things brings much servitude—how can one serve others? These all abandon the self to imitate others, following them to harm the self.

The true person of old bore righteousness yet did not form cliques, seemed insufficient yet did not submit; was angular yet not rigid, expansive in emptiness yet not ostentatious; seemed joyful, compelled as if by necessity, rich in bestowing countenance, restraining with virtue, broad as if worldly, towering and uncontrollable, connected as if fond of seclusion, forgetful of words.

Taking punishment as the body, ritual as wings, knowledge as timing, and virtue as the path: taking punishment as the body is the ease of execution; taking ritual as wings is how one moves through the world; taking knowledge as timing is acting only when unavoidable; taking virtue as the path means accompanying those with feet to the hill, yet others truly believe them to be diligent in conduct.

Thus what they like is one, what they dislike is one. Their oneness is one, their non-oneness is one. Their oneness joins them with Heaven; their non-oneness joins them with others. Heaven and others do not overcome one another—this is called the true person. They accord with things yet form no factions.

Empty and without excess, as if insufficient; yielding yet nothing is not elevated, seeming insufficient yet not submitting. They wander alone yet do not cling. Vast and without attachment, they reach fulfillment. The utmost person has no joy, yet flows in harmony, thus seems joyful. In movement and stillness, action and rest, they always dwell in the utmost of necessity, not allowing things to harm the self. They have no direction.

The utmost person has no harm, moving with the world, thus seems harmful. Lofty and self-content, far-reaching and profound, none see their gate. Unknowing and unperceiving, Heaven’s mechanism moves of itself, thus they are forgetful of words. Ritual is how the world moves itself—it is not of my making. Knowledge is the movement of the times—it is not of my initiation. Virtue is what others follow—it is not of my creation.

Entrusting governance to its natural execution, thus though it kills, it is lenient. Following what the world does, thus nothing is not done. High and low receive one another—this is an irreversible flow; small and great form groups. This is the momentum of necessity. Vast and without emotion, it is the repository of all knowledge.

Bearing the convergence of a hundred streams, dwelling at the teacher of people’s utmost—what is there to do? Entrusting to the world’s knowledge, committing to what must be, yielding to the world alone. The hill is the root; speaking of nature, it is nature’s root. Things each have what is sufficient, sufficient in the root. Entrusting to the self-following of all virtues, this accompanies those with feet to the root. When the root is reached, principle is exhausted.

All this is completed by others, not by oneself. Thus though dwelling at the utmost of ten thousand affairs, one is always at leisure and self-content, suddenly unaware of affairs passing through the body, forgetful of words in the mouth. Yet people’s great delusion is truly believing the utmost person to be diligent in conduct.

Always without intent and following others, thus liking and disliking, approving and disapproving, are no different from others. Their oneness is Heaven’s companion; their non-oneness is others’ companion. The true person unites Heaven and humanity, equalizes self and other, not allowing their oneness to differ from non-oneness. Having nothing that is not one—this is Heaven. The true person unites Heaven and humanity, levels the myriad outcomes. The myriad outcomes do not oppose one another; Heaven and humanity do not overcome one another. Thus they are vast and without non-oneness, dark and without absence, mysteriously one with others.

Death and life are fate; their constancy of night and day is Heaven. What humans cannot partake in are all the conditions of things. They take Heaven as father and still love the self—how much more that which transcends!

Man merely takes having a lord as better than himself, yet still dies for him—how much more that which is true! The constancy of day and night is the Dao of Heaven. Thus to know that death and life are the ultimate of fate, not some random occurrence, is like night and day—what is there to cling to? The true person in daytime gains day, in nighttime gains night. Taking death and life as day and night—how could there be anything unattained! That humans have what they cannot attain and harbor sorrow or joy in their hearts is merely the condition of things, not principle. The transcendent is called "solitary transformation." The achievement of mutual dependence is not as great as the perfection of solitary transformation. Thus what humans depend upon is Heaven; what Heaven gives birth to is solitary transformation. All take Heaven as father, so even the changes of day and night, the seasons of cold and heat, they dare not resent, but follow Heaven and find peace in it. How much more the transcendent solitary transformation, reaching the realm of profound darkness—how could one not entrust oneself to it! Having entrusted oneself, then death and life, transformation, only follow fate.

That which is true borrows nothing from things but is natural. The unavoidability of the natural—how could it be merely the command of a lord! When a spring dries up, fish find themselves together on land, blowing moisture on one another, moistening one another with foam—better to forget one another in rivers and lakes. Rather than praising Yao and condemning Jie, better to forget both and transform through the Dao. Rather than loving one another in insufficiency, how much better to have abundance and forget one another! Praise and blame both arise from insufficiency. Thus the utterly sufficient forgets good and evil, leaves behind death and life, unites with transformation, vast and without obstruction.

Hiding a boat in a ravine, hiding a mountain in a marsh—one calls this secure! Yet in the dead of night, a strong man carries it away, and the deluded do not know. Hiding the small and large as is fitting still allows for escape. But to hide the world within the world and find no escape—this is the great truth of things. Merely possessing a human form and still delighting in it—if the human form is but one encounter among ten thousand transformations, never beginning or ending, how could its joy be measured? Thus the sage roams where things cannot escape and all are preserved. Excelling in early death, excelling in old age, excelling in beginning, excelling in end—people still emulate this, how much more that to which the myriad things are tied, that upon which the one transformation depends!

Form, birth, aging, death—all are I. Thus form carries me, birth labors me, aging rests me, death gives me repose. Though these four change, they have never ceased to be I—why should I惜 them? Death and life are both fate. Without goodness, there is death; with goodness, there is life—not goodness alone. If one takes my life as good, then my death is also good. Speaking of the inescapability of death, life, and transformation, one first presents the ultimate of inescapability, then clarifies it with the sign of inevitable change, to entrust oneself to transformation without attachment.

The power of powerlessness is greatest in transformation. Thus it lifts Heaven and Earth to pursue the new, shoulders mountains and ridges to discard the old. Without the slightest pause, in an instant it has traversed the new—thus Heaven and Earth, the myriad things, have no time when they do not shift. The world is all new, yet one takes it as old; the boat changes daily, yet one sees it as before; the mountain alters daily, yet one sees it as the same. Now one clasps an arm and loses it—all has departed in darkness. Thus the I of a moment ago is no longer the I of now. I and the present both pass away—how could one constantly guard the old! Yet the world does not perceive this, stubbornly claiming that what one encounters now can be tied down and retained—how deluded!

Having nothing to hide and entrusting all, one is without obstruction in things, without division in transformation. Thus there is no outer, no inner, no death, no life, embodying Heaven and Earth and uniting with transformation—seeking escape yet finding none. This is the great truth of constant existence, not some partial, minor intention. The human form is but one encounter among ten thousand transformations—insufficient to delight in alone. Within the boundless, what one encounters is all like the human form—how could only the human form be delightful while other things have no joy!

The Dao has essence and trustworthiness, is without action and without form; it can be transmitted but not received, can be attained but not seen. It is self-originating, self-rooted, existing before Heaven and Earth, enduring since ancient times. It spiritually empowers ghosts and gods, gives birth to Heaven and Earth. Before the Great Ultimate, it is not high; below the Six Poles, it is not deep. Before Heaven and Earth were born, it was not long-lasting; older than high antiquity, it is not aged. Fu Xi obtained it to grasp Heaven and Earth; Fuxi obtained it to inherit the Mother of Qi; the Northern Dipper obtained it and never errs; the sun and moon obtained it and never cease; Kanpi obtained it to inherit Kunlun; Feng Yi obtained it to roam great rivers; Jian Wu obtained it to dwell in great mountains; the Yellow Emperor obtained it to ascend the cloudy heavens; Zhuan Xu obtained it to dwell in the Dark Palace; Yu Qiang obtained it to stand at the North Pole; the Queen Mother of the West obtained it to sit in Shaoguang, knowing neither beginning nor end; Peng Zu obtained it, reaching from the time of Youyu to the Five Hegemons; Fu Yue obtained it to assist Wu Ding, encompassing the world, riding the Eastern Ridge and the Tail of the Winnowing Basket to join the ranks of the stars.

There exists an emotion beyond emotion, thus the practice of non-action; there is a constancy beyond constancy, thus the absence of form. From antiquity to the present, it has been transmitted and dwelt within, yet none can possess it. All find their own accommodation in it, yet none perceive its form. Illumination requires nothing, for it is not dependent on existence to be non-existent.

How could the non-existent give birth to the numinous? It does not render ghosts and emperors divine, yet ghosts and emperors become divine of themselves—this is the numinosity of non-numinosity. It does not generate Heaven and Earth, yet Heaven and Earth arise of themselves—this is the generation of non-generation. Thus, the numinosity of the numinous is insufficient to be truly numinous; only in non-numinosity does true numinosity reside. What achievement is there to claim? What endeavor is there to rely upon?

To speak of the Dao’s omnipresence: when it is on high, it is without height; when it is profound, it is without depth; when it endures, it is without duration; when it ages, it is without aging. It is present everywhere, yet wherever it is present, it is as nothing. Moreover, that which pervades above and below cannot be described in terms of high or low; that which reaches within and without cannot be named as inner or outer; that which moves in unison with transformation cannot be called enduring; that which is eternally without beginning or end cannot be termed old.

The Dao is without agency. This speaks of attaining it through the Dao, yet it is only to clarify that such attainment is self-attained. Being self-attained, the Dao cannot cause one to attain it; if I have not attained it, I cannot force attainment. Thus, all who attain it do not rely on the Dao externally nor depend on themselves internally—they suddenly attain it of themselves and transform alone.

The difficulty of life is like this self-transformation and self-attainment. Having attained life, why then fret over whether life is attained and strive for it? Thus, striving for life is insufficient to preserve life fully, for life does not arise from one’s own doing—striving for it only harms one’s true life.

Nanbo Zikui asked Nü Yu, “You are advanced in years, yet your complexion is like that of a child. How is this?” Nü Yu replied, “I have heard the Dao.”

Having heard the Dao, one allows life to arise of itself—thus one’s qi and complexion remain whole.”

Nanbo Zikui said, “Can the Dao be learned?”
“Alas! How could it be? You are not the right person. Now, Pulam Yi possesses the talent of a sage but not the Dao of a sage, while I possess the Dao of a sage but not the talent of a sage. I wished to teach him, hoping he might truly become a sage. If not, even imparting the Dao of a sage to one with the talent of a sage would be easy. Yet I persisted in instructing him: after three days, he was able to let go of the world; having let go of the world, I persisted further, and after seven days, he was able to let go of things; having let go of things, I persisted again, and after nine days, he was able to let go of life. Only then could he attain clarity at dawn; with clarity at dawn, he could perceive the solitary; perceiving the solitary, he could transcend past and present; transcending past and present, he could enter the realm of neither death nor life. Those who kill life do not die; those who give life to life do not live. As for this state—nothing does it not escort, nothing does it not welcome, nothing does it not destroy, nothing does it not complete. Its name is *yingning* (serene amidst entanglement). *Yingning* is that which is completed through entanglement.”

*Wai* (letting go) means to abandon. Things are what one requires morning and night, difficult to forget due to their immediacy. To abandon them entirely is to let go of life, and thus not to abhor death. Not abhorring death, one finds peace in whatever arises, suddenly free from obstruction, acting in accord with the moment—this is clarity at dawn. Moving in unison with the solitary, one who clings to life thus invites death, and one who abhors death thus invites life. Only by being free from clinging and aversion can one transcend death and life. Allowing things to escort themselves, nothing is not escorted; allowing things to welcome themselves, nothing is not welcomed; allowing things to destroy themselves, nothing is not destroyed; allowing things to complete themselves, nothing is not completed. One who merges with things is entangled by them yet remains undisturbed.

Nanbo Zikui said, “From whom did you alone hear this?”
He replied, “I heard it from the disciple of *Fumo*, the disciple of *Fumo* heard it from the grandson of *Luosong*, the grandson of *Luosong* heard it from *Zhanming*, *Zhanming* heard it from *Nieshu*, *Nieshu* heard it from *Xuyi*, *Xuyi* heard it from *Yu’ou*, *Yu’ou* heard it from *Xuanming*, *Xuanming* heard it from *Canliao*, and *Canliao* heard it from *Yishi*.”

If things entangle yet one alone remains unentangled, one is ruined.

Thus, when entangled yet allowing it, nothing fails to be bent into completion.

*Xuanming* is so named because it is "without" yet not "nothing." One who ascends by names to reach the nameless must attain the nameless beyond the realm of names. Therefore, even *Xuanming* does not reach the ultimate, and so it is further entrusted to *Canliao*—this is the mystery within mystery.

The principle of spontaneity is such that it is formed through accumulated practice. Indeed, one ascends from the near to the far, refines from the coarse to the subtle, and so only after seven layers does one reach the name of "without," and after nine layers does one doubt that "without" is the beginning.

Zisi, Ziyu, Zili, and Zilai spoke among themselves, saying: "Who can take the absence of being as the head, life as the spine, and death as the tailbone? Who knows that death and life, existence and nonexistence, are of one body—we shall befriend them!" The four looked at one another and smiled, their hearts in perfect accord, and so they became friends.

Soon after, Ziyu fell ill, and Zisi went to visit him. He said: "How wondrous! The Creator is about to make me like this—all crooked and bent."

The man’s back was hunched, his spine protruded, his five organs pressed upward, his chin tucked into his navel, his shoulders towered above his crown, and his goiter pointed to the sky—his yin and yang qi had fallen into disorder. Yet his heart remained at ease, untroubled. He paced to the well, gazed at his reflection, and sighed: "Alas! The Creator is about to make me like this—all crooked and bent."

*Disorder here means chaos or disharmony.*

He did not take it as suffering. For one who entrusts himself to the transformations of spontaneity, there is no lament—only the world laments on his behalf.

Zisi asked: "Do you resent this?"

He replied: "No, why should I resent it? If, little by little, my left arm were transformed into a rooster, I would use it to herald the dawn. If, little by little, my right arm were transformed into a crossbow, I would use it to hunt an owl for roasting. If, little by little, my tailbone were transformed into a wheel, and my spirit into a horse, I would ride it—why would I need another steed?

Moreover, to gain is a matter of timing; to lose is a matter of following the natural course. At peace with the time and in accord with the course, sorrow and joy cannot enter. This is what the ancients called *suspended release*—yet those who cannot release themselves are bound by things. Besides, things have long been unable to overcome Heaven. Why should I resent anything?"

"Jìn" means gradual. When the body undergoes transformation and merges with change, there is nowhere it does not follow, and nothing it follows that is impermissible. What one encounters in time, the world calls *gaining*. Time does not pause even briefly; to follow it as it departs, the world calls *losing*. If one cannot release oneself, then the myriad things bind one together. Thus, those who can release themselves are released from all things, while those who cannot are released from nothing.

Heaven cannot be without day and night; how can I be without death and life and resent it? Soon after, Zilai fell ill, gasping as if near death. His wife and children gathered around, weeping. Zili went to visit him and said: "Shoo! Away! Do not be startled by transformation!" Leaning against the doorframe, he spoke to him: "How wondrous is the Creator! What will it make of you next? Where will it send you? Will it make you into a mouse’s liver? Will it make you into an insect’s limb?"

Death and life are like waking and sleeping. By principle, one ought to sleep, and one does not wish to be startled awake. To transform and die is fitting—there is no cause for alarm. Zilai said: "For parents and children, east, west, south, or north, one follows their command. Yin and yang are to man no less than parents. If they approach my death and I do not heed them, I would be obstinate—what fault would lie with them? The great clod bears me with form, wearies me with life, eases me with age, and rests me with death. Thus, to treat my life well is to treat my death well.

Now, if a great smith were casting metal, and the metal leapt up, saying, ‘I must become a *Mo Ye* sword!’ the smith would surely deem it inauspicious metal. Now, if one assumes human form and says, ‘I am human! I am human!’ the Creator would surely deem it an inauspicious person. If one takes heaven and earth as a great furnace and the Creator as a great smith, where could one go and not be acceptable?" He fell asleep peacefully and awoke with a start.

Since ancient times, some have been able to defy their parents’ commands, but none have been able to defy the changes of yin and yang or resist the cycles of day and night. Death and life are like day and night—nothing so distant. When the time for death comes, it is not something to forbid; yet to stubbornly harbor a defiant heart only hastens one’s end by resisting principle. The swiftness of death arises from one’s own obstinacy, not from death itself. *That* refers to death; in life, one thus regards death as *that*. Principle is ever-present in both.

"I am human! I am human!"—one only wishes to remain human. It is like the metal leaping up: the world knows such metal is inauspicious, yet cannot allow it to transform of its own accord. The Way of transformation and change encounters nothing it does not meet. Now, if one encounters human form, was it done deliberately? Life is not deliberate; it arises of itself in time. To strive to possess it is delusion!

People all know that metal bound by attachments is inauspicious. Thus, if one realizes one’s own nature is no different from metal, the attachments of the heart can be released. If they can be released, there is nothing that cannot be released. Waking and sleeping remain as they are, and one’s heart is not burdened by life and death.

Zisang Hu, Mengzi Fan, and Ziqin Zhang spoke among themselves: "Who can join together without joining, act for one another without acting? Who can ascend to heaven and wander in the mists, roam without limit, forget life, and have no end?" Those who embody heaven and earth and darkly merge with transformation—though their limbs have different functions and their five organs distinct offices—never join together, yet their hundred joints harmonize as one. This is joining without joining. They never act for one another, yet inner and outer are both fulfilled. This is acting without acting.

If one were to exert one’s mind to care for one’s limbs, or move one’s thighs and arms to manage one’s organs, the more one strives to nurture them, the more inner and outer become exhausted. Thus, those who take the world as one body have no partiality in their care. They entrust all things without exception. Forgetting life, they forget all things, and thus can follow change and entrust transformation, all without limit.

The three looked at one another and laughed, their hearts in accord. They became friends. Yet was this truly friendship? It was but a lodging of luminous intimacy, free from the nearness of affectionate attachment.

After a while, Zisang Hu died, his body yet unburied. When Confucius heard of it, he sent Zigong to attend to the rites. Some wove funeral garlands, others played the zither, and together they sang in harmony: *"Alas, Zisang Hu! Alas, Zisang Hu! You have returned to your true nature, while we remain as men!"* Zigong hastened forward and said, *"May I ask—is it proper to sing before the dead?"*

To weep when others weep is but the trace of custom. To equalize life and death, to forget grief and joy, to sing before the dead—this is the utmost of what lies beyond the world. The two looked at each other and laughed: *"How could he know the meaning of ritual!"*

One who knows the meaning of ritual must wander beyond to order what is within, guard the mother to preserve the child, and follow the truth of feeling without deviation. If one is bound by reputation and constrained by form, then filial piety cannot bear sincerity, kindness cannot bear reality, and father and son, brothers, harbor feelings and deceive one another—how could this be the great meaning of ritual?

Zigong returned and told Confucius: *"What manner of men are they? They cultivate no conduct, yet treat their bodies as external. They sing before the dead without change of countenance—there is no name for them. What manner of men are they?"*

Confucius said: *"They wander beyond the world, while I wander within it. Beyond and within do not reach one another, and yet I sent you to offer condolences—how shallow I was! They are now companions of the Creator, roaming in the single qi of heaven and earth. To them, life is but an excrescent growth, a hanging tumor; death is but the bursting of an abscess, the draining of a sore. How could such as they know where life and death, before and after, reside? Borrowing from different things, they lodge in the same body; forgetting their liver and gall, they abandon their ears and eyes. They turn and return, beginning and ending, without knowing where it starts or ends. Vaguely they wander beyond the dust and grime, carefree in the work of non-action. How could they trouble themselves with the rituals of the vulgar world to impress the eyes and ears of the crowd?"*

Zigong said: *"Then what Dao do you follow?"*

The principle has an ultimate limit, where beyond and within merge. There has never been one who attained the utmost of wandering beyond yet did not merge within, nor one who merged within yet did not wander beyond. Thus, the sage always wanders beyond to merge within, is without mind to follow what is, and so though all day long he appears in form, his spirit and qi remain unchanged. Though he bends and rises with the myriad affairs, he is serene and at ease. To see form yet not reach spirit—this is the common burden of the world.

Thus, when one sees him moving with all things, none can say he abandons things and departs from men; when one sees him responding to affairs while embodying transformation, none can say he sits in forgetfulness and attains self-possession. How could it be only the sage who is not thus? It must be said that the ultimate principle has no such thing.

Therefore, Zhuangzi, in clarifying the lineage of the great tradition to awaken the world, did not simply praise Confucius in this way. Had he done so, some might have clung to what they saw and rejected it. Thus, he transcended the sage’s inner traces and lodged the beyond in those few disciples. One should forget what is lodged to seek the great meaning of his composition. Then the Dao of wandering beyond to merge within will be clear and self-evident, and Zhuangzi’s book will indeed be discourse that crosses the vulgar and covers the world.

Offering condolences is a minor affair of the world within; to apply it to the world beyond is shallow. All are merged, thus there is no duality. Like a tumor that hangs of itself, a growth that attaches of itself—this is the temporary gathering of qi, not what one delights in. Like an abscess that bursts of itself, a sore that drains of itself—this is the natural dispersal of qi, not what one regrets. Life and death alternate without end; if one goes along with them, there is nowhere one cannot go. Thus, one does not know where victory or defeat lies.

*"Assuming"* means *"relying on."* Now life and death, gathering and dispersal, change without pattern—all are different things. There is nothing different that is not relied upon, so though what is relied upon differs, all together form one body. If even the five organs are forgotten, what thing is worth recognizing? Without recognition from the start, one can freely follow the path of transformation, darkly merge with the waves of turning and returning, and not know where beginning and end reach their limit.

This is called *"wandering in principle and merging with what is beyond."* The *"work of non-action"* is not mere folded hands and silence; *"beyond the dust and grime"* is not hiding in the mountains and forests. What they show to the crowd is but the dust and grime—it is not the dark merging of what lies beyond.

Zigong had not heard of *"nature and the Dao of heaven,"* so he saw what they relied upon but not what they relied upon *for.* What they relied upon *for* is not reliance—how could the world be aware of it?

Confucius said: *"I, Qiu, am one punished by heaven. Nevertheless, I share this with you."*

To take what lies within the realm as shackles is to clarify that what is prized lies beyond it. Those who wander beyond rely on what is within; those who depart from humanity conform to custom. Thus, those who possess the world do not take the world as their possession. Only by relinquishing things can one enter the multitude; only by sitting in forgetfulness can one respond to affairs. The more one relinquishes, the more one attains. Should one abide in this ultimate state, then even if one wished to cast it aside, principle would naturally come of its own—this is what neither heaven nor humanity can pardon.

Zigong said: *"May I ask the method?"*
Though bound by the world’s shackles, it is but to share this with you. It is clear that I myself ever abide beyond.

Confucius said: *"Fish come together in water; humans come together in the Dao. Those who come together in water dig ponds to be nourished and provided for; those who come together in the Dao are without affairs and attain tranquility. Thus it is said: Fish forget one another in rivers and lakes; humans forget one another in the arts of the Dao."*

Asking the meaning of wandering beyond while sharing what is within, Confucius said: *"Fish come together in water; humans come together in the Dao. Those who come together in water dig ponds to be nourished and provided for; those who come together in the Dao are without affairs and attain tranquility. Thus it is said: Fish forget one another in rivers and lakes; humans forget one another in the arts of the Dao."*

Zigong said: *"May I ask about the extraordinary person?"*

Those who come together in different ways—whether in water or in the Dao—all alike attain their ends through non-action, unite what is within by wandering beyond form, and thus find nourishment and tranquility. Yet none are aware of this, and so they achieve effortless action.

It is said: *"The extraordinary person is extraordinary among humans yet equal to Heaven. Thus, Heaven’s petty persons are humanity’s noble ones; humanity’s noble ones are Heaven’s petty persons."*

Where each is self-sufficient and forgets the other, nothing in the world is otherwise. The perfected person is ever self-sufficient, and so ever forgetful.

Someone asked: *"Where then lies what was earlier called ‘wandering beyond form and unyoked to custom’?"*

To be inwardly obscure is to wander outwardly. Only one who can wander outwardly while inwardly obscure, allowing the spontaneity of the myriad things and enabling each to fulfill its Heaven-given nature, brings the Dao of emperors and kings to completion—this is to be extraordinary among humans yet equal to Heaven.

Speaking in terms of spontaneity, there is no small or great among humans. Speaking in terms of human principles, one who is equal to Heaven may be called a noble person.

Yan Hui asked Confucius: *"Mengsun Cai’s mother died. He wept without tears, his heart felt no grief, and he did not mourn with sorrow. Lacking these three, he was nonetheless deemed the best at mourning in all Lu. Is it truly possible to gain a reputation without substance?"* Yan Hui found this deeply strange.

In Lu, they observed his ritual conduct, while Yan Hui examined his heart. Confucius said: *"Mengsun Cai has exhausted it—he has advanced beyond knowledge. Though he sought simplicity, he could not attain it, for he had already attained a kind of simplicity. Mengsun Cai knows not why he lives, nor why he dies. He knows not whether to follow what comes first or what comes after. If he is transformed into a thing, he awaits the transformations he does not yet know. Moreover, as he is in the midst of transformation, how could he know what is not yet transformed? As he is in the midst of non-transformation, how could he know what has already transformed? You and I are but dreamers who have not yet awakened!

Yet he may take on startled forms without harming his heart, dwell in new abodes without depleting his essence. Mengsun Cai alone is awake—others weep, and so he weeps, for that is simply what he must do. Moreover, we merely share the same ears—how do we know that what I call 'I' is not truly 'I'? You may dream of being a bird soaring through the heavens, or a fish diving into the depths. Who can say whether the speaker now is awake or dreaming? One reaches contentment before laughter arises; one offers laughter before arranging it. Resting in arrangement, one moves with transformation and enters the vast oneness of Heaven."*

One who fully grasps the principle of life and death, responding fittingly to both inner and outer, moves in accordance with Heaven’s course—this is no match for knowledge. To select between life and death yet find no difference is like the progression of spring, autumn, winter, and summer. Having made a selection yet found nothing, one is at ease in all things. Being at ease in all things, one does not weigh life and death in the mind but entrusts them to spontaneous transformation. Wherever one encounters, one is content. One does not resist transformation.

Life and death are but twists in the same flow, merging with transformation. One forgets what one knows in the present—how could one fret over what one does not yet know? Having transformed and come to life, how could one know the time before birth? Not yet transformed and facing death, how could one know what comes after? Thus, there is nothing to avoid or pursue—one simply moves with transformation. Life and death are but waking and dreaming. If in a dream one believes oneself awake, there is no way to prove that waking is not a dream. If one cannot prove that waking is not a dream, then one cannot prove that life is not death.

Life and death, waking and dreaming—where do they reside? In whatever one encounters, one finds contentment. Why grieve over this while worrying about that? Transformation startles the form, but it does not burden the heart. The changes of the bodily frame are but daily renovations of one’s dwelling—one’s nature does not take them as death. The one who is always awake meets no resistance in any direction, so when others weep, he weeps, for that is simply what is fitting.

Life, death, and transformation—all are mine. Since all are mine, what have I lost? Never having lost myself, what cause for sorrow? Without resistance, when others weep, he weeps. Without sorrow, though he weeps, he is not grieved. Nothing is not mine, so he harmonizes inner and outer, pervades past and present, and renews daily with transformation—how could he know where his true self resides? This means there is nowhere one does not find contentment.

When dreaming, one believes oneself awake—how then can one know that the present is not a dream? How can one know it is not waking? The transformations of waking and dreaming extend everywhere without hindrance, so the changes of life and death are never worth lamenting. Wherever one arrives is fitting, so one forgets fitting—thus, laughter does not arise. *Pai* means to shift or move. In ritual, weeping must express sorrow, and laughter must express joy. If sorrow and joy linger in the heart, one cannot move with what is fitting.

But Mengsun is always at ease, so though he weeps, he is not grieved, and he moves with transformation. At peace with shifting, he moves with transformation and thus enters the silence to become one with Heaven. From this point up to Zisi, their attainment is the same. Their approaches to mourning differ, so their songs and weeping vary.

Yi’erzi went to see Xu You. Xu You asked: *"How has Yao aided you?"* *Zi* here means to supply or assist. Yi’erzi replied: *"Yao told me: 'You must personally practice benevolence and righteousness and clearly distinguish right from wrong.'" Xu You said: *"Then why have you come here? Yao has already branded you with benevolence and righteousness and mutilated you with right and wrong. How will you wander the boundless, carefree, and ever-shifting paths?"*

He speaks of how one will mutilate and diminish oneself through penal teachings, unable to wander again in the realm of self-attainment or the path unbound by ties. Yi’erzi said: *"Even so, I wish to wander within its fence."* He dared not seek to tread the central Way, but wished only to wander along its edges. Xu You said: *"Not so. The blind have no part in the beauty of brows and eyes or the radiance of countenance; the deaf have no part in the splendor of blue and yellow, of embroidered robes and patterned silks."*

Yi’erzi said: *"Wu Zhuang lost her beauty, Ju Liang lost his strength, and the Yellow Emperor lost his wisdom—all within the forge and hammer’s reach. How do we know the Maker of Things will not cease my branding and mend my mutilation, allowing me to mount completion and follow you, Master?"* He speaks of how not all things in the world necessarily perfect themselves; the principle of spontaneity also requires smelting and forging to become vessels. Thus, these three figures, too, having heard the Dao, afterward forgot their former pursuits. These are all borrowed words, meant to dispel the burdens of mere verbal constructs.

Those who follow their nature and proceed directly act spontaneously; if they proceed and harm their nature, and having harmed it, can change—this, too, is spontaneity. How do we know that my nature is not fated to have its branding ceased and its mutilation mended, allowing me to mount the Way of completion and follow you? To cast me aside and refuse instruction—this may not be the utmost of the Maker of Things.

Xu You said: *"Alas! It cannot be known."*

"Let me speak to you in broad strokes: O my master! O my master! He pulverizes the myriad things yet does not act for righteousness; his beneficence extends to ten thousand generations yet does not act for benevolence. He is older than high antiquity yet does not act for age; he covers and bears heaven and earth, carves and sculpts the forms of all things yet does not act for skill. This is the realm in which he roams!"

All things are thus of themselves, with no preference or deliberate action in their midst—where then could benevolence and righteousness reside? They are ever-renewing. It is spontaneous, and thus not skillful. To roam in non-action and take as master the masterless.

Yan Hui said: "I have advanced."
By taking reduction as advancement.
Zhongni said: "What do you mean?"
He replied: "I have forgotten rites and music!"
He said: "Good, but not yet sufficient."

The benevolent leave traces of universal love; the righteous achieve the work of completing things. Love is not benevolence, yet the traces of benevolence are enacted; completion is not righteousness, yet the work of righteousness appears. To retain benevolence and righteousness is insufficient to understand that the origins of love and benefit lie in non-intention—thus, to forget them is permissible. But merely forgetting their traces and works still falls short of profound penetration.

Another day, he again saw him and said: "I have advanced."
He asked: "What do you mean?"
He replied: "I have forgotten benevolence and righteousness."
He said: "Good, but not yet sufficient."

Rites are the functions of the physical form; music is the instrument of joy in life. To forget the instrument is not as profound as forgetting that which enables the instrument.

Another day, he again saw him and said: "You have advanced!"
He asked: "What do you mean?"
He replied: "I have attained *sitting in forgetfulness*."
Confucius started and said: "What do you mean by *sitting in forgetfulness*?"
Yan Hui said: "I discard my limbs and body, dismiss my hearing and sight, detach from form and abandon knowledge, and merge with the Great Thoroughfare—this is called *sitting in forgetfulness*."
When nothing is not the same, there is never discontent; when there is never discontent, what preferences or aversions could there be?

Confucius said: "To be the same is to have no preferences; to transform is to have no constancy. And you are indeed worthy! I, Qiu, would follow after you."
One who is the same as transformation follows only where transformation leads—thus there is no constancy.

Ziyu and Zisang were friends. When continuous rain fell for ten days, Ziyu said: "Zisang is likely in distress!" He wrapped rice and went to feed him. Arriving at Zisang’s gate, he heard sounds like singing, like weeping, as Zisang plucked his zither and cried:
"Father! Mother! Heaven!"

"Humanity indeed!"—yet his voice could not bear the strain, so he hastened to recite his verse.

Ziyu entered and asked: "Why do you sing this poem in such a manner?"

These two were such that they acted for one another without acting for one another. Now, wrapping rice to feed him was but following Heaven’s principle and acting spontaneously—it was not that he went because of acting for him. To suspect the presence of emotion is to rush forth and depart far from the principle.

He said: "I ponder what has brought me to this extremity, yet I cannot find it. Would my parents wish me to be poor? Heaven covers all without partiality; Earth bears all without partiality—would Heaven and Earth single me out for poverty? I seek what has caused this, yet I cannot find it! Yet to have reached this extremity is fate indeed!"

All things are thus of themselves; there is no doer behind them.

---

*From the "Inner Chapters" of the Zhuangzi, "The Equalizing Lord of the World" (Chapter Seven):*

Nie Que questioned Wang Ni four times, and four times Wang Ni professed ignorance. Thereupon Nie Que leapt with great joy and went to tell Master Pu Yi.

Master Pu Yi said: "Have you only now come to know this? The Lord of Yu did not attain to the level of the Lord of Tai. The Lord of Yu concealed benevolence to entice men—thus he gained men, yet never emerged from the realm of the unnatural. The Lord of Tai slept in tranquil ease and awoke in leisurely fashion. At times he took himself for a horse, at times for an ox. His knowledge was true and his virtue most genuine, yet he never entered the realm of the unnatural."

One who is without intent and follows spontaneous transformation is fit to be an emperor-king. The Lord of Yu and the Lord of Tai were but traces of worldly affairs, not that by which traces are made. That by which traces are made is traceless—who in the world could name it? Never having been named, what victory or defeat could there be? Yet the traceless one rides the myriad changes, treading through ten thousand ages. The world has its level plains and perilous passes, thus traces sometimes fall short.

One who takes what he likes as the "right" person and what he dislikes as the "wrong" person is confined within the bounds of right and wrong. One who can emerge from the realm of the unnatural must enter the realm where there is no unnatural. Thus there is no gain, no loss, no right, no wrong—how could it be merely concealing benevolence to entice men? Being thus, what right or wrong persons could there be? This may be called emerging from the realm of the unnatural.

Following one’s own knowledge, thus emotions are true. Following one’s own attainment, thus there is no falsity. Not entering the realm of right and wrong is how one cuts off from the age of the Lord of Yu.

---

Jian Wu met Jie Yu the Madman.

Jie Yu asked: "What did Zhong Shi tell you at noon?"

Jian Wu replied: "He told me that a ruler of men must issue laws, standards, principles, and measures from himself—who would dare not listen and be transformed?"

Jie Yu said: "This is deceptive virtue. In governing the world, it is like crossing the sea to dig a river or making a mosquito carry a mountain. Does the sage govern externally? He rectifies himself and then acts, ensuring only that he is capable of his task. Moreover, birds fly high to avoid the harm of arrows and nets; the field mouse burrows deep beneath the sacred mound to escape the peril of fumigation and digging—yet do these two creatures lack knowledge?"

To impose one’s self upon things is to cause things to lose their truth.

To entrust all to the myriad things is to accomplish without action; to impose one’s self upon the world is to leave no task complete and no burden borne. Preserve only what lies within one’s nature and allotted span. Each rectifies its own nature and destiny. Do not attempt what one cannot. Even birds and beasts each have their means of self-preservation—thus when emperors and kings leave them to their own, they fulfill themselves. Have you truly not known that these two creatures each preserve themselves without need of instruction?

Tian Gen wandered to Yin Yang and arrived at the banks of Liao River, where he encountered the Nameless Man and asked: “I beg to ask how to govern the world.” The Nameless Man replied: “Away! You are a vulgar man—why do you ask so unpleasantly? I am about to become human with the Creator of Things. When weary, I shall ride the boundless bird to soar beyond the six directions and wander the land of Nothing Whatever, dwelling in the vast wilderness. Why trouble my heart with your questions of governing the world?”

To ask how to govern the world is not to rise from the Great Beginning nor to rest in the Dark and Obscure. Let people act of themselves. "Boundless" means the myriad fragments. Riding the myriad fragments, driving the ten thousand things, one moves freely without constraint or obstruction. To release all into the realm of self-attainment is to govern without governing.

He asked the Nameless Man again: “If you let your mind wander in simplicity, harmonize your qi with stillness, follow things as they are without private intent, then the world will be governed.” To follow one’s nature without adornment is simplicity. To rest in stillness within one’s nature is stillness. To let nature arise of itself is impartiality; to seek to augment it with the heart’s desires is partiality. Partiality cannot sustain life, but following impartiality preserves it wholly.

Yang Ziju saw Lao Dan and said: “Suppose there were a man swift, strong, and resolute, with penetrating insight and clear understanding, who studies the Dao without weariness—could such a one be compared to an enlightened king?” Lao Dan replied: “In the eyes of the sage, such a man is like a clerk or artisan, bound by skill and toil, exhausting his body and startling his mind. Moreover, the patterns of tigers and leopards invite hunting; the agility of apes and monkeys invites capture. Could such a one be compared to an enlightened king?”

Yang Ziju, disconcerted, said: “I venture to ask about the governance of an enlightened king.” Lao Dan said: “Such skill leaves no room for the self—how could it compare to a sage-king? The governance of an enlightened king: his achievements cover the world yet seem not his own; he transforms and bestows upon the ten thousand things, yet the people do not rely on him. No name is raised, and things delight of themselves. He stands in the unfathomable and wanders where there is nothing.”

If the world had no enlightened king, none could attain self-fulfillment. That they do so is truly the king’s achievement—yet his achievement lies in non-action and entrusting the world to itself. When all in the world act of themselves, it seems not the king’s doing. The enlightened king fully meets the nature of things, so each person says, “I am thus of myself,” without knowing their reliance on him. Though his achievements cover the world, he does not claim them as his name, so all things believe they have attained of themselves and rejoice. He dwells in the path of transformation, daily renewed without fixed form. Identifying with the ten thousand things, he wanders in emptiness. If one cannot merge with things, one is ever at odds with them—how could one wander in emptiness?

In Zheng there was a divine diviner named Ji Xian, who knew men’s deaths and lives, survival and ruin, fortune and misfortune, long life and early death, predicting the year, month, ten-day period, or day as if by spirit. When the people of Zheng saw him, they all fled. Liezi saw him and was enchanted. Returning, he told Huzhi: “At first I thought your Dao was supreme, but now there is something even greater.” He did not delight in hearing his own death day.

He said Ji Xian’s attainment surpassed even the Master’s. Huzhi replied: “I have shown you only its outward pattern, not yet its true substance. How could you have truly attained the Dao? A flock of hens without a rooster—what eggs could they lay? When you parade the Dao before the world with such certainty, you make yourself readable, and thus others can divine your fate. Come, bring him here and let him see me.”

This showed that Liezi had not yet embraced the Dao. Without embracing the Dao, one still has a mind; having a mind, one asserts one aspect to the exclusion of others, and thus becomes predictable to the world—hence others can divine one’s fate.

The next day, Liezi brought Ji Xian to see Huzhi. Upon leaving, Ji Xian said to Liezi: “Alas! Your master is dead! He will not live. It will not be counted in tens of days! I saw something strange—like damp ashes.”

Liezi entered, his tears soaking his lapels as he told Huzhi. Huzhi said: “Earlier I showed him the pattern of earth—still and unshaken, as if sprouting yet unmoving. This must be what he saw: my blocked virtue-mechanism. Come, bring him again.”

Still and unmoving as a sprout, neither correcting itself nor standing upright, sharing the barrenness of withered wood and the lifelessness of damp ashes—this is the moment when the perfected person is without responsiveness. The perfected person moves like heaven, rests like earth, flows like water, and abides in profound stillness. Profound stillness and flowing water, heavenly motion and earthly rest—though they seem not to act, they are spontaneously so, and in this they are one.

Now, when Ji Xian saw him dwelling like a corpse and sitting in oblivion, he declared him near death; when he glimpsed his spirit stirring and heaven following, he took it for signs of life. Truly, if one responds without engaging the heart-mind, the pattern naturally aligns with the mysterious tally; if one ascends and descends with transformations, taking the world as measure, then one becomes master of things, following the timeless without limit—thus beyond the diviner’s reckoning. This is the great meaning of "Responding to Emperors and Kings."

When the virtue-mechanism does not activate, it is called "blocked."

The next day, Liezi brought Ji Xian to see Huzhi again. Upon leaving, Ji Xian said to Liezi: “Fortunate indeed! Your master has met me—he is recovering! Fully alive now! I see his blocked mechanism has shifted!”

Liezi entered and told Huzhi. Huzhi said: “Earlier I showed him heaven and earth—names and realities did not enter, yet the mechanism arose from the heels. This must be what he saw: my good mechanism. Come, bring him again.”

*Shift* here means *mechanism*. Now he realizes that what he saw yesterday was the *blocked mechanism*, hence his declaration of impending death. Between heaven and earth, the work of covering and bearing is manifest—compared to the pattern of earth, is this not a step beyond? This is the appearance of responsive resonance. When one entrusts oneself to natural covering and bearing, the heavenly mechanism mysteriously responds, and the adornments of fame and profit all become discarded things. The mechanism always arises from the utmost height. When it activates and excels in the other, the other perceives it.

The next day, Liezi brought Ji Xian to see Huzhi again. Upon leaving, Ji Xian said to Liezi: “Your master is unsettled—I cannot divine him. Let him settle, and I will divine him again.”

Liezi entered and told Huzhi. Huzhi said: “Earlier I showed him the Great Vastness where nothing prevails—this must be what he saw: my balanced qi-mechanism. The whirlpool formed by the *ni* fish is a deep pool; the still water that forms a deep pool is a deep pool; the flowing water that forms a deep pool is a deep pool. There are nine names for deep pools—here I showed him three. Come, bring him again.”

Dwelling at the utmost height of the Great Vastness, one’s heart drifts in boundless tranquility and merges mystically with the ten thousand directions—thus victory and defeat cannot find place within it. There is no direction that is not level, all blending into oneness. Those who peer at heaven through a tube see no horizon, and so it seems uneven. The deep pool is but the name for stillness and silence.

Water is ever without intent, yielding and following external things. Thus, whether flowing or still, whether the *ni* fish coils or the dragon leaps, it remains profoundly self-possessed, never losing its stillness and silence. The perfected person acts when employing it and halts when setting it aside; though action and stillness differ, the mysterious silence remains one. Thus, I briefly presented three variations to clarify this.

Though the waves shift through nine transformations, and order and chaos swirl in confusion, one who dwells at this height remains serene and self-content, drifting in forgetfulness of all doing.

The next day, Liezi again brought Ji Xian to see Huzhi. Before he could settle into his stance, Ji Xian lost himself and fled. Huzhi said: “Pursue him!” Liezi chased after him but could not catch up. Returning, he reported to Huzhi: “He has vanished, he is gone—I could not reach him.”

Huzhi said: “Earlier, I showed him what has never yet departed from my source. I met him with emptiness and followed his winding path, so he knew not who or what I was. Taking me for drifting grass, taking me for flowing waves, he fled.”

Though transformations are ceaseless, they ever strike deep roots in the hidden utmost. Without intent, one follows the transformations of things, drifting unbound by any tether. As changes crumble and decay, as worldly affairs surge like waves, there is nowhere one does not accord.

The perfected person is one, yet responds to the world’s changes and moves with the times—thus the physiognomist found no foothold for his gaze and fled in self-loss. This clarifies that one who would respond as emperor or king has no fixed method.

Thereafter, Liezi returned home believing he had never yet begun to learn. For three years he did not venture out, cooked for his wife, and fed pigs as he would feed people, showing no partiality in his dealings. Carved and polished, he returned to simplicity, standing alone as a mere clod of earth. Though the world swirled in chaos, he remained thus to the end. He forgot distinctions of noble and base, attending only to what he encountered. He shed ornament for substance, casting off external embellishments. Though he moved, his true nature never scattered.

Let things each fulfill their own ends. Be not the host of fame, the storehouse of schemes, the bearer of tasks, or the master of knowledge. Embody the boundless and wander without trace. Exhaust what you receive from Heaven, yet perceive no gain—be empty, and nothing more!

The perfected person’s mind works like a mirror: it neither sends forth nor greets, responds yet does not store, and thus can master things without harm. Accord with things, and each will naturally fit its name. Let things each devise their own plans, entrust tasks to each as they are suited, and without intent, let each direct its own knowledge.

Follow the world’s spontaneous self-governing, and you may gallop through the ten thousand things without exhaustion. Entrust yourself to things, and you leave no trace. When you are content, you stop. To perceive gain is to know no stopping. Without emptiness, one cannot bear the weight of all realities.

Mirror things without emotion. When they come, respond; when they depart, cease. Things arrive, and one reflects them—reflecting not with the mind, thus though the world be vast, there is no burden of wearying the spirit.

The Lord of the Southern Sea was Shu, the Lord of the Northern Sea was Hu, and the Lord of the Central Realm was Hundun. Shu and Hu would often meet in Hundun’s land, and Hundun treated them exceedingly well. Shu and Hu plotted to repay Hundun’s virtue, saying: “All people have seven apertures for seeing, hearing, eating, and breathing—only he has none. Let us try to bore them for him.” Each day they bored one aperture, and on the seventh day, Hundun died. Those who act ruin things.
